HEADLINE: On My Mind;
How Dare We?

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL

 BODY:
How dare the United States do this thing?
Alexander M. Haig Jr. wants to know. How dare the U.S. "go around telling the rest of the world" that it must live up to American human rights standards? He has China in mind. 
 "I think the time has come to take a different tack here," he said in Beijing. "And Tiananmen is a long way behind us."
Mr. Haig has a distinguished record -- Secretary of State, NATO commander, White House chief of staff. He was in Beijing to introduce the new president of United Technologies Corporation to Chinese Communist leaders. United Technologies is in the defense business.
Mr. Haig is a take-charge kind of guy. Right after President Reagan was shot in 1981, Mr. Haig, then Secretary of State, told the country that he himself was "in control" at the White House; Vice President Bush was in a plane flying to Washington.
The Haig decision did not sit well, probably because neither Mr. Reagan, Mr. Bush nor Congress had also so decided.
Anyway, you can see Mr. Haig deserves an answer and Washington better give him one in a hurry. Until then, I will do my best.
Mr. Haig, it really is a matter of taste and, if you forgive the expression, the moral responsibility of American business.
The taste of Congress, the incumbent President and most Americans kind of runs against governments that use forced labor in and out of prison as an official part of the economy, and torture as an inducement to both labor and political obedience.
In China, the Government uses the economic advantages of that labor, and of trade with the capitalist nations, to build the world's largest army -- and to sell missiles, nuclear material and weapon technology, mostly to nations hostile to America.
Mr. Haig, those Americans not well disposed to gulag government do not want to bomb China or isolate it. All they say is that it is hypocrisy and destructive of liberty to say we disapprove of Communist Chinese techniques of government and yet refuse to do what we can to pressure the gulag wardens into at least lowering the torture quotient of political life, in China proper and imprisoned Tibet.
Congress's taste, and expressed will, is to put a deal to your Beijing hosts: Loosen the handcuffs or we will lift the trade privileges that have made possible the $20 billion trade advantage China enjoys over the U.S.
Business is no more holy than diplomacy or journalism. Either can help build freedom or help maintain despotism.
Certainly it is obscene that profits made through the capitalist free-enterprise system be used to strengthen a Communist society that refuses to give its citizens and Tibetans the freedoms of choice that should be the very essence of capitalism.
I know of two U.S. companies that have adopted a human rights policy that does honor to them, their country, free enterprise and their customers. They have withdrawn their plants in China or are preparing to do so rather than contribute to the daily violations of decency that exist all around those factories.
So I am writing this dressed in a long-wearing, stylish pair of Timberland boots and long-wearing, stylish Levi Strauss shirt and pants.
Beijing may not tremble before Levi Strauss and Timberland. Neither did Pretoria tremble when some American company was the first to say: "No longer."
Mr. Haig says we should help China to offset Japan. Then we can balance the two superpowers of Asia against each other, instead of having to deal alone with Japan. Neat? Presumably Japanese and Chinese cannot figure that out and one day get together to give us a two-superpower squeeze.
Patrick E. Tyler, the New York Times bureau chief in China, who interviewed Mr. Haig earlier this month, writes that he was seething at U.S. policy toward China.
Mr. Haig is an excellent seether. I always liked him for that. We two may have faults but concealment is not one of them.
A press colleague told me Mr. Haig had said he would dearly love to punch me in the mouth for asking him a political question he thought impertinent in substance and manner. It was. Mike Wallace egged me on.
But since Mr. Haig never did punch me in the mouth I took the threat as mere wishfulness. If not, I hope he gets tired of waiting in line.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Economy Won't Grow With Tax Subsidies

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Charls E. Walker, former Deputy Treasury Secretary, applauds Presidents who favored investment tax credits and liberalized depreciation rules, and criticizes those, like President Clinton, who do not advocate tax subsidies to encourage capital spending (letter, Oct. 18). 
 When there is a demand, and the ability to pay, for particular goods and services, providers will invest in the capital equipment to meet the demand with or without a tax subsidy. If there is insufficient demand or purchasing power, they are unlikely to buy unneeded equipment, even with a tax credit or larger deduction.
The best way to stimulate economic growth is to reduce the income taxes of those with lower incomes who have the greatest propensity to spend their extra dollars. Reducing taxes on income from savings and investment (such as interest, dividends and capital gains) is less effective in stimulating economic growth because those who receive unearned income are more likely to save or reinvest than spend tax savings.
Moreover, savers and investors do not need preferential tax rates to invest. What they need is a free market that encourages better goods and services, and consumers with the purchasing power to buy them. Our tax policy must fuel the growth cycle by priming the demand side, and the supply of investment capital for the new equipment and additional jobs will follow.
 
STEPHEN A. HOCHMAN
 White Plains, Oct. 20, 1993

HEADLINE: Newark's $150 Million Answer to Lincoln Center Is Under Way

BYLINE: By EVELYN NIEVES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEWARK, Oct. 28

 BODY:
In a big tent pitched yards from a park where homeless men spend their days, the air was heady with hope and promise and symbolism.
As the New Jersey Symphony Orchestra played Aaron Copland's "Fanfare for the Common Man," arts mavens, corporate heads and politicians were joined by 21 schoolchildren from each of New Jersey's 21 counties to break ground today for what will clearly be more than just a new building in downtown Newark. 
 To some, it is New Jersey's Lincoln Center, a world-class venue second to none. To others, it is an economic boon creating hundreds of new jobs. And to all involved, the New Jersey Performing Arts Center, which will stand literally a stone's throw from where Newark's founders landed in 1666, is a giant stake in the future of the state's largest city.
The center is riding on the hope that a dazzling showplace for acclaimed acts will transcend Newark's image as a dangerous, crime-ridden place.
 
From Ashes of Hotel
 Much as Lincoln Center transformed a forlorn section of Manhattan when it was built in the 1960's, the $150 million New Jersey center is expected to breathe new life into this struggling city of 275,000 residents. It will rise from the ashes of the old Military Park hotel, a building that stood vacant and crumbling for years before it was imploded in May. The center will include a 2,700-seat theater and a 500-seat playhouse fronted by a public plaza, Theater Square, approximately the size of Rockefeller Center Plaza.
Planners say the center, which is scheduled to open in 1996, is expected to draw an annual audience of 650,000 to downtown Newark. It would increase business by $25 million a year in Newark alone, with an additional benefit of $35 million a year to the state.
It is expected to create at least 625 new construction jobs. Economic impact studies also count 470 permanent jobs, with most of those in Newark. And the ripple effect of the center, according to studies, would help support 6,700 jobs in the city.
Lawrence P. Goldman, who directed the development of Lincoln Center and presides over the New Jersey Performing Arts Center, said that the New Jersey center would be both an international arts center and a home for state arts groups like the New Jersey Symphony Orchestra.
 
'The Greatest Performers'
 "It will be multifaceted," Mr. Goldman said. "We're going to have the greatest performers in the world and we're going to do experimental things. Our real challenge is to be meaningful both to those people who patronize the arts and those who wouldn't ordinarily attend, either because they can not afford to or haven't been exposed."
The center has several potential audiences, including the 55,000 people who travel to jobs each day in Newark's downtown business enclaves. The hope is that it will also attract visitors from New York and the suburbs who will eat at Newark restaurants, take in the world-renowned Newark Museum, even stay at the city's hotels.
The center, designed by Barton Myers of Los Angeles, is also expected to attract new businesses. A large empty building across from where the center will be has already been bought for $2.5 million by private investors banking on the center's success. The investors plan to spend $25 million to renovate the building, the former Hahne's department store, which was famous in Newark's heyday, for retail shops and offices.
Newark's historic Military Park, which stands across from where arts center will be built, will get a much-needed sprucing-up. The Military Park Garage, underneath the park, will be renovated and modernized.
 
Not on Projects Alone
 In a note of caution, James W. Hughes, chairman of the Department of Urban Policy and Planning at Rutgers University, has said that while the center would be important to serve the city's residents and recapture business that drifted to the suburbs, it was important not to stake the city's entire revitalization on projects alone.
But for the planners today, there were no qualms about the project's value. "At a time when society is too divided, at a time when conflict in New Jersey and elsewhere is too heavy, the arts center has united all different kinds of people," Mr. Goldman said.
The center was sponsored by both public and private dollars. Several organizations donated more than $1 million, including the S. I. Newhouse Foundation, the Merck Company Foundation and the Prudential Foundation.
The soprano Kathleen Battle and the Newark Boys Chorus performed for hundreds at the ceremonial groundbreaking.
But not everyone was there to celebrate. A coalition of Hispanic and black contractors protesting their exclusion from the center's construction, protested across from the groundbreaking tent, chanting as Mayor James made his remarks.

CORRECTION-DATE: October 30, 1993, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about the groundbreaking for the New Jersey Performing Arts Center misstated the background of Lawrence P. Goldman, president of the arts center. He oversaw the renovation of Carnegie Hall, not Lincoln Center. Because of an editing error, the article also included an incomplete reference to Newark's Mayor. He is Sharpe James.

HEADLINE: U.S. SAYS ECONOMY GREW AS INFLATION SLOWED IN SUMMER

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
The economy perked up during the summer, expanding at a 2.8 percent annual rate despite floods and drought that cut farm output, the Commerce Department reported today.
At the same time, inflation subsided to a 1.8 percent annual rate, the weakest rise for a quarter since oil prices collapsed in 1986. 
 This combination of stepped-up growth and slackened inflation was hailed by the Clinton Administration as early dividends of its economic policy, particularly the legislation enacted in mid-summer aimed at cutting the budget deficit.
"Lower deficits, lower interest rates have sparked the beginning of a significant economic recovery," President Clinton said at a news conference.
 
Proof of Acceleration
 Today's figures supported the growing belief among many economists that there has been a clear acceleration of the economy, beginning in late summer. The report, which covers July through September, not only showed improvement from earlier in the year but also confirmed more recent signs of strength in car and home sales and business at department stores. Earlier this week, the Government announced a hefty jump in orders for long-lasting factory goods.
"The problems that depressed the economy in the first half -- weak consumption and the inventory cycle -- have been put behind us," said Jerry J. Jasinowski, president of the National Association of Manufacturers. "The growth rate in the third quarter is consistent with a modest and steady economic expansion."
 
 
Substantial Improvement
 The 2.8 percent annual growth rate was the briskest since last year's final-quarter surge, when the economy expanded at a 5.7 percent rate, and considerably better than the weak advances of eight-tenths of a percent in the first quarter and 1.9 percent in the second. The quarterly rise was the 10th consecutive gain since the end of the latest recession, officially designated as March 1991.
The economic revival, though widespread, is far from uniform and has eluded some areas, especially California, which has been hit heavily by military cutbacks.
That state's unemployment rate was 9.4 percent in September, more than twice the 4.2 percent rate in North Carolina, which enjoyed the lowest rate among the big industrial states. The national average is 6.7 percent.
A Labor Department tally released today showed that first-time claims for unemployment insurance nationwide fell 4,000 last week, to 347,000, a change considered inconsequential. Job worries remain a leading factor in depressed consumer confidence.
Over all, the Government's report on gross domestic product -- the total value of goods and services produced within the nation's borders -- was about as expected. But many analysts found it somewhat stronger in light of Commerce Department calculations that Midwest floods and Southeast drought had lopped six-tenths of a point off G.D.P. in crop losses alone.
Further impact of the floods could not be quantified, but there should be added growth in coming quarters as companies and households continue to replace flood losses.
Another retarding factor was weak automotive production, with assemblies dropping at a 31 percent annual pace in the July-September quarter. This cost G.D.P. a full percentage point, according to Lewis Alexander, the Commerce Department's chief economist. In recent weeks, however, sales of cars and trucks have surged and the third-quarter loss of output will be regained if producers meet current production schedules.
W. Van Bussmann, associate economist at the Chrysler Corporation, said he was encouraged by the report, which showed consumer buying to be among the strongest factors.
 
 
Money After Mortgage Payments
 "The ability of consumers to make purchases, both large and small, is good," Mr. Bussmann said. He and other economists said that while confidence remained low, at least as measured by formal surveys, this summer's budget deal seems to have dispelled some uncertainty.
And low interest rates have inspired mortgage refinancing, which has provided extra money for consumer spending.
Spending on personal consumption climbed at a 4.2 percent pace during the third quarter, up from a gain of 3.4 percent in the spring, today's report showed. Still, modestly rising household incomes, sizable increases in installment credit and the benefits of refinancing mortgages were not enough to pay for the spending, and the personal savings rate for the quarter slumped to 3.7 percent from 4.4 percent.
Investment in new equipment also helped power third-quarter growth as companies sought to minimize unit labor costs by substituting machinery for workers and their increasingly expensive health benefits. The 9 percent rate of increase, however, was only half as large as the increase in the first half of the year.
Exports fell somewhat, however, because of continuing weakness in the economies of many of America's trading partners. But the decline was less than in the April-June period. The Commerce Department assumed a small widening of the trade deficit in September, for which results are not published until mid-November.
Military purchases resumed their decline after a record first-quarter cutback and negligible second-quarter uptick.
 
'Decent Acceleration'
 Despite the drags, the Clinton Administration and most private economists expect G.D.P. in the current quarter to exceed the 2.8 percent third-quarter pace reported today.
"I'm talking 4 percent," said Elliott Platt of the investment firm of Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, pointing to what he called a "decent acceleration" in the economy.
The lower inflation in today's report meant that most of the advance in G.D.P. was actual growth, rather than adjustments for inflation.
The fixed-weighted price index for gross domestic purchases, which measures prices paid by United States residents, climbed at a 1.8 percent pace in the summer, well below the 2.9 percent spring pace.
G.D.P. expressed in current dollars, rather than dollars adjusted for inflation, rose at a 4.4 percent rate in the quarter just ended, barely ahead of the second quarter's 4.3 percent pace.
Receding inflation means more of each added dollar of G.D.P. reflects added production, rather than simple price increases.
 
Details on 3d-Quarter G.D.P.
 Data included in the gross domestic product for the third quarter follow, with all dollar amounts in billions at seasonally adjusted annual rates. Percentage changes are from the previous quarter.

LENGTH: 1631 words

HEADLINE: MODERATE GROWTH IN SEESAW PATTERN SEEN FOR ECONOMY

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
The economy is showing more signs of life, but even third-quarter growth of 2.8 percent, announced on Thursday, is not likely to be the start of a prolonged period of smooth, strong expansion.
Many economists predict that the economy will grow moderately in coming months. But the process is likely to continue to be choppy, they caution, with periods of solid expansion alternating with anemic growth like that in the first half of this year. 
 While this kind of up-and-down existence may seem unusual -- and discomforting -- such a ride is not uncommon. Economic dynamics are such that spending and production rarely happen smoothly. The reason the uneveness stands out now and feels so painful, economists say, is that the underlying pace of growth is so modest.
 
Sensation of Growth
 In a more robust recovery -- like most of those in recent decades -- one quarter might grow at an annual rate of 2 or 3 percent and the next quarter at 8 or 9 percent, but the overall sensation would still be of an economy moving ahead strongly, creating new jobs at a hefty clip. Although there is often a good deal of volatility, growth in the years after a recession typically averages about 5 percent.
With an economy like the current one, with growth swinging between half a percentage point and 5 percent or 6 percent, the feeling is one of teetering on the edge of no growth at all, and average growth is no more than about 3 percent, considerably lower than usual.
"You've got some big quarters and some little quarters, but by and large 2.5 percent to 3 percent is what you see and what you'll get," said David Wyss, a forecaster at Data Resources Inc.
That is not to say that there is no difference between the 1 percent growth rate of the first half of the year and the roughly 3 percent of the summer. If the economy persistently grew at 1 percent, unemployment would climb and aggregate wages would almost certainly fall.
 
Good for Long Haul
 At 3 percent, employment grows and so do wages, although progress on both fronts may be so slow as to seem nearly imperceptible at times, and is not likely to make most Americans a lot happier any time soon.
"Over the short haul, 3 percent doesn't do a lot," said William Dickens, a labor economist at the President's Council of Economic Advisers. "But over a few years, it will push the unemployment rate below 6 percent and create some real wage growth."
Slower overall growth also increases the likelihood that certain regions and certain workers will be left behind.
"The economy recovering doesn't mean a recovery for all workers," said Kevin Murphy, an economist at the University of Chicago. "That's been one of the major lessons of the last decade, and I expect it to continue.
"We economists tend to focus on the national numbers, but when it gets down to individuals, the national numbers are only a small part of the picture. What's going on in an industry, a region or your educational group is what matters for the individual."
Strikingly, executives and economists say growth in the range of 2.5 percent to 3 percent will not make Americans feel much better very fast, any more than cruising on a highway in second gear makes it fun to drive a BMW.
"Prospects are getting better, but exceptionally slowly," said Harvey Rosenblum, research director at the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas. "Most Americans feel that 2 percent to 3 percent growth is not enough."
For one thing, at that rate it would take another couple of years for the unemployment rate to get down to 6 percent, the rate many economists said they thought was the long-run full-employment rate.
"What makes people feel better is for the unemployment rate to go down and wages to go up," Mr. Dickens said. "Unemployment won't come down soon and wages won't be going up a lot."
Unemployment, which fell an entire point in the last year, could easily stay in the 6.5 percent range in the next year if labor-force growth picks up a bit, as it almost always does as job opportunities grow, and if productivity gains keep coming, as many business executives said they believed they would. As for wages, palpable gains beyond inflation are not likely until the slack disappears in the labor market.
 
 
Winners and Losers
 Growth in the 3 percent range also means that lots of Americans -- whether military-industry engineers or Californians or young workers with high school degrees -- will continue to feel as if the recession lingers for them, even as software engineers or Coloradans or biotechnology doctorates do well.
Despite the cautions, the recent strength could hold the seeds of further growth. In contrast to just a few months ago, many business executives and consumers are acting -- and sounding -- as if they expect the moderate growth and gradual improvement in the job market to continue.
Business is certainly behaving as if it expects the economy to keep expanding. Business help-wanted advertising is about 20 percent higher in most regions than it was a year ago. And, outside of manufacturing, hirings have outpaced layoffs by 150,000 jobs a month since last fall. Orders for business equipment are 14 percent higher than a year ago and spending on new equipment, especially high-technology hardware, is up by double digits.
Consumers are also sounding more hopeful. The University of Michigan reported yesterday that consumer expectations, one ingredient in the Government's forecasting gauge, jumped in October, though expectations remain relatively low.
While expectations do not always translate into more spending, these figures follow months of solid spending. Consumption rose at a strong 4.2 percent annual rate this summer, after increasing at a rate of 3.5 percent in the spring.
 
Mortgage Refinancing Helps
 The spending has been financed partly by gains in wage and salary income, which have been modest, and by mortgage refinancings (which have freed up roughly $100 billion in household cash flow in the last three years, said Lyle E. Gramley at the Mortgage Bankers Association) as well as by gains in the stock and bond markets.
Besides, some threats to growth have receded. Business stockpiles are extremely lean, so extra sales should translate into production over the next several months. There are few signs of capacity shortages or inflationary pressures to cut off growth. And, with banks advertising heavily to draw new business customers, the credit crunch is less of a constraint on most small- and medium-sized businesses.
Like the 1980's expansion, the 1990's expansion, which is taking place against a backdrop of huge shifts in Government policy and in the marketplace, is apt to benefit different industries, regions, and, most of all, individuals.
Take wages. The fact that they have stopped falling in the aggregate masks large increases for some and big declines for others.
"If you're in a period where inequality is growing, peoples' fortunes don't turn so much on what happens to the economy as a whole," said Professor Murphy, who contends that the forces that were creating million-dollar ballplayers and driving down the pay of workers at the bottom in the 1980's still seem to be at work in the 1990's.
New York is a case in point. Personal income in the region surged in the last year at the same time that the region's labor force was shrinking as workers left the area or became too discouraged to keep looking for work. What is happening, regional economists say, is that very high-skilled workers -- bond traders, for example -- are still in great demand and are being rewarded accordingly.
 
Manufacturing Loss Stunning
 The same disparities are evident when it comes to job creation. Manufacturing has lost a stunning 1.5 million jobs since the recovery began -- a surprise even to most economists who said they thought manufacturing's share of employment would decline in the 1990's. On the other hand, nursing homes, hospitals and school districts have added about that many new jobs to payrolls.
Thus, a teacher who lives in Tarrytown, N.Y., faces radically different prospects from those confronting a worker at General Motors' Tarrytown plant, which is scheduled to close in a few years, or a 50-year-old I.B.M. manager, threatened by cutbacks.
Furthermore, there are plenty of risks lurking in this uneven situation. The slump that stretches from Toronto to Tokyo could get worse. Higher taxes could dent spending. The Clinton health care program could keep small- and medium-sized businesses from hiring as they sort out how hard they will be hit. Or consumers could get giddy again this Christmas, the way they did last year, spend beyond their means and set the economy up for a fall.
Most economists said they thought the risks were likely to be averted. Despite the recent improvement in mood, they say, consumers are not likely to turn as euphoric as they did last year, after the Presidential election. At the same time, housing and autos are clearly picking up even as other economic sectors, namely exports and military spending, weaken. Builders dug enough foundations this summer to assure that construction spending will keep rising for several more months and the perception that interest rates are about as low as they are likely to go is apt to stimulate spending on big-ticket items.
"The economy in the second half looks good," said Bruce Steinberg of Merrill Lynch, "and it's probably not going to collapse again in January."
George N. Hatsopoulos, the president and chief executive of the Thermo Electron Corporation, which has been benefiting from strong business investment spending, said: "I think it's likely we'll see a little higher growth than in the spring. It won't be a booming scenario though."

HEADLINE: Bonds Fall on New Economic Data

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Treasury prices retreated yesterday as reports of economic strength made investors nervous as they looked ahead to the October employment figures due at the end of next week.
The price of the Treasury's main 30-year bond fell 8/32 of a point, while its yield, which moves in the opposite direction, rose to 5.96 percent from 5.95 percent on Thursday. 
 Of particular interest to fixed-income investors was a survey showing a sharper-than-expected improvement in Midwest manufacturing conditions this month. While the survey of Chicago-area purchasing managers is viewed in the credit markets as a minor report, it attracted interest because it is considered an indicator of a national purchasing managers' report due on Monday.
Another survey, by the University of Michigan, pointed to improving consumer confidence in October, traders said. The survey is not released publicly, but there were reports that the index of consumer sentiment rose to 82.7 for October, from 77.9 for September.
Those results seem to defy a report released Monday showing that consumer confidence plunged in October. The Michigan report and the Chicago purchasing managers' survey increased concerns that the economy would show sharply higher growth in the fourth quarter.
"It confirmed other news that manufacturing is doing better," said William Dudley, senior economist at Goldman, Sachs & Company.
The bond market tends to react negatively to news of economic growth because it raises the likelihood of higher inflation, and inflation can reduce the value of bonds and other interest-bearing securities.
In addition, bond market investors fear that the Federal Reserve will react to threats of higher inflation by pushing up short-term interest rates on newly sold securities. Higher rates tend to curb inflation pressures, but they can also reduce the value of existing bonds.
Alan Levenson, the money-market economist at UBS Securities, said some economists were revising their forecasts higher ahead of the October employment report. Projections had previously called for an increase of 150,000 nonfarm payroll jobs.
In trading yesterday, short-term Treasury notes fell 3/32 to 5/32 of a point, while intermediate-term notes fell 3/16 to 13/32 of a point.
The Lehman Brothers Daily Treasury Bond Index, reflecting price movements on bonds with maturities of a year or longer, fell 2.52, to 1,324.59.
The discount rate on three-month Treasury bills was unchanged at 3.02 percent. The discount rate on both six-month and one-year bills rose a basis point, or a hundredth of a percentage point, with the six-month rate at 3.17 percent and the one-year rate at 3.32 percent.
In the tax-exempt market, the Bond Buyer index of 40 actively traded municipal bonds closed at 10413/32 , up 2/32 from late Thursday. The average yield to maturity was unchanged at 5.48 percent.

HEADLINE: Consumer Spending Up for a Sixth Month

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Consumers spent more in September, racking up a sixth consecutive monthly rise, the Commerce Department reported today.
Consumer spending, which powers two-thirds of national growth, rose three-tenths of 1 percent last month, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $4.43 trillion, after a revised identical gain in August. 
 The higher spending was evident in Thursday's third-quarter gross domestic product report, which showed that national output rose at a 2.8 percent annual rate in the quarter after rising at a 1.3 percent pace in the first six months. The stronger growth rate -- the best quarterly performance this year -- was attributed primarily to rising consumer spending.
The Commerce Department also said total incomes grew by two-tenths of 1 percent in September, to a rate of $5.44 trillion, following a 1.3 percent increase in August. It was the second consecutive month for income gains after a decline of three-tenths of 1 percent in July.
Another survey suggested that consumers were confident about their economic future. That survey, a report by the University of Michigan on consumer confidence -- a measure of optimism -- showed a surge to 82.7 in October from 77.9 in September.
And the Purchasing Management Association of Chicago said its index of corporate buying plans ticked upward to 57 percent in October, from 54.5 percent in September.
"I think we have kicked onto a slightly higher growth track for the economy so that our trend rate for the next year or so will be around 3 percent," said Raymond Worseck, an economist at A. G. Edwards & Sons Inc. in St. Louis.
In another report today, the Agriculture Department said prices received by American farmers declined seven-tenths of 1 percent in October after surging in September. Compared with a year earlier, farm prices were 4.3 percent higher in October.
Personal savings fell in September, to an annual rate of $190.2 billion, from $195.8 billion. The rate shows savings as a percentage of disposable income. It fell to 4 percent in September, from 4.1 percent in August. That meant Americans were saving just 4 cents out of each dollar they earned after taxes last month.
Spending on expensive, long-lasting durable goods was virtually unchanged in September from August and most of the overall increase was for more purchases of nondurables like food and paper products.
Spending on services, like accounting and medical care, gained slightly in September.

GRAPHIC: Graph: "Personal Income" shows total personal income from April 1992 to Sept. 1993. (Source: Commerce Department)

HEADLINE: Clinton Pushes Trade as New Frontier

BYLINE: By GWEN IFILL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BOSTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Evoking the memories and language of John F. Kennedy to make a case for continued American involvement abroad, President Clinton said today that the United States cannot turn away from global economic commitments that he said constitute "our new frontier."
Mr. Clinton, who joined members of the Kennedy clan at a ceremony marking the opening of a new museum at the John F. Kennedy Library here, said that his domestic and foreign policy objectives are what President Kennedy would have worked for. 
 Surrounded by hundreds of former Kennedy staff members, replicas of rooms in the Kennedy White House and photographs of the Kennedy Presidency, Mr. Clinton did not hesitate to proclaim himself heir to the Kennedy mantle.
But when he spoke of foreign policy he talked about trade, not American military involvement, insisting that the late President would have supported the Clinton Administration's efforts to pass the North American Free Trade Agreement. As Congress approaches a vote on the agreement with Mexico and Canada in two weeks, he said, lawmakers in Washington are at a moment of "high decision."
 
Photo of a Handshake
 Mr. Clinton has often credited President Kennedy with planting the seeds of his interest in politics after meeting the President at a Rose Garden ceremony honoring participants in Boys' Nation in the summer of 1963.
A grainy bit of film capturing that handshake became a stock piece of Clinton campaign footage last year and is now on view at the museum here. Another film, chronicling the 1962 Cuban missile crisis that was one of the high points of the Kennedy Presidency, is also on view at the library and Mr. Clinton watched it today with rapt attention.
Mr. Clinton found a receptive audience among the more than 400 Kennedy friends and admirers who gathered at the museum and stood in line -- some of them for more than an hour -- to shake Mr. Clinton's hand afterward.
Each of the Kennedys who spoke at the ceremony today praised President Clinton as a worthy successor to President Kennedy. Representative Joseph Kennedy, whose father, Robert, was assassinated in 1968, said that both his father and his uncle would have approved of Mr. Clinton.
 
'My Father Is Smiling'
 "Somewhere in this universe, my father is smiling on today's events," Mr. Kennedy said. "I believe he would smile upon your Presidency."
Caroline Kennedy Schlossberg, who with her husband, Edwin, has spearheaded the effort to renovate the library, added, "President Kennedy lives on in those he inspired to enter public life."
Mr. Clinton has never discouraged comparisons of himself to President Kennedy, and today he appeared to bask in the regard of Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis, Ethel Kennedy and others. Mrs. Onassis leaned over to grip his hand after he spoke today and said, "That was wonderful."
In describing the accomplishments of his 10-month-old Administration, Mr. Clinton drew comparisons between the Peace Corps and the National Service Corps, between the launching of the space program and the continuation of funding for the space station.
President Kennedy, he said, renounced isolationism in favor of an expansionist view of the world.
 
Subtext: The Trade Agreement
 "He believed that billions of lives depended upon our leadership and our ideals, and in turn, that our own security and prosperity are tied to reaching out to the rest of the world," Mr. Clinton said.
In effusively praising the late President, Mr. Clinton never strayed far from his subtext, the trade agreement. He even jibed Senator Edward M. Kennedy for not taking a stand in favor of the trade agreement, pointing out that Thomas P. O'Neill Jr., the former House speaker, and Representative Kennedy have both endorsed it.
"And I believe if President Kennedy were still representing that seat in Congress, he would endorse it as well," Mr. Clinton said, prompting Representative Kennedy, who was seated on stage nearby, to clap his hand on his uncle's shoulder. Senator Kennedy responded by grabbing his necktie and pretending to choke himself with it.
Senator Kennedy said later that the pitch from the President and his nephew was "very persuasive," but that he remained uncommitted on the trade agreement.
As Mr. Clinton has traveled this week to New York and Boston, he has hammered hard on the need for passage of the trade agreement. He visited the South Boston headquarters of the Gillette Company today to continue that plea, standing beside Representative John Joseph Moakley, who opposes the trade agreement, as he asked Gillette employees to ask their Congressional representatives to support the pact.

GRAPHIC: Photo: President Clinton talking with Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis and John F. Kennedy Jr. yesterday as they attended the opening ceremonies of the newly redesigned John F. Kennedy Library and museum in Boston. (Agence France-Presse)

HEADLINE: Labor Official Retreats on Higher Minimum Wage

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Under pressure from the White House, Labor Secretary Robert B. Reich said today that he would delay until next year his plans to recommend a higher minimum wage.
Two weeks ago Mr. Reich said that by the end of October he would recommend that the minimum wage, now $4.25 an hour, be increased by about 25 cents. But White House officials warned him immediately afterward that such a move would be unwise because it could antagonize business leaders at a time when President Clinton needs them for his health care and trade proposals. 
 Mr. Reich said in a statement today that "in light of the historic deliberations over health care," he had advised Mr. Clinton "to defer any decision on a minimum-wage increase until Congress has had further opportunity to consider the President's health care plan." He said he had advised the President to "revisit the issue next year."
Several Administration officials said that calling for a minimum-wage increase now would only complicate efforts to win Congressional approval of the President's health care plan because business owners are already worried that the health plan would raise their costs.
In an appearance at the National Press Club on Oct. 12, Mr. Reich said a 25-cent increase in the minimum wage, when coupled with an increase in benefits like insurance, which is called for in the President's health plan, could actually bring the value of the overall increase to 50 cents an hour.
Mr. Reich said today that after reviewing economic studies on the effects of minimum-wage increases, he had concluded that "the minimum wage could be increased moderately with no loss of employment." Business groups dispute this, however, asserting that wage increases quickly translate into fewer jobs.
When he testified about Mr. Clinton's health plan before a subcommittee of the the House Energy and Commerce Committee on Thursday, Mr. Reich told the panel that some studies showed that increases in the minimum wage "induced more employment because people came into the market who would not otherwise want to come into the labor market."
In last year's Presidential campaign, Mr. Clinton promised to raise the minimum wage, and since that time Mr. Reich has become the Administration's champion of such an effort. He often points out that the current wage, after taking inflation into account, is 30 percent below its 1968 level.
Last January, some Administration officials said they would push for a higher minimum wage in the summer and were considering an increase of $1 an hour, to $5.25. But they delayed making a recommendation, partly because the business community was so incensed with the Administration over tax increases in the President's deficit-reduction plan.
 
Complaints From Business
 Lobbyists for small-business groups are already angry with Mr. Clinton for proposing that all businesses be required to buy health insurance for their employees. They say this mandate will greatly increase small businesses' wage costs, although Mr. Reich asserts that it will increase wage costs for minimum-wage workers by 15 to 35 cents an hour.
Administration officials said that proposing a higher minimum wage not only would further alienate small businesses but could discourage them from hiring more workers just as the economy is gathering steam.
Mr. Reich has often contended that raising the minimum wage is an important step toward lifting all working families above the poverty line.

HEADLINE: Westchester Sees Replay Of Challenge By Legislator

BYLINE: By JAMES FERON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WHITE PLAINS, Oct. 29

 BODY:
During six terms as an Assemblyman, Richard L. Brodsky developed a reputation as a zealous and often abrasive legislator.
A New York Post columnist last year named him "most obnoxious lawmaker of the year" because he was so "arrogant and nasty." 
 Now, as Mr. Brodsky, a 47-year-old Democrat, makes his second bid to unseat Westchester's longtime County Executive, Andrew P. O'Rourke Jr., supporters say he has matured and learned the art of compromise. But they offer no apology for Mr. Brodsky's aggressive approach.
"Richard may be abrasive, but you need to be to get things done," said Assemblyman Paul A. Tokasz, a Buffalo Democrat and a member of the Assembly Committee on Environmental Conservation, which Mr. Brodsky heads.
 
The Breaking of Furniture
 "He made some serious staff changes and told the senior staff members that 'we're going to be aggressive,' especially about some proposed legislation that had been 'hanging around for 10 years.' "
The committee passed the Clean Air Act under Mr. Brodsky's prodding, and not without difficulty, Mr. Tokasz said. "He had to deal with Assemblymen representing upstate communities, where we rely more on cars than they do in the New York metropolitan area, but Richard was able to craft legislation that passed."
Lee Wasserman, who heads the environmental planning lobby in Albany, said, "Richard always has a point of view on strategy, who needs pushing and who needs cajoling." He threatens, flatters and twists arms, and, Mr. Wasserman said, he "often treats people he's working with the same as he treats his staff," which is not always kindly.
But Audrey G. Hochberg, an Assembly Democrat who served for several years on the Westchester Board of Legislators with Mr. Brodsky, said his ability to work with others improved "when he took on a leadership position in Albany."
 
An Attack on Ethics
 "When he wants to make something happen he is willing to compromise, accommodating the needs of others, to make it work," she said. Still, there is the "breaking-furniture" syndrome, as Assemblyman Tokasz put it, when Mr. Brodsky is pursuing something he considers vital.
These days, he is on such a quest, arguing that Mr. O'Rourke, a Republican, lost interest in his job while awaiting a judicial appointment that evaporated with the election of President Clinton. He also charges that Mr. O'Rourke has transformed the County Executive's office into a family employment office, seeking jobs for friends and relatives and directing county contracts to companies employing his children or friends.
Mr. O'Rourke has denied wrongdoing and pointed out that a grand jury found he had broken no law. To that, Mr. Brodsky says the post of county executive deserves a higher standard than not being indicted.
"Andy O'Rourke," he said, "is a worn-out, self-contented politician who has become mired in scandal and who doesn't have the energy or the programs to keep Westchester moving, to keep it a good place to live."
Mr. Brodsky said his rival's approach is "similar to that of I.B.M. five years ago to look backward and hope things stay good."
 
Behind in Poll Calculations
 "Westchester needs a chief executive, not an administrator," he says. "We need somebody who understands what the loss of 40,000 jobs means, to understand that the unemployed are not merely the disadvantaged, to know what it's like to live and not work in Westchester."
As county executive, Mr. Brodsky said, he would tighten ethics laws, establish job-creation procedures, help establish small businesses and seek to revitalize downtowns.
He also has pledged to end what he calls the gridlock of the county legislature in its dealings with the County Executive's office and would focus on reaching an agreement with New York City on water quality of the reservoirs in Westchester County.
Although he has been endorsed in the editorial pages of The New York Times and the Gannett Suburban Newspapers, both of which supported Mr. O'Rourke last time, Mr. Brodsky admits he faces an uphill fight.
A poll released today by the Gannett papers show Mr. O'Rourke leading Mr. Brodsky by 48.5 percent to 37.8 percent.
 
A History of Confrontation
 The poll, which has a margin of error of plus or minus 4 percentage points, indicated that Mr. Brodsky remained unknown to many potential voters. When those polled were asked their general opinion of the candidates, Mr. O'Rourke was viewed favorably by 53.3 percent and Mr. Brodsky by 35.3 percent.
Mr. Brodsky said the poll was flawed because it selected respondents randomly, rather than selecting them from election registration records. He said his own polled showed him to be much closer, although still behind.
Mr. O'Rourke and Mr. Brodsky have been confronting each other for years, at least since they were members of the County Legislature, Mr. O'Rourke as chairman of the Republican-controlled body and Mr. Brodsky as his chief tormentor.
Both have finely honed senses of humor, and their barbed exchanges often punctuated the board's meetings. They have continued the practice since Mr. Brodsky began his siege of Mr. O'Rourke's office four years ago. He lost narrowly in that election.
The two have differed on the economy's impact on Westchester. Mr. O'Rourke says unemployment in the county is a relatively low 5.7 percent; Mr. Brodsky says Westchester has lost jobs, two-thirds of them white-collar, in 47 of the last 50 months.
Another major issue is taxes, with Mr. O'Rourke saying that he has held the line on county taxes. Mr. Brodsky encourages audiences to "go home and look at your 1993 tax bill to see for yourself if taxes did not go up."
Mr. Brodsky said he would cut the county executive's salary of $119,000 by 10 percent and reduce office staff by the same fraction. Mr. O'Rourke would like to add several assistant deputy county executives to oversee certain county departments. Department heads currently report to Mr. O'Rourke's deputy, Neil DeLuca.
 
Politics Outside of Washington
 Mr. Brodsky says his interest in politics "came out of my family's traditional sense of concern about community, society and justice." He adds: "It also came out of law school. The question I faced after leaving Harvard was, do I go into the private sector?"
He decided to work for the Robert Kennedy Memorial Foundation as chief of staff for a study of high school journalism. "We concluded that journalism in schools was a wondrous place to teach the rights of expression, and that high school newspapers should see their role as something more than covering sports and social activities," he said.
It was then that he decided to get into politics but out of Washington. He returned to Westchester, serving as legal counsel for County Executive Alfred B. DelBello, the first Democrat ever to hold that post in Westchester. Mr. Brodsky would be the second.
He was born in Brooklyn. The family moved to Westchester when Richard was 10. He graduated from Ardsley High School, Brandeis University and Harvard University Law School, beginning the practice of law upon graduation in 1971.
His specialty was entertainment and labor law, which led to his meeting Paige Massman. They were married in 1983 and have two daughters, Emily Lynn, 8, and Julianne Willie, 2. Mr. Brodsky spends his vacations in Montana, his wife's family's home.
The candidate is an accomplished piano player who specializes in blues and plays occasionally with a band called the Rugged Individualists. The Brodskys live in a historic house in the town of Greenburgh, just outside the village of Elmsford.
"It was the setting for James Fenimore Cooper's 'The Spy' and was restored after the British burned it down in the middle 1600's'," Mr. Brodsky said, adding that the house was "where the patriots met to defeat the Tories, and still is."
 
PROFILE: RICHARD L. BRODSKY
 
Born: May 7, 1946; Brooklyn
 
Hometown: Greenburgh, N.Y.
 
Education: Ardsley High School, 1964; Brandeis University, B.A. 1968; Harvard University Law School, J.D. 1971.
 
Career: Practiced law 1971-1982; Westchester County Legislator, 1974-1982; New Yor Assemblyman, 1983-present.
 
Family: Married Paige Massman, actress and teacher in 1983. Two daughters, Emily Lynn, 8, and Julianne Willie, 2.
 
Interests: Trout fishing, gardening, reading books on history and public policy, playing the piano and wood carving.

HEADLINE: U.N. Envoy Proposes Talks To End the Impasse in Haiti

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Oct. 29

 BODY:
The United Nations' envoy to Haiti called today for new talks with the nation's military leaders and renewed demands that they take steps to comply with a negotiated agreement restoring democracy in the next two days or face a rapid cutoff of trade.
Speaking at a news conference on the eve of the original date set for the return of the nation's exiled President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, the special United Nations representative here, Dante Caputo, said his organization would have "no alternative" but to extend sanctions if Haiti's Army leaders did not respond favorably to the call for new talks. 
 Mr. Caputo said that, at the least, Haiti could avoid tighter sanctions if its military leaders presented plans for compliance with the accord to bring democracy to Haiti, even if the compliance did not occur by the original deadline of Oct. 30.
There was no immediate response from Haiti's military leaders.
 
A Fixed Agenda
 Mr. Caputo said that the fixed agenda for the talks would include the return of a United Nations military training mission that was prevented from landing here earlier this month by a dockside protest by armed civilians who are believed to be closely allied with the military.
Asked what he would do if the military defied this latest initiative, Mr. Caputo said, "We are going to stick it out."
Mr. Caputo said the other points in the proposed discussion, which would be held here early next week, would include the need for Haiti's Parliament to pass laws separating the Haitian police from the military and a possible amnesty for the leaders of the military coup that ousted Father Aristide two years ago. Beyond these matters, the United Nations official, who has led diplomacy here for nine months, said that he would seek from the military commander, Lieut. Gen. Raoul Cedras, an early date for his retirement and for the quick return of Father Aristide.
In an interview on a local radio station Thursday night, General Cedras said that if the two parties to the agreement had failed to adhere to its provisions, the international community should have no other choice but to extract itself from the mediation.
General Cedras, who in the settlement had agreed to retire on Oct. 15, maintained throughout the interview that the Haitian Army has been joined in violating the country's settlement by both Father Aristide, who also signed it, and the United Nations, which had brokered it.
Mr. Caputo said that the agenda for his latest initiative had been fixed beforehand to prevent Haiti's military leaders from approaching the talks as if they were an invitation to renegotiate the previous agreement, which was signed by Father Aristide and General Cedras on Governors Island in New York City in July.
The garbage-strewn streets of Port-au-Prince were emptied of most automobile and pedestrian traffic today by the latest of several strike calls by newly formed far-right political parties, which have urged the military to continue holding out against Father Aristide's return.
As heavily armed police auxiliaries, some of them equipped with machine guns, machetes and hand grenades, patrolled the streets aboard pickup trucks and paraded through the downtown area, members of these rightist parties went on the radio to argue that if Father Aristide cannot return to Haiti by tomorrow's deadline, the entire Governors Island accord should be renounced.
 
New Elections Sought
 One of the groups, the Haitian Front for Advancement and Progress, said at a rally today that new presidential elections should be organized as quickly as possible. Mr. Caputo responded strongly today to these urgings, saying that any such move would be considered a grave violation by the Security Council, which would immediately apply new sanctions here.
"That would be a fake democratic transition aimed at ignoring the will of the Haitian people," Mr. Caputo said.
Haiti's economy is already slowing dramatically under the effect of a United Nations-imposed embargo on oil and arms supplies. Diplomats estimate that the country's gas stations have only enough supplies on hand to last 12 days. Likewise, diesel supplies are thought to be as low as 24 days' worth.
The effects of the fuel squeeze can be felt throughout the country, as electricity has begun to be tightly rationed and traffic has fallen off sharply.
If new sanctions are applied, United Nations officials say that they would probably include almost all forms of trade with Haiti, excepting food, medicine and other humanitarian supplies.
 
Tensions Rising
 Tensions have risen sharply here in the final days before Father Aristide's originally scheduled return date. For the first time in several days here, a United States warship appeared in plain view in the harbor to reinforce the message of the sanctions. In addition to the increasingly frequent patrols of the police auxiliaries, dead bodies have begun to turn up with frequency each night in the streets of the capital.
Late Thursday a group of the auxiliaries, known here as attaches, followed a journalist's car downtown, waving guns in his direction before driving off and shooting a dog dead in the street. Early this morning, the bodies of two people were found shot nearby, close to the entrance of the Holiday Inn hotel.
Diplomats say that the pressure of economic sanctions and the resurgence of reports of heavy involvement by Haitian Army leaders in drug trafficking have increased tensions among units of the nation's military. The army command has issued two statements in the last 24 hours denying any involvement with cocaine trafficking.
A signed letter published earlier this week by enlisted members of the Presidential Guard strongly dissented from the direction in which General Cedras and the powerful Port-au-Prince police chief, Lieut. Col. Joseph Michel Francois, were taking the country.
Diplomats said that they could not confirm the authenticity of the letter. Senior officers at the military headquarters left little doubt, however, that they were taking it seriously.
"If I knew who they were, I'd kill them all," said a high-ranking officer who spoke on condition of anonymity. "They have no place in this army."

Boat People Face Prosecution
 PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Oct. 29 (Special to The New York Times) -- Four of the 15 Haitian boat people repatriated here early this week after being picked up at sea by United States ships could be indicted by the public prosecutor as early as Wednesday on charges of organizing their aborted voyage, a United States Embassy spokesman said today.
Refugee advocates in the United States have criticized the Clinton Administration policy of summarily repatriating Haitian boat people picked up on the high seas because of the violence and political unrest here.
"The repatriation policy should be halted until President Aristide is restored," said Steven Forester, supervising attorney for the Haitian Refugee Center in Miami. "Until that is achieved, don't hand these people back to be prosecuted."
Mr. Forester said he had been told by diplomats that the returnees had been released. But it could not be confirmed late Friday.

HEADLINE: SHIFT ON CAMERAS BY NORTH KOREANS

BYLINE: By TIM WEINER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
North Korea continues to block thorough inspections of suspected nuclear-weapons sites, but will allow the resumption of routine maintenance of surveillance cameras, international atomic-energy officials and diplomats said today.
The North Korean concession on the cameras forestalls a threat of possible United Nations sanctions, but falls short of the regular inspections the country had promised the International Atomic Energy Agency. 
 Two sets of negotiations on the issue continue. The talks have been low-key but the stakes are high, said James Lilley, the American Ambassador to South Korea from 1986 to 1989.
In continuing talks at the United Nations, State Department representatives have held out the possibility of political and economic inducements to Pyongyang if they resolve the nuclear inspection issue. But they have told North Korean diplomats that serious talks about normalizing relations are impossible until it is resolved.
 
An Offer on War Games
 In three meetings at the demilitarized zone dividing North and South Korea, South Korea has offered to cancel a joint military exercise with the United States, in 1994 if the North will agree to exchange presidential emissaries and talk seriously about its nuclear program, diplomats said.
In January 1991, North Korea agreed to let the International Atomic Energy Agency inspect its nuclear sites, which include a small reactor, two large reactors under construction and a suspected plutonium reprocessing plant. An impasse arose in late 1992 when the agency began to suspect the North was concealing stocks of reprocessed plutonium, which can be used to make a nuclear weapon. Since the summer, North Korea has refused to allow maintenance on the cameras, and their batteries and film supply are due to run out around the end of the month.
In February, the agency challenged North Korea to allow full inspection of two suspected nuclear-waste sites. North Korea responded by threatening to withdraw from the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty. In April, the Clinton Administration decided to negotiate directly with North Korea.
 
Meeting Raised Hope
 Robert L. Gallucci, the assistant secretary of state for politico-military affairs, and North Korea's deputy foreign minister, Kang Jok Su, met in New York in June and in Geneva in July. Those talks raised hopes of the possibility of reduced political tensions between North and South Korea if the nuclear-inspection issue could be resolved.
North Korea's continuing refusal to allow outside inspections raised the possibility that a third meeting between the two might be canceled. It will not take place until the issue is resolved, diplomats said.
Negotiating with the North Koreans has been frustrating, said Arnold Kanter, an undersecretary of state in the Bush Administration who met with North Korean officials last year.
"It's an exquisitely isolated, insulated country," he said. "People on the outside really don't understand what they're up to, and God only knows what they perceive about the outside world. Perhaps they think the security of their country is at stake and that the only thing to guarantee its survival is the nuclear option. So they play that card for all it's worth."

HEADLINE: Mayoral Agenda: A periodic report on key issues facing the election winner -- Education;
Shaken School System Presents Major Challenges

BYLINE: By JOSH BARBANEL

 BODY:
From the day he takes office, the victor in Tuesday's mayoral election in New York City will have to take up the problems and the promise of the largest public school system in the country, in a major test of the future of public education in America.
He will have to find a way to renew public confidence in a shaken and divided public education system, while cutting spending to close a $2 billion long-term city budget gap. He will have to lead the fight for more state school aid, while coming up with $1.2 billion to pay for a five-year backlog in school maintenance. 
 In the face of these challenges, Mayor David N. Dinkins and his Republican-Liberal opponent, Rudolph W. Giuliani, both came up with plans that promise more innovations than cash.
Both say they want to push power downward to parents, principals and teachers. Both support new small high schools begun by the last Schools Chancellor. Both want better security, improved maintenance and schools that function as neighborhood centers.
Their main difference concerns how the Board of Education should be structured. The Mayor wants to get control of the board by expanding its membership and appointing a majority. Mr. Giuliani supports the establishment of five borough boards and the elimination of a central one.
 
Conflicts Over Money
 Many of the conflicts between mayors and the school system have centered on money: the city raises it, the schools spend it. What is unclear from the candidates' platforms is whether either man is willing to make schools a top priority and exert the leadership and the financial resources needed to rebuild the system.
The number of students has been growing -- it is at one million -- but when inflation is taken into account the city is spending about 8 percent less per student than it did in 1990. City budget officials attributed much of the reduction to cuts in state aid.
Schools are overcrowded, and the system faces a backlog of more than $1.2 billion in school maintenance and repairs -- 40,000 separate requests for everything from door locks to new roofs. Class size, even in the early grades, rose between 1990 and 1992 -- enough to eliminate the need for 180 teachers from kindergarten to third grade.
The cuts appear to have hurt spending on teachers and general education, which dropped by 5.6 percent in real terms, rather than ancillary services like school buses, leases and special education. According to the Board of Education, just under half of the school system's $8 billion budget goes to instruction.
 
Battling for State Financing
 For Mr. Dinkins, as for other mayors before him, one of the largest financial issues is getting a fair share of state financing. New York City has 37 percent of the state's students but receives 34 percent of aid from the State Legislature, a loss of $274 million last year, city officials said. Mr. Dinkins joined the Board of Education in suing the state over the difference.
Joyce F. Brown, a Deputy Mayor, said that Mr. Dinkins has already laid the groundwork for his vision for the future. He pledges to continue to provide for community groups to keep some schools open as community centers. Last year, 16 of the 1,069 school buildings were open after hours and during weekends. Four more opened in September, said Frank Sobrino, a Board of Education spokesman, with 12 more scheduled to be opened by the end of October.
He calls for the use of $200 million in surpluses from the Municipal Assistance Corporation to provide an endowment for public education to provide grants to innovative schools, and an extension of the school day and year, when funds become available. In the past he opposed efforts to use the surpluses for the schools.
Mr. Giuliani has also called for turning schools across the city into community centers. He said that he, too, wants to get more money out of Albany. But in a meeting with school advocates in July he dismissed the lawsuit as a "dumb way" to get results.
Instead, he said he would cut down on the bureaucracy, shift the money to pay for teachers and prove to the Legislature that additional school spending would not be wasted. Mr. Giuliani has also said he would pursue a demonstration project to see whether private contractors can do a better job of running schools than the school system. He cited an experiment in Baltimore in which a private company, Educational Alternatives, is subcontracting with the school board to run schools -- and make a profit.
 
Conflicts With the Board
 New York's is a school system that sometimes seems to be at war with itself, where during the last four years the influence of the Mayor on the Board of Education has sunk to perhaps its lowest level ever.
And after four years of social warfare, budget pressure, political maneuvering and declining mayoral influence, the system has lost the confidence of most New Yorkers -- fewer than one in seven voters now say they are satisfied with the quality of the schools.
While Mr. Dinkins wants to shift authority from the central bureaucracy to the schools, he is also pressing for legislation, as other mayors have done before him, to give the mayor control of an expanded central Board of Education by having him appoint 6 of 11 members.
It is a telling shift in position for the Mayor -- in part a response to a structural change in city government and in part to the difficulties of dealing with a large, unwieldy system -- with one million students, 65,000 teachers, 32 local school boards and 6,000 bureaucrats, the largest in the country.
During the 1989 campaign Mr. Dinkins, the challenger, said that he did not believe that mayoral control of the Board of Education, which the incumbent sought, was an important issue "because the mayor has considerable control through budgets and through the supports of his office."
But once in office, Mr. Dinkins was increasingly at odds with four of the seven members of the Board of Education, to which each of the five borough presidents appoints one member and the mayor appoints two.
 
Patronage and Social Issues
 At first the issue was patronage, but gradually it spread to social issues -- like the "rainbow curriculum" and condoms in the classroom -- which led to the ouster last spring of Schools Chancellor Joseph A. Fernandez.
The victor in the mayoral election will inherit a board majority and a Schools Chancellor who are not directly accountable to the mayor. A non-mayoral appointee is now president of the board -- the first in seven years -- and in late August the board appointed a new Chancellor, Ramon C. Cortines, over the Mayor's objections.
Whether Mr. Dinkins or Mr. Giuliani can regain authority over the board remains an open question. The Mayor and his supporters attribute the loss in influence to changes in the city charter, which took effect in Mr. Dinkins's first year in office, that limit the budget powers of the borough presidents, thereby making them less dependent on the mayor.
His opponents attributed the shift in the balance of power to staff bungling and a lack of attention by the Mayor. They noted that the critical vote in the decision not to renew Mr. Fernandez's contract was made by Carol A. Gresser, now the board president, who was appointed by Borough President Claire Shulman of Queens, who is a Democrat.
While the Mayor blames his trouble with the board on board members "recruited as pawns in a cynical game of Republican mayoral politics," it is not clear that Mr. Giuliani would have an easier time with a board celebrated for its contentiousness. In any event, the terms of all board members expire next June.
 
Borough Boards
 Mr. Giuliani is instead supporting, with some caveats, legislation proposed by two Democratic borough presidents to shift the powers of the Board of Education to five borough boards, and to the 32 school districts and 1,069 schools. He said it was a way to shift more money from the bureaucracy into the classroom.
Mr. Giuliani's position on the issue was shaped, campaign officials say, by Edward N. Costikyan, a Democratic leader involved in the decentralization of New York City schools in 1969, who drafted the borough board bill for Mrs. Shulman and Borough President Ruth W. Messinger of Manhattan.
Mr. Giuliani expressed doubts about the plan in July, citing a concern that it would add to patronage and waste, but announced his support in August, adding that he wanted to see a provision limiting the combined spending of the borough boards to no more than what is now spent centrally.
Ms. Messinger attacked Mr. Giuliani's position, complaining that by supporting her legislation he had "flip-flopped."
Mrs. Shulman welcomed Mr. Giuliani's support. "If Ed Costikyan is his adviser, he knows what he is doing," she said. "I support the Mayor strongly; his heart is in the right place. His sentiment is for the children. But I disagree with him on education."
 
Student Performance
 Student performance has been stagnant at best. Mr. Dinkins or Mr. Giuliani will have to find a way to nurture reforms and demand accountability or, some say, see the system of public education disintegrate under competitive pressures from the voucher movement, private schools and other alternative forms of education.
"The public schools are either going to make it in the next several years or they are going to go out of business," said Robert Berne, an associate dean at the Wagner School at New York University and the staff director of a major study of educational reform in New York.
Four years ago, when the mayoral campaigns turned to education the candidates focused on sweeping systemwide issues like the elimination of building tenure for principals or a simplified system for hiring teachers.
 
Power to the Parents
 Now both candidates have grounded their campaigns in school-by-school reforms, and say they support putting the money and power into the hands of those dealing directly with the children.
"I demand intense and radical change in our school system," Mr. Dinkins said in his major campaign speech on education last month. "Change that puts schools into the hands of parents and principals and neighborhoods."
Mr. Giuliani took a similar view in his position paper on education. "Across the country, educational reform has centered on management at the school level," he said. "Our central bureaucracy is such that these innovations too often occur despite, rather than because of it. Clearly we need a system that encourages creativity at the school level."
Behind this change is the limited school self rule, known as school-based management, in which parents, teachers and administrators share decisions.
Both candidates support more and better school security, improved school maintenance and reform of the costly and sometimes ineffective special education programs. They talk about accountability. Mr. Giuliani at once calls for a "back to basics" approach and for support for multicultural programs "inculcating racial and ethnic pride."
 
 
 
THE SERIES SO FAR
 The Mayoral Agenda series previously examined the city's impending budget crisis, Sept. 27; social services issues, Oct. 2; crime, Oct. 11; the economy, Oct. 18, and the city's infrastructure needs, on Thursday.

HEADLINE: With European Union's Arrival, Fears on Economy Cast a Shadow

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BRUSSELS, Oct. 29

 BODY:
This was to have been a day for the leaders of the European Community to celebrate finally the coming into effect of the Treaty on European Union. But instead, the summit meeting today was overshadowed by anxiety about growing unemployment and mounting welfare costs in their recession-stricken continent.
In a communique, the European Community leaders agreed to work more closely together to create jobs and economic growth after the Treaty on European Union goes into effect on Monday. 
 But even today, the 12 countries bickered for hours about how to divide 11 new European agencies among themselves before agreeing on Frankfurt as the site of the European central bank, which would some day administer the common European currency foreseen in the pact.
 
Ratified With Difficulty
 The treaty is known as the Maastricht treaty after the Dutch city in which it was signed. It was ratified late and with great difficulty by national electorates and elected Parliaments, which feared that Europe's separate nations would be swallowed up in a federal state dominated by powerful bureaucrats in Brussels.
"The debate has been salutary," the leaders declared here today at the end of an all-day meeting. "It has revealed weaknesses. It is now important to bring the union treaty to life."
Negotiated two years ago, the treaty had been scheduled to go into effect last January. It lays out a plan to link European currencies tightly enough together by 1997 to allow at least some countries to use a common currency.
But the European monetary system nearly came apart last summer under the strain of economic imbalances caused by recession and the reunification of Germany in 1990. Now, despite vows by the leaders today to try again, it is not clear that a common currency allowing the others to escape the domination of the German mark can come into effect even by the end of the century.
 
A Better Future
 Germany was the last country to ratify the pact formally, earlier this month. Chancellor Helmut Kohl and other leaders came together here today to acknowledge the mistakes and difficulties of the past and to vow to make the economic future better than the troubled present.
"The citizens of Europe know that the Community has brought them an end of bloody wars, a higher level of prosperity and greater influence," their declaration said. "They know that today, even more than yesterday, isolation and retrenchment are false solutions, always illusory and sometimes dangerous."
But there was no discussion here today of the threat of a trade war, which could follow failure of a global agreement on tariffs and trade, imperiled by a dispute between the United States and France over agricultural subsidies.
With the total number of unemployed in their countries now approaching 20 million, the European leaders agreed today to try to come up over the next six weeks with a set of principles to stimulate more growth. But leader after leader acknowledged that unemployment would not go away when economic growth resumed, because for decades European labor costs had risen faster than productivity.
 
A Concern About Jobs
 "The Community's share of world markets has declined by about a fifth since 1980," Prime Minister John Major of Britain told his colleagues. "If we had been as good at creating jobs as other industrial countries over the same period, there would be nine million more people in work in the Community today."
He noted that since their last summit in June, about 250,000 more people had been forced onto Europe's unemployment rolls.
Britain did not agree at Maastricht to a catalogue of pledges to preserve the achievements of Europe's social welfare states. The 11 other leaders signed that part of the agreement then, but recently Germany, France and Italy have all scaled back generous unemployment benefits and companies all over Europe have eliminated thousands of jobs and threatened to cut wages to increase productivity.
Today, tens of thousands of Belgian workers demonstrated in anger in the streets of Brussels as the leaders met, following similar demonstrations in Bonn and strikes in France and Italy.
 
Homes for Agencies
 The concrete achievements of the politicians here were very modest. Mr. Major gave up on trying to get the European Monetary Institute, the forerunner of a European Central Bank, located in London instead of Frankfurt, though Britain does not want to join the single currency even when it is established. The monetary institute is to begin work in Frankfurt on Jan. 1.
The European leaders also decided that Europol, an organization for closer coordination of the fight against organized crime and drug smuggling, would be based at The Hague, and that the European environmental agency would be based in Copenhagen.
The leaders will meet here again Dec. 10-11 to discuss proposals to be worked out by Jacques Delors, the president of the European Community's Executive Commission, to combat the worst economic structural crisis on the continent since World War II.
 
A Call to Use Technology
 Mr. Delors, in a speech today, called for greater investment in transportation, telecommunications and other technological fields as a way to cope with the unemployment problems.
The leaders also vowed today to make a new start toward another of the Maastricht treaty's goals, a common foreign and security policy. They condemned recent atrocities by Croatian forces in the Bosnian village of Stupni Do and called once again for an end to all violence in the Balkans. They vowed to send more peacekeeping troops and said they would use "all appropriate means" to keep food, medicine and other relief aid flowing to victims of the fighting in Bosnia and Herzegovina, calling for specified sanctuary routes to allow passage of aid.
Diplomats conceded that these appeals probably would be no more effective than past ones and said the main thrust of the renewed attempt to formulate common European foreign policy would be elsewhere: toward the Middle East, South Africa and Russia, where expectations of success were more modest and realistic.
