HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Big Loss and Job Cuts for Amdahl

BYLINE: By LAWRENCE M. FISHER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Oct. 29

 BODY:
The Amdahl Corporation, the struggling mainframe computer manufacturer, reported a third-quarter loss today far in excess of analysts' already-pessimistic estimates.
The company also said it had suspended its quarterly dividend and would eliminate 1,800 jobs. 
 Amdahl said the $275.7 million loss reflected substantial restructuring charges associated with cutbacks in employment, manufacturing capacity and selected product-development programs, as well as other cutbacks tied to continued weakness in the markets for large-scale computing systems. The company, which is based in Sunnyvale, Calif., said it was reorganizing its operations to bring its expenses in line with its lower business volume.
Amdahl released its results hours after the stock market closed today and postponed a conference call with securities analysts until Monday. Earlier, Amdahl's shares ended trading at $4.625, up 12.5 cents, on the American Stock Exchange.
 
Mainframes Called Vulnerable
 Analysts said Amdahl's core business, large mainframe computers, was particularly vulnerable to economic downturns. And they said that unlike the International Business Machines Corporation, its main competitor, Amdahl lacked other lines to fall back on. Reports of the demise of the mainframe have become a self-fulfilling prophecy, prompting large customers to defer purchases or consider alternatives, these analysts said.
For the quarter, which ended Sept. 24, Amdahl's net loss of $275.7 million was about nine times its net loss of $30.8 million in the 1992 third quarter. Revenues dropped 33.2 percent to $393.7 million from $588.8 million in the third quarter of 1992. This year's third-quarter results included pretax restructuring charges of $235 million; the 1992 quarter included pretax work-force reduction charges of $19 million.
Along with reducing its total work force by about 1,800 people, the company said it would eliminate a half million or more square feet of unneeded plants. The costs of those cutbacks have been included in the third-quarter restructuring charges. Some reductions will take place immediately, while others will be carried out over the next several quarters, with savings from these actions expected to be realized in 1994 and 1995.
Amdahl already cut its 8,500-member work force by about 1,100, or 13 percent, in late May.
Amdahl said it intended to pursue additional strategic alliances, like the agreement it entered last month to resell certain computers made by Sun Microsystems Inc. and another pact signed earlier in the year with the Electronic Data Systems unit of General Motors to jointly create and market applications development and production software.
The company said that uncertain demand and volatile pricing made it difficult to forecast future operating results.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Job Cutbacks Ahead at Dun & Bradstreet

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The Dun & Bradstreet Corporation, a leading supplier of business information, said yesterday that it would cut several thousand jobs over the next two years.
The company said the reductions were in response to a weak global economy and would be made through attrition and possible layoffs. Dun & Bradstreet, whose operations include the Moody's bond rating company and Nielsen television service, has 52,000 employees worldwide. 
 As part of the cuts, Dun & Bradstreet's information services unit, based in Murray Hill, N.J., has offered voluntary severance packages to its 7,000 United States employees. The goal is to eliminate several hundred jobs in the division, a spokesman for the unit, David Monfried, said. The workers have until Nov. 5 to sign up for the package, which offers up to 12 weeks of pay and additional money based on years of service.
Mr. Monfried said the cuts were partly a result of the slow United States economy, which was hindering business demand for Dun & Bradstreet services.
Indeed, one of Dun & Bradstreet's own surveys, released on Wednesday, showed that small-business owners have grown more pessimistic than ever about the nation's economic future.
Dun & Bradstreet reported earlier this month that its third-quarter earnings rose 5.7 percent but that revenue slipped 2.7 percent, partly a result of weakness in its directory information and software services businesses.
Among its other businesses are the Nielsen market research and television ratings services and Moody's Investors Service, the credit rating agency.
Word of the planned cutbacks came after the stock market had closed. Shares of Dun & Bradstreet finished at $67, up 37.5 cents, on the New York Stock Exchange.

SECTION: Section 1; Page 23; Column 3; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 871 words

HEADLINE: Incentives of $31 Million Keep Kidder, Peabody in New York

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK

 BODY:
In the latest high-priced deal to persuade a company not to move to the suburbs, Mayor David N. Dinkins said yesterday that Kidder, Peabody & Company, the Wall Street firm, had accepted $31 million in incentives and would move its headquarters from lower Manhattan to Rockefeller Center.
The deal, in the wake of a series of corporate relocations out of New York City, came at a politically sensitive time, since Rudolph W. Giuliani, Mr. Dinkins's opponent in the mayoral election on Tuesday, has repeatedly criticized the Mayor for losing too many battles to hold onto companies. 
 It came a day after Brown Brothers Harriman & Company, another Wall Street firm, said it would move 550 workers, about half its Manhattan work force, to Jersey City.
In early October, Lehman Brothers Inc., another Wall Street giant, said it would move 900 back-office employees to Jersey City, but would not move 5,000 other Manhattan employees, as had been feared by City Hall.
"The decision by Kidder, Peabody demonstrates in dramatic fashion that our job retention strategies are working," said Mr Dinkins, who has repeatedly described his efforts to retain big business as one of the strengths of his first term. The mayor claims credit for keeping 35,000 jobs in the city, in most cases through the use of tax incentives.
 
Lining Up at City Hall
 Others close to the deal said it came at a time when companies, hoping to extract incentives from the city, have been lining up at City Hall.
"Obviously there has been a flurry of activity that is tied to the upcoming election," said one high-ranking official of the Dinkins administration, who spoke on condition of anonymity. He added that companies are trying to negotiate with the city now because of "uncertainty about who they would be dealing with" after the election.
Kidder, Peabody, a unit of the General Electric Company, said it would move its headquarters and 2,000 jobs from its current offices in lower Manhattan to 30 Rockefeller Plaza, which is owned by General Electric.
It said the rest of its Manhattan work force of about 1,000 workers, most of them now assigned to a data-processing center in lower Manhattan, would be moved to another site near Wall Street that has not been determined. Leases on the company's current headquarters and data-processing center will expire in 1995, and the company is known to have been looking for new sites for more than a year.
A Kidder spokeswoman, Helen Keehner, said yesterday that the company had been "very close" to a decision to move its data-processing operations to Jersey City and its headquarters to Stamford.
The incentives Kidder will receive are in many ways similar to those extended to a host of large corporations that have threatened to leave since the mid-1980's. In exchange for its commitment to keep its 3,000 workers in Manhattan for 15 years, the company is to receive subsidized electricity, sales tax breaks on equipment and services it buys and property-tax reductions worth a total of $31 million.
The incentives "enable Kidder, Peabody to continue to operate in Manhattan on a cost-competitive basis," said Michael A. Carpenter, the firm's chairman.
In an interview, Carl Weisbrod, president of the city's Economic Development Corporation, who has been one of Mr. Dinkins's chief corporate negotiators, said the Kidder, Peabody deal was considered particularly important because of the firm's prominence on Wall Street.
"From our perspective, Kidder is the last remaining large investment bank that was at risk" of leaving the city, he said. Several others, including Morgan Stanley and Company and Prudential Bache Securities, have accepted similar incentives from the city over the last two years after they, like Kidder, threatened to move to New Jersey or Connecticut.
In the case of Morgan Stanley, which threatened to move its Manhattan work force of 4,100 workers to Stamford, the Dinkins administration last year agreed to an incentive package worth $39.6 million.
 
Incentives Criticized
 Some officials, including Mark Green, the former Consumer Affairs Commissioner who is now the Democratic candidate for public advocate, have criticized the Mayor's use of incentives as a huge loss of future tax revenues in a game of corporate bluff with executives who may have no real intention of leaving Manhattan, particularly at a time when real estate prices have plummeted.
But that criticism has not become a major point of contention between the mayoral candidates. While Mr. Dinkins takes credit for saving jobs, Mr. Giuliani has repeatedly criticized the Mayor for allowing too many companies to leave, despite the city incentives.
Regardless of who wins Tuesday's election, no one expects large companies to stop seeking incentives from the city when their leases are due to expire.
But Mr. Weisbrod said yesterday that the incentives to keep Kidder, Peabody were well worth their price because the firm, along with other Wall Street giants that have been persuaded to stay, will bolster New York City's position as a global center of finance.
"By obtaining this commitment we have, in effect, assured our financial dominance well into the 21st century," he said.

HEADLINE: Two Ways to Run a City;
Mayoral Candidates Offer a Clear Choice Between Opposing Views of Government

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL

 BODY:
Beyond the questions of competence and temperament, crime and race, the 1993 campaign for mayor of New York City has offered voters two candidates with sharply different philosophies of local government.
David N. Dinkins and Rudolph W. Giuliani have not met this time in a debate to spell out those ideologies, and the campaign has often wandered into more visceral diversions. But the differences between the two men emerge with great clarity from their campaign statements, position papers and weeks of appearances in the five boroughs of the city. 
 Reared in New York City's Democratic Party, Mr. Dinkins, who is 66 years old, is in many ways a traditional New Deal liberal with an expansive vision of government. He speaks with pride of New York's Democratic heritage, stretching from Franklin D. Roosevelt. His associates say the Mayor remains firmly convinced that while liberal policies may have been rejected in other parts of the country, they are the very essence of what makes New York New York.
Mr. Giuliani, 49, models himself after Republican and centrist Democratic mayors who favor reducing government, encouraging competition and asking the poor to take more individual responsibility. Mr. Giuliani, the Republican-Liberal candidate, says that New York can learn a lot from cities like Philadelphia or Indianapolis.
The race has even challenged a maxim of municipal politics: that there is no Democratic or Republican way to take out the garbage. Mr. Giuliani says that if as Mayor he could not persuade sanitation workers to change their work methods to insure an eight-hour day, then turning part of the Sanitation Department's work over to private collectors is an "option that you have to consider."
The clash of philosophies also extends into different visions of how a mayor should reach out to the multiethnic city: whether, like Mr. Dinkins, he should celebrate all the constituent parts of New York's "gorgeous mosaic" or, like Mr. Giuliani, call on New Yorkers to transcend race, gender, sexual preference and ideology and view their "common bonds."
The mayor of New York, of course, must operate within many restrictions. There are court orders, legislative mandates and fiscal constraints. He is required to balance the budget, which limits what even the most devoted liberal can spend on social problems. But even within the constraints, which both candidates acknowledge, they have found different approaches.
Mr. Dinkins spelled out his vision of government in his Inaugural Address in 1990. "This administration will renew the quest for social justice," he said that New Year's Day, when George Bush still controlled the White House. "We will speak out against national policies that offer cold indifference instead of compassion. And we will set our priorities to strengthen the weak, to help those who have been hurt, and to keep the middle class living in New York City."
In office, when economic recession and dire budgetary straits allowed, he has emphasized social activism by city government. His programs have been much more limited than he had envisioned in his last campaign, but he points with pride to the creation of programs to lower infant mortality and health-care clinics for the poor. He says the homeless have a right to housing. He hails a modest initiative to keep some schools around the city open on nights and weekends as the "21st-century version of settlement houses."
 
'Reinventing Government'
 Even his promised approach to job creation in a second term is activist government -- a municipal version of industrial policy, with the city using an economic development bank and venture capital funds to help industries grow.
Mr. Giuliani, a former Federal prosecutor, likes to point to experiments by fiscally conservative Mayors across the country in "reinventing government" by improving management, encouraging competition and contracting out government services.
Unlike Mr. Dinkins, Mr. Giuliani argues that the scope of government must be reconsidered to reduce the city's tax burden and because increases in federal aid are unlikely. He would seek to privatize three to four of the city's municipal hospitals. He has also said that Kennedy International Airport should be turned over to private concerns. He has pledged to cut roughly 35,000 jobs from the city work force over four years.
His approach to social programs is to try to encourage more individual responsibility, arguing that to do otherwise is to foster passivity in the name of compassion. He has proposed a 90-day limit on stays in homeless shelters for all but the "chronically homeless" to encourage people to seek other solutions to their lack of housing. He would also stop using the shelter system as a route toward city-provided apartments, saying such a system discourages the work ethic.
The two candidates also have significant philosophical differences on how government should relate to the racial, ethnic and sexual identities of its constituents.
Mr. Dinkins, who describes New York as a "gorgeous mosaic," has worked aggressively to bring minorities and women into government. "He's been taught by a lifetime to hate unfairness," begins one of his closing-stretch campaign commercials.
Mr. Dinkins created an affirmative-action program that directs 20 percent of the city's $3 billion in contracts to companies owned by women or minorities, even when their bids are as much as 10 percent higher than those of other companies.
 
Promoting Diversity
 And the city's first black Mayor speaks proudly of the diversity of his City Hall. "The way he constructed the administration there are more women, Latinos, lesbians and gays, and African-Americans in city government than there were under any mayor ever," said Andrea Bernstein, Mr. Dinkins's deputy campaign manager.
"Our argument is unless you are willing to face the fact that this is a diverse, multicultural city and deal with that, you'll never be able to correct historical inequalities," she said.
Mr. Giuliani's approach embraces the critique by neoconservative commentators of what they have described as a wave of "political correctness" sweeping the nation's campuses and cultural life. Such writers have argued that the nation, once a melting pot, is being torn asunder by "identity politics" in which racial minorities, women and homosexuals proclaim group identities as separate from and victimized by the majority culture. The commentators have also contended that ideological intolerance by members of such groups has stopped political debate.
For months, Mr. Giuliani has called on his audiences to look at their "common bonds" as New Yorkers, with common concerns about schools and safety. "We have to also look at the things that make us the same," he has said, over and over.
 
Appointments on Merit
 Mr. Giuliani says he would "work hard" to make his administration representative of the city, but he also says he would make appointments on merit, "irrespective of race, religion, ethnic background, sexual orientation."
"Right now in the city administration," he said at a campaign appearance this week, "it appears as if the principal reason why people are selected is symbolic. The principal reason is to create a representative government rather than to select the most talented person."
He opposes the city's affirmative-action program for women and minority contractors, contending that City Hall should not subsidize the contractors if they cannot be as cost-efficient as their competitors. He would instead create development banks as another way to help minority businesses.
Whoever wins next Tuesday will be based on many imponderables, including Mr. Dinkins's record, Mr. Giuliani's image, the racial identification of the voters, and which campaign best gets out the vote. But it may also have a lot to do with which candidate has a better philosophical reading of New York.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Brewer Revives a European Tradition in Texas

BYLINE: By FLORENCE FABRICANT

 BODY:
Pierre Celis is about to prove you can go home again. The 68-year-old Belgian brewer, who sold his brewery in Belgium to a giant brewer several years ago, has been making beer in Texas for the last year and a half. Now, he is going to sell beer in Belgium once again.
Regardless of whether his beers should be classified as Belgian-style Texas beer or Texas-style Belgian beer, there is no question that Mr. Celis, a Ross Perot-size man who often wears a bolo tie, has carved out a niche for himself in the beer business. He has sucessfully established himself as one of the leaders in the growing international taste for white, or wheat, beer. 
 Celis beers, made in a small new brewery in Austin, are sold widely in the United States. But in November, Celis White Austin Texas Beer will be made and sold in Belgium. So no one gets the wrong idea about where Mr. Celis's loyalties lie, the label shows a cowboy wrangling a longhorn, an attention-getter for a country where beers are often made in monasteries.
 
Light, Fruity Beer
 White beers, known as wheat beers because of the high percentage of wheat in the mash, are traditionally light and fruity. They recently have been rediscovered in Germany and Belgium, and have caught the fancy of America's boutique brewers. In the Bavarian region of Germany, wheat beers account for 30 percent of all beer sold. Belgium now has 15 brands, up from none in 1966.
In America, nearly half of all microbreweries are making wheat beer, especially in the West, said David Edgar, the assistant director of the Institute for Brewing Studies in Denver. Wheat beers account for about 10 percent of America's microbrewery production of about one million barrels with some well-known brands like Samuel Adams and August Schell in wide distribution.
America's biggest brewers have not been much for wheat. In the mid-80's the Miller Brewing Company test-marketed a German-style wheat beer called Dakota, but found consumers were not interested and dropped it.
But last summer, Garrett Oliver, the owner of Manhattan Brewing Company in SoHo, introduced a cloudy, unfiltered wheat beer and "it's taken off like a rocket," he said. "People are very curious about it."
 
Made for Centuries

 In Belgium the pale, milky-looking white beers, known as witbiers, were made for centuries, especially in and around the town of Hoegaarden (pronounced who-garden), near Liege, where Mr. Celis lived. But the beer went out of style, and by 1957 the town's last brewer of white beer closed and sold his equipment to a museum.
"In those days people wanted big new brand names, not these old-fashioned products," Mr. Celis said.
Then came the hot summer of 1966, finding Mr. Celis, who had gone into his father's livestock and dairy business, lamenting the disappearance of white beer, which is often considered summer beer because of its refreshing flavor. "When I was growing up I had worked in that brewery and I really knew what was involved in making the beer, so I decided to try to make some myself," he said.
 
'A Substantial Investment'
 He produced 100 liters of the beer, which is made with roughly equal parts of wheat and barley and also seasoned with coriander and bitter orange peel. (Wheat beers are never made entirely from wheat.)
Mr. Celis gave samples to neighbors who liked it and encouraged him. "At that point I got nervous," he said. "To continue meant making a substantial investment."
With about $10,000, he bought a small brewery and began producing 20 barrels a year. "I had success so I became more daring," he said.
By 1978 he had bought a former lemonade factory with two Dutch partners, and after converting it into a brewery, he tripled production of his Hoegaarden white beer.
Then, in 1985 the brewery was destroyed by fire. It would take the equivalent of $10 million to rebuild. "We were not well insured," he said.
 
Found Willing Backer
 But he found a willing backer in Interbrew, the giant Belgian brewer that owns Stella Artois and several other brands and commands a 1.4 percent share of the global market, according to Impact, a research service for the beverage industry. (The Anheuser-Busch Companies is the leader with a 9 percent share.)
Mr. Celis sold the brewery but his contract -- for a seven-figure sum he will not disclose -- required him to remain with Interbrew until 1990, by which time the new brewery was producing 300,000 barrels a year and was one of 15 Belgian breweries making white beer.
"When I started, it was just me and my wife, Juliette," he said. "And we conquered the world." But conqueror or not, Mr. Celis was by no means ready for an easy chair. "Selling the brewery was a crisis for me," he said.
 
Seven Acres in Texas
 His daughter, Christine, who had worked in the brewery, and his son-in-law, Peter Camps, who was also a brewer, wanted to try to make Belgian-style beer in America. The Celises knew some people in Texas, so they settled in Austin. They bought seven acres of land for $13 million and built their Texas brewery.
"America is a big market," Mr. Celis said, "but it's difficult." For a brief period he had tried to export his Belgian white beer to America, but it was too expensive. "You can't sell much beer if you have to charge $9 or $10 for a six-pack," he said.
Last year, the brewery started by making Celis White, plus a rich and hearty pale bock and a Pilsener-style golden beer. Grand Cru, a creamy, fruity ale, was added last December.
Sandy Monblatt, whose company, Sanford Trading of Sausalito, Calif., distributes Celis beers on the West Coast, said the white beer is acquiring a small but loyal following that is growing. "It will take time," he said. "People have to taste the beer to understand it."
 
Daughter Steps In
 Last year when a group of 65 Belgian brewers visited the brewery in Austin, they suggested that Mr. Celis export his beer to Belgium. "That was out of the question," Mr. Celis said, "because of the 40 percent tax imposed on American products by the European Economic Community." So his daughter arranged for a Belgian brewery to make it under license.
Surprisingly, his agreement with Interbrew did not prevent him from making beer in Belgium again. "Interbrew can't be happy about it, but there's nothing they can do to stop us," he said.

The beer will soon be available in Belgium. (Zigy Kaluzny for The New York Times)(pg. 37); Pierre Celis carved himself a niche brewing Celis White Austin Texas Beer. Made with wheat in the mash, the white beer is light and fruity. (Naum Kazhdan/The New York Times)(pg. 39)

HEADLINE: Campaign Stop: A periodic report on neighborhood reaction to the mayoral race. -- 5. Morris Heights;
Dinkins Supporters Stay the Course

BYLINE: By IAN FISHER

 BODY:
Christopher Kennedy's face registered nothing as the crack of four gunshots, perhaps a block away, echoed off the rain-dampened brick of his apartment building on University Avenue in the Bronx. He just looked up the street as he waited in the drizzle for the school bus to drop off his 9-year-old stepdaughter.
"Minorities are suffering," he said, flicking his cigarette to the sidewalk. "We're suffering a lot." 
 That is why, he said, he intends to vote for Mayor David N. Dinkins. More than Rudolph W. Giuliani, Mr. Dinkins understands the plight of the poor, Mr. Kennedy said, even if his performance has not been all that many people expected four years ago.
"Rome wasn't built in a day," Mr. Kennedy said. "So you've got to give him a little bit of time."
 
The Clear Favorite
 In Morris Heights, a poor neighborhood but by no means the city's poorest, Mr. Dinkins is the clear favorite. For some voters in this black and Hispanic area, the reason is straightforward: Mr. Dinkins is the city's first black Mayor and his opponent is white. For others, the choice has other dimensions because poorer neighborhoods rely heavily on government programs, from welfare to condominium conversions for middle-income families to programs for the handicapped. In interviews, Mr. Giuliani was often described as an enemy on these issues and Mr. Dinkins as a friend.
Support for Mr. Dinkins here appears unshakable; only one person among the dozen interviewed was planning to vote for Mr. Giuliani. The imperative for Mr. Dinkins in neighborhoods like Morris Heights, where the percentage of registered voters is extremely low, is quantity, to try to swing a tight election: how many people will turn out next Tuesday?
 
Dinkins Got 90% in 1989
 Four years ago, 90 percent of the vote in this census tract -- No. 245, which runs from Sedgwick Avenue east to University Avenue, from West 180th Street south to West Tremont Avenue -- went to Mr. Dinkins. Voter turnout was 61 percent, above the city average of 55 percent, and atypically high for a poor neighborhood.
The Dinkins campaign staff intends to get out the vote by making phone calls and even going door to door in areas like Morris Heights, where the percentage of registered voters in 1989 was extremely low, only 25 percent of the voting-age population. That figure was not expected to be much higher this time, since registration rolls have barely moved. The city average in 1989 was 56 percent, with some neighborhoods substantially higher.
Based on interviews in this census district, there appear to be at least some registered voters who do not intend cast a ballot this year.
"It's just the same thing over and over again," said Reginald Robinson, 25, who is unemployed but who is working on making a film about women in the armed services. "Each candidate bickers with each other about what changes they are going to make. But there are no changes. It's all talk."
 
Changes for the Better
 Even though skepticism has made inroads here, many residents said they had seen significant and positive changes, and offered them as reasons to vote for Mr. Dinkins.
Renovated buildings containing a check cashing place, a new grocery store and many apartments have replaced the blackened shells that stood for years along Burnside Avenue and other streets. The drug trade is still potent but has thinned out as community policing has begun to take effect. Gunfire is less frequent, despite the shots Mr. Kennedy heard. Mr. Dinkins deserves credit, residents said, even if some initiatives come from the state or Washington or began before he took office.
"There are any number of houses that have been renovated and redone under his administration," said the Rev. A. B. Duncans, 75, who has led efforts to clear his street, West 179th Street, and others of drug dealers. "There is a hope of the neighborhood coming back to where it was when I first came."
Few people said they were undecided, and only one woman said she would vote Mr. Giuliani. The woman, who would not give her name, lives in a four-room city-subsidized apartment and she is outraged that her rent has risen from $219 a month a few years ago to $400.
"It's too much money," she said. Of Mr. Giuliani, she said, "They say he looks O.K., that he is going to do better things for the people. I hope he doesn't raise things, make everything go too high."
 
Government Help
 Around Morris Heights many people mentioned programs or other help that they, their family or friends received from the government as a reason to vote for Mr. Dinkins, even in cases when the money came from sources other than city government. Alice Baez Lozada, 36, was thrilled by the construction of a wheelchair ramp in a local city park and improvements in bus service for her 20-year-old son, Edgar, who suffers from cerebral palsy.
Janet Barrett, 34, has lupus and began receiving Medicaid after her insurance ran out a year and a half ago. Lillian Rodriguez, 42, a single mother of four children and two foster children, bought her apartment on West Burnside Avenue after the city converted it into cooperatives and replaced the ceilings and windows and installed a new intercom system.
Ms. Rodriguez intends to vote for Mr. Dinkins, but not without some reservations.
"He could have done much better," she said. "He is a black Mayor and he should have done more to stop the racial problems and the corruption. But if they give him another chance I think he will make it."
As far as Mr. Giuliani is concerned, she and many others simply doubted that he cared about minorities or the poor. "I think he is going to take a lot out of poor people," she said. "He does show less compassion for us people. He doesn't seem like he wants to give."
 
Invigorated by Dinkins
 Many residents who said they supported Mr. Dinkins felt invigorated by his election as New York City's first black Mayor. There was a sense that white New Yorkers have been too quick to judge Mr. Dinkins, who they say has looked out for their interests, even when they could not name instances in which he helped them or their neighborhood.
"I felt a black mayor would give more thought to black people than a white person," said Kenneth Jones, a 37-year-old cook at Harlem Hospital, explaining his vote for Mr. Dinkins four years ago.
Four years later, Mr. Jones said he is not disappointed, though he acknowledged that Mr. Dinkins has not solved the city's most pressing problems of crime, poverty and poor relations between the races. "He did what he could do," he said, echoing the opinion of many in Morris Heights. "The city was so messed up to start with. You can't do everything in four years. I think he cares more. He knows what we are up against, the black people."

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Closes With Gains After Weakness at Opening

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
The dollar ended firmer against other currencies yesterday, recovering from a shaky start after a steep decline on Thursday.
The selloff on Thursday "confused people at first, but then we saw a resumption of the dollar's bullish trend," said William Arnold, Chemical Banking Corporation's chief dealer and vice president for global securities foreign exchange. 
 In late New York trading, the dollar rose to 1.6858 German marks from 1.6740 marks on Thursday. It also rose to 108.65 Japanese yen, from 108.17 yen.
Gold prices were slightly lower. On the Commodity Exchange in New York, gold for December delivery declined 60 cents, to $369.60 an ounce.
Dealers noted that the dollar's gains were made almost entirely on a midafternoon flurry of buying, exaggerated by extremely thin volume. "This week's volatility gave many people an opportunity to lose money," said Richard Vullo, a Bank of Montreal corporate dealer. "People didn't need excuses to take a breath."
Another dealer said there was "a short squeeze on a very quiet afternoon." This meant that traders who had sold dollars in anticipation of buying them back at a lower price were forced to cover those short positions as the dollar instead rose in value.
Some currency traders stayed out of the market yesterday in advance of a holiday weekend in Europe. Several European markets will be closed on Monday in observance of All Saints' Day.
Reports yesterday on the American economy had limited effect on the dollar in early trading.
The Chicago Purchasing Management Index for October, which rose to 57 percent from 54.5 percent in September, exceeded market expectations, but did not set off a rally in the dollar's exchange price.
And the Government reported that September personal income and spending rose two-tenths and three-tenths of a percent, respectively, roughly in line with forecasts.
Dealers said the University of Michigan survey of consumer sentiment, which traders said rose to 82.7 in its final October report, from 77.9 for September, got a mixed reception.
"The month-to-month increase was good, but the final October number was not as strong as some interim numbers," said Michael McKeaver at Fuji Bank.

HEADLINE: Cost Curbs Help Macy Cut Losses

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM

 BODY:
R. H. Macy & Company, which operates the Macy's, Bullock's and I. Magnin store chains, tripled its cash flow in September by keeping a tight rein on expenses, according to documents filed in United States Bankruptcy Court yesterday.
The company almost broke even last month, posting a $600,000 operating loss compared with an $18.8 million loss in the corresponding period last year. Sales, however, dipped slightly as the company continued to struggle against weak economies in California and the Northeast, where it has the most stores. 
 Macy has been operating under bankruptcy protection since January 1992.
"Clearly the aggressive steps we have been taking to restructure our operations and systems and to reduce expenses are now beginning to have their desired effect," Myron E. Ullman 3d, Macy's chairman and chief executive, said in a statement.
The company's junk bonds traded at their highest levels in more than a year yesterday, with the most senior class of bonds trading at $41 at one point during the day.
 
Topped Its Own Goal
 For the fiscal year that ended on July 31, the company topped its own goal with $210.1 million in cash flow, in contrast to a negative cash flow of $38.9 million in fiscal 1992, year-end documents show. Macy had earlier predicted its 1993 cash flow would be $204 million.
"The year-over-year swing in operating cash flow is impressive and indicates significant progress in profit margin improvement and expenses," said David H. Ford, senior analyst at the Delaware Bay Company.
Sales declined 2.3 percent in fiscal 1993, to $6.3 billion, in part because Macy closed stores and eliminated much of its consumer electronics business. At stores open for at least a year, sales rose 1.7 percent in 1993.
The improvement in Macy's business, in contrast to the sales slippage, shows how far the company has pared its costs. In September, for example, its expenses decreased 19 percent. Had the company not cut costs so drastically, its cash flow last month would have been less than in the same period last year.
 
Cost Control a Top Priority
 "Controlling their costs was their No. 1 priority, and they're proving they can do that," said Jack Hersh, director of research at M. J. Whitman L.P., which trades distressed securities.
Despite its progress, Macy is not likely to emerge from bankruptcy before the spring of 1995.
"They've laid the path for improved performance this year, but I don't anticipate them coming out of bankruptcy any earlier than 1995," said Walter F. Loeb, president of Loeb Associates Inc., a retail consulting firm.
But many players in the junk bond market are keeping an eye on next March, when Macy's period of exclusivity -- the period in which it alone can present a plan of reorganization to its creditors -- will be up. At that time, some creditors who hold its bank debt and senior bonds might try to push the company out of bankruptcy before 1995 because they would be more likely to receive equity, and a stake in Macy's future, in an early reorganization.
Most analysts say, however, that the judge presiding over the Macy bankruptcy will probably just extend the period of exclusivity.

HEADLINE: Advice for a Fraction of the Price of a Personalized Plan

 BODY:
Can't afford a financial planner? With planners' bills often reaching thousands of dollars, the problem is hardly rare. Luckily, alternatives exist.
The telephone is one option. For $3.95 a minute, a caller to 800 ASK-A-CPA can get financial advice from an accountant or certified financial planner. "We want people to call us back and back and back, so we try to answer questions as quickly as possible," said John Howland, a partner in the venture, called Money Minds of Glenview, Ill. 
 Along with telephone advice, Money Minds offers written financial plans. For $495, a customer will receive a complete plan plus a year of unlimited phone consultations.
Economy-minded clients can find good deals with in-person planners, too. "Sometimes, a planner will sit down with you on a one-time basis for a few hours and get you on the right financial track," said Barbara Roper of the Consumer Federation of America. The cost, a few hundred dollars, is about the same as some seminars.
"We've done seminars for groups at $250 or $300 per person for two four-hour sessions," said Lyle K. Benson, an accountant and planner at Coyne & McClean in Baltimore. "It provides the basic tools."
While often valuable, seminars are also "the way many con artists work," Ms. Roper warned. Even if no fraud is involved, such courses may be used for sales pitches. If the speaker spends undue time touting particular products, beware, she said.
If you have some discipline, books by reputable authors like Andrew Tobias and Jane Bryant Quinn are very good, Ms. Roper said. Mr. Benson said that computer programs, including Quicken, serve well, too.
Want something for nothing? Good sources of material are large no-load mutual fund familiies and many state cooperative extension services.

HEADLINE: INSURANCE;
Confusion Over Policies Leads to Talk of Change

BYLINE: By Leonard Sloane

 BODY:
BUYERS of life insurance are entitled to descriptions of their policies and how they work. But the computer-generated tables supplied by insurance agents often show results that are just not realistic.
Even after scouring these documents, people frequently cannot determine what the policies' benefits would be if they lived, died or stopped paying premiums at any given time. 
 "People are buying the wrong things," said J. Robert Hunter, the president of the National Insurance Consumer Organization, who is about to become the Texas Insurance Commissioner. "That's because the disclosures are incredibly arcane and easy to manipulate."
After a long period of consumer complaints, Congressional hearings and industry reports on the insurance tables, widely known as illustrations, state regulators are preparing to take action. The National Association of Insurance Commissioners intends to adopt by March disclosure guidelines for illustrations that the states can use as a model to create their own laws.
At least until new laws are enacted, shopping for life insurance will remain difficult. "It's absolutely buyer beware," said Mary Griffin, insurance counsel for Consumers Union. "And the past couple of years have gotten worse."
Insurance companies have been using illustrations for many years to sell life insurance policies, which essentially are complex long-term financial instruments. The illustrations list death benefits and cash values over time making specific interest rate assumptions. And those assumptions account for much of the confusion.
For example, an illustration from one company might assume a 10 percent annual rate of return, which was common in the 1980's but is unlikely in the near future. An illustration from another company might assume a different rate of return.
While acknowledging some abuses in illustrations, insurers and actuaries say buyers often misunderstand what is being presented to them. "Life insurance is not like a bank account where you know what the interest rate is going to be," said Judy Faucett, a partner in Coopers & Lybrand and chairwoman of the task force on illustrations for the American Academy of Actuaries. "An illustration is not a guarantee, or even a best estimate, of how that policy is going to look in 20 years."
Walter Miller, vice president of the Prudential Insurance Company of America, said: "Illustrations are not predictions. But they are better than no illustration at all in explaining how policies work."
Critics say a major problem with illustrations is inappropriate comparisons of the performance of different policies. That is because no two companies define their interest earnings, mortality experience and expenses exactly the same way. Other problems are no standard format and inadequate policy descriptions.
Four principal solutions to these problems have been proposed: standardizing assumptions about investment return, prohibiting growth projections, creating a uniform policy design and adopting penalties for misleading the consumer.
"People are literally taking on risks when they buy a policy and they have no idea of the extent of those risks," said Joseph M. Belth, editor of the Insurance Forum newsletter. "Maybe the time has come for a prospectus-type disclosure of those risks." As used in the mutual fund industry, a prospectus would publish figures on historical performance, rather than project into the future.
Mr. Belth said: "First, there must be disclosure to the buyer at the point of sale. Second, there must be periodic, continuing disclosure to the policyholder, preferably once a year."

HEADLINE: Q & A

BYLINE: Leonard Sloane

 BODY:

Savings Bond Changes
 
Q. Will the reduction in interest from 6 percent to 4 percent on series E savings bonds affect the original interest on a bond purchased 15 or more years ago?
STANLEY LIKOFF, Bellerose, L.I.
 
A. The original interest on your bond was not affected, but interest earned afterward may be. The change in the guaranteed minimum rate for United States Savings Bonds affects bonds bought after March 1 and bonds entering new extension periods after that date.
The interest you receive on a bond depends on its issue date. Assuming you bought a bond in October 1978, the rate was fixed for the first five years, after which it went into a 10-year extension period, in which interest was paid on a variable-rate basis. The guaranteed rate for the current extension period is 4 percent, but you may get more if the market-based average rate is higher.
"If you hold the bond long enough, it will enter another extension period at the 25-year point," said Richard J. Schneebeli, deputy director of the Savings Bond division of the Treasury Department. "It will then earn the guaranteed minimum rate or the market-based variable rate prevailing at that time, whichever is higher."
 
Taxes on Noncitizens
 
Q. My husband and I are legal residents but not United States citizens. What Federal gift and estate taxes apply to us?
ROSEMARY WEBER, Manhasset, L.I.
 
A. Most gift and estate tax rules apply to both citizen and noncitizen residents. The same tax rates apply, and the first $600,000 in an estate is exempt from Federal estate tax. An important exception, however, is that transfers to a noncitizen spouse do not automatically qualify for the unlimited marital deduction on estate taxes. Such gifts are limited to $100,000 annually.
"If you are married to a noncitizen, you should consider including in your will a qualified domestic trust that will be eligible for the estate tax marital deduction," said Robert W. Benjamin, a partner in the New York law firm of Esanu Katsky Korins & Siger. "If all else fails, the surviving spouse will have a short grace period either to reform the will in court or to become a U.S. citizen."
 
I.R.A. Cutoff Point
 
Q. Can one, working during the year he or she turns 70 1/2, make a deductible contribution to an I.R.A., while at the same time becoming subject to I.R.A. withdrawal requirements?
BERNARD MOSS, Washington
 
A. Taxpayers who work, but who do not participate in their employer's retirement plan, can make tax-deductible contributions of up to $2,000 a year to an individual retirement account.
But beginning with the year in which a taxpayer reaches 70 1/2, tax-deductible contributions can no longer be made to an I.R.A. by or for the benefit of that taxpayer.
"There are also special rules allowing a married working taxpayer to make tax-deductible contributions to an I.R.A. for the benefit of his or her spouse, even if the taxpayer is over age 70 1/2," said Jerry Uslander, a principal of William M. Mercer Inc., a New York employee benefits consultancy. "This deduction ceases once the spouse reaches age 70 1/2, no matter what the age of the taxpayer."

HEADLINE: Canada's Quarrel With Nafta

 BODY:
Supporters of the North American Free Trade Agreement hoped that the Liberal Party, once it won the Canadian elections, would drop its vow to renegotiate the trade pact. But this week Jean Chretien, the Prime Minister-elect, resurrected the threat. That's unfortunate, because renegotiation could bury Nafta -- an important pact that would raise living standards in Canada, Mexico and the U.S. and lock Mexico into its pro-market reforms. The threat is also unnecessary, because Nafta doesn't bear on the trade issues that disturb Mr. Chretien the most.
The dispute is mostly about subsidies the Canadian Government gives industries like wheat and lumber. These subsidies are designed to solve domestic problems, like bailing out flagging industries and developing downtrodden regions. But the two countries' economies are so intertwined that domestic subsidies inevitably affect trade between them. Subsidies help Canadian producers lower their prices, giving exporters an "unfair advantage," according to their U.S. competitors. 
 Under existing trade rules, the U.S. -- after finding that subsidies have materially injured domestic producers -- can impose offsetting taxes, known as countervailing duties, on the offending imports. Canada has challenged U.S. actions and has often persuaded a dispute panel to throw out the countervailing duties. But Mr. Chretien wants more protection, perhaps some kind of immunity from retaliation.
It's a difficult dispute to resolve, because both countries have behaved responsibly, yet each feels wounded. Fortunately, there may be a way out. The draft accord from the Uruguay Round of international trade talks would permit countries to provide general subsidies to industries located in poor regions without fear of retaliation. The U.S. has already embraced this provision. The two countries should be able to adopt a similar provision, even without a formal Uruguay agreement and without holding up Nafta.
Canadian pressure could hand American consumers a big victory. U.S. Administrations, bowing to industry, have often brought charges of unfair trade practices where none exist. If Canada compels the U.S. to retaliate only when justified, then more foreign goods will enter the U.S., driving down prices. For consumers, that's like having Washington deposit a big check in their bank accounts.
Nafta doesn't address the unfair-trade issues that Mr. Chretien wants resolved. And the accord should not be derailed by such ancillary disputes. The solution to the subsidies dispute is hard negotiations. Nafta doesn't preclude such negotiations and should not be held hostage to their resolution.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

HEADLINE: Teachers Union Acts Shamefully to Mayor;
Not Race Alone

 BODY:
To the Editor:
I concur with the thrust of "Clinton Spoke the Truth on Race" by Lani Guinier (Op-Ed, Oct. 19), that honest discourse is essential to improving relations between our citizens.
Yet, when we limit any type of discourse on race to black and white, we omit other real elements of difference that contribute to this crisis: age, gender, social and economic status, sexual preference, physical ability, ethnicity and religious affiliation. 
 The question history is waiting for our nation to answer is whether or not citizens of varying backgrounds, life styles and beliefs can look beyond those differences to fulfill the principles of freedom, justice and equality. In answering this question, each one of us has a stake and responsibility in making this a world where a person is judged by the content of his or her character.
 
CHRISTOPHER B. WOLF
Fair Lawn, N.J., Oct. 21, 1993
