HEADLINE: FILM;
Wanted: Actress to Play Pakistani, Chilean or Indian

BYLINE: By LAUREN DAVID PEDENR

 BODY:
Sarita Choudhury is decked out in navy warm-up pants, a tatty blue T-shirt and worn Army boots -- all topped by a leonine mane of unbrushed hair. She appears worlds away from the traditional, sari-wearing Mina, the character she played in her first film, "Mississippi Masala." But after ordering a cappuccino at a restaurant in Greenwich Village and lighting up the first of several Marlboros, it becomes apparent that Ms. Choudhury has at least one thing in common with her fictional counterpart.
"Don't write how much I smoke," she says in a throaty baritone. "My parents will kill me!" 
 Despite the success of the 1992 film "Mississippi Masala," the interracial love story between a rural black man and an Indian woman that became an unexpected art-house hit, Ms. Choudhury has started her acting career over again, at the bottom. Although she has parts in three forthcoming films, either the movies are small and idiosyncratic or her part is minor.
In "Wild West," which opens on Friday, she appears as a Pakistani woman fleeing her abusive husband. The woman becomes a singer for three Pakistani brothers who seek success as country-and-western stars in London. In a forthcoming screen adaptation of "House of the Spirits," Isabel Allende's 1985 novel about a Chilean family, Ms. Choudhury has a small part as a working-class woman who is raped by an aristocratic landowner (Jeremy Irons); the film, directed by Bille August, opens early next year. In "Fresh Kill," a low-budget apocalyptic fable awaiting a distributor, she plays an Indian lesbian raising a black child with her white partner.
The 27-year-old actress, who is single and lives in the SoHo section of Manhattan, has hardly faced typecasting, nor has she gone Hollywood. She does not have a publicist. Next year, she says, she may break down and visit the West Coast.
The daughter of an Indian father and an English mother, Ms. Choudhury (pronounced CHOW-dur-y) was raised in Jamaica, Mexico and Italy. She studied economics at Queens University in Ontario, then, after some resistance on the part of her father, a biologist with the United Nations ("He thought I was having a breakdown"), she switched to movie making. Armed with a degree in film studies, she went to Italy to look for work, then headed for London, where she got a job editing a travel guide to Italy.
She began modeling part time, and as a lark, she auditioned for "Mississippi Masala." Mira Nair, the director, had been searching for someone who could evoke Mina's demure yet wild spirit (and hold her own with the co-star Denzel Washington).
"I had asked a casting director to scour England for me," Ms. Nair recalls, "and among the photographs was one bent-out-of-shape, torn, out-of-focus picture of this woman who did not want to be photographed. She scowled into the camera, and she had these luscious lips and this massive hair. I was riveted."
When Ms. Choudhury arrived to audition, Ms. Nair says, "I immediately saw in her a combination of intelligence, fearlessness and this sexuality she just couldn't help exuding."
The fearlessness evaporated when Ms. Choudhury met Mr. Washington, who had just won an Oscar for best supporting actor for his role in "Glory." "The first time we met, I was so shy," she says. "I just sat there while he and Mira talked. I was so worried about him thinking, 'Who is this girl?' " At the beginning, she says, "I was nervous, and he let me play with that. He never made me feel like, "I'm not working with this.' "
Although her performance was widely praised by critics, the actress felt she needed to begin again. "It was hard for me," she says, "because even though I'd done a film, I was just learning how to audition. And people didn't know what I'd be right for." Her three new roles followed months of reading, she says, "for anything."
After "Masala," Ms. Choudhury was eager to work with people like herself, or, as she puts it, "people my age and not famous." She read the script for "Wild West" and lied about her singing ability to get the part.
"Who knew she could sing?" asked Ms. Nair, who has seen a trailer for the film. The fact is she can't, at least not well enough to be heard in the movie. The director, David Attwood, had the songs dubbed using the voice of the singer Rebecca Price.
In "House of the Spirits," she will again appear with someone who is distinctly not her age and is indeed famous, Mr. Irons. The larger challenge, though, has been to understand the mind-set of Chileans in the 1920's. "In that era, the working class was raped by the upper class, and your mother would tell you, 'Shut up, have the baby, and don't cause trouble, because I'll lose my job,' " she says in disbelief. She was also eager that the rape scene be shot so as "to show the violence, because it's not supposed to be erotic."
"It was really hard to play a woman who is not resisting that much," she added. "I mean, I'm from the 90's, and I just wanted to kick him!"

HEADLINE: PERSPECTIVES;
Spreading the Gospel of Improvement Districts

BYLINE: By ALAN S. OSER

 BODY:
AN astute director of a business improvement district usually has some telling statistics at the ready. Gretchen Dykstra has more than a few.
Ms. Dykstra, the president of the 18-month-old Times Square Business Improvement District, had it at the tip of her tongue last week that purse snatching and pickpocketing in the district were down 43 percent in the three months from April through June this year. In that period last year the district's uniformed but unarmed security officers were just getting started. 
 "Now we know who the pickpockets are, to say nothing of the scalpers," Ms. Dykstra said. "And there's been a 70 percent decrease in three-card monte players from June to August."
The Times Square district covers 404 properties with 30 million square feet of space in the area from 40th to 53d Street, basically from Sixth Avenue to just west of Eighth Avenue. With an annual budget of $4.6 million, it is the latest in a series of improvement districts in Manhattan business centers. The districts are bringing improvements in security, sanitation, signage, lighting and assorted public services beyond the duty or capacity of the city Government to provide.
The insights of the district administrators are getting an attentive hearing in areas outside their boundaries. Daniel A. Biederman, president of the Grand Central Partnership and the 34th Street Partnership, the operators of the improvement districts in those areas, got such a hearing a few days ago when he carried the gospel downtown.
He was a speaker at a half-day seminar on the future of the financial district co-sponsored by Lehrer McGovern Bovis, the construction management company; Rose Associates Inc., the building and management concern, and BOMA/New York, the Building Owners' and Managers' Association of Greater New York.
Against the background of the 20 percent vacancy rate downtown, the obsolescence of many of its structures for office use and the decline in employment, panelists emphasized the area's fundamental long-term planning needs. Transportation improvements, across-the-board tax relief and less oppressive building and zoning regulations were high on many lists.
Quality of life issues are considered less pressing than they are in other parts of Manhattan. But the downtown area is nevertheless forming an improvement district. The Downtown-Lower Manhattan Association has received a designation from the city Government to take on the task. Robert R. Douglass , the chairman of the association and vice chairman of Chase Manhattan Bank, is leading the effort.
The fullest demonstration of the possibilities inherent in an adequately financed business improvement district is coming from the Grand Central district. The physical improvements it is ushering in will probably not be in place for a year or two. It is doing work beyond what the Government can offer, but to succeed it needs cooperation from city government, especially from the police, sanitation and transportation departments. Mr. Biederman praised their cooperation.
The district embraces 50 midtown blocks with 220 properties and about 51 million square feet of commercial space. Property owners are assessed 12.4 cents a square foot, which New York City bills, collects and rechannels to the partnership. The $6.33 million it brings in is supplemented by $2.4 million from other sources.
Going further, the partnership raised $32.3 million in bond financing last year. About a third of the assessments on the property owners are set aside to pay for the debt.
The same technique was used in August for the 34th Street district. There, 240 properties embrace 31 million square feet of office and residential space and 2.5 million square feet of retail space.
The assessment of 19 cents a square foot, discounted somewhat for the major retail complexes, was enough to raise $6 million the first year. About 30 percent of this pays debt on a $24.1 million bond issue. The bonds from both districts were rated A1 by Moody's Investors Service.
The Grand Central district has a staff of 34 street sweepers and maintenance people. Its 44 uniformed, unarmed security people operate shifts seven days a week from 7 A.M. to 11 P.M., with 8 to 15 on the job at a time.
The tasks of security personnel include helping the flow of traffic at taxi stands and assisting tourists in getting taxis at rail and bus terminals. That narrows the opportunities for cheats who take money for the service but do not provide it.
OVER the next four years the Grand Central district will spend $30 million on new street lighting, pale-red granite street corners and curbing, trash receptacles, flagpoles and trees. The testing ground for much of this is Vanderbilt Avenue from 42d to 43d Streets, and 43d Street from Vanderbilt to Fifth Avenue.
The emphasis is on white lighting and lots of it. At the corners there will be 400-watt metal halide lamps mounted on steel stanchions 29 feet high. Along the block will be double white-halide lamps on stanchions only 16 feet high. There will be a lamp every 30 to 60 feet.
Low height brings lighting closer to pedestrians. White lights have a shorter life than the yellow-orange sodium lamps the city uses but are more pedestrian-friendly. The improvement district will provide the replacement lamps when necessary.
"With the rise of the automobile, traffic engineers were in the driver's seat on lighting," said Arthur Rosenblatt, the vice president of the Grand Central Partnership in charge of capital programs. "Unfortunately they lost interest in the needs of a pedestrian on a darkened evening."
All this will enable Grand Central ultimately to say in promotional literature, "Come locate in the gorgeous Grand Central district." The 34th Street district is working in the same direction; so far the major change is in sanitation services and cleaning up grafitti.
The most ambitious social-service task in the midtown districts is directed at homelessness. Jeffrey Grunberg, vice president for social services, presides over a staff of 135, most of them formerly homeless. About half are getting stipends during a four- to six-month training period. Of the 65 full-time staff members, 45 are former homeless people earning $10,000 to $30,000 a year.
Staff members, wearing shirts and ties, and jackets that identify them as "outreach" workers, approach street people with inducements to start on the road to social adjustment and employment. They have roused 700 sleeping homeless people since March 1991 and sought to bring them, as a first step, to the partnership's multi-service center in the former St. Agnes High School for Boys, at 152 East 44th Street.
Once a month these 5 A.M. wake-ups are coordinated with a network fair run by the 34th Street Partnership, at which homeless people can choose among assistance programs offered by social services agencies.
Since 1989, 6,000 homeless people have walked through the door at St. Agnes. How many of them are still homeless the partnership says it has no way of knowing. On any given day, 300 people use the center. About a third are staff or trainees. The rest are there to see social workers or for a meal and companionship.
ROUGHLY 150 former homeless people hired by the partnership have left its employment with better-paying, full-time jobs, Mr. Grunberg said.
Improvement districts have been tailoring their activities to their specific needs. The 14th Street-Union Square district has emphasized safety and sanitation. It has a budget of $800,000 and covers 148 properties.
The Times Square district is aiding tourists and also tackling homelessness. "We've identified the 'Times Square 33,' " Ms. Dykstra said. They are permanent street dwelllers. The challenge is to persuade them to do better.
The problems of the downtown are not basically quality of life issues, though the streets are certainly dark and ominous at night and there are sanitation and park-maintenance issues. Every little bit of betterment helps.

HEADLINE: Q and A

 BODY:

Satisfying Arrears On a Condominium
 
Q. I own a condo in New York City. Because of the recession, I am behind in my mortgage and maintenance and have filed for bankruptcy. The trustee in my case has abandoned the real estate because there is no equity. 
 Recently, though, I discussed a reaffirmation of the mortgage. The condo board, however, demands that I make a full satisfaction of my arrears to them. Am I not discharged from owing the money to the association? . . . Joan Fiore, Huntington, L.I.
 
A. According to Ronald A. Sher, a lawyer in White Plains, the treatment and dischargeability of condominium common charges and assessments has differed in the courts.
He added, though, that most lawyers would advise that parties holding liens, including condominium associations, may foreclose on those liens and end your possession of the condominium even if the arrearage has been discharged by the bankruptcy court.
 
Finding Financing For Smaller Co-ops
 
Q. Five years ago, I purchased a 550-square-foot studio co-op in Forest Hills for $52,000. The mortgage is unassumable and the bank will not refinance properties less than 600 square feet. I was also told that most banks now will not finance apartments that are less than 600 square feet in Queens.
I am considering paying down this mortgage. Should I decide to sell the property within the next year or two, how will a purchaser obtain financing? Is there anything I can do besides hold the mortgage myself? . . . M. McNulty, Forest Hills, Queens
 
A. Yes. Try using a mortgage broker to find a bank that does refinance mortgages for co-ops under 600 square feet, said Mitchell A. Steinberg, assistant vice president at the Apple Bank for Savings in Manhattan. Also, he added, your bank may reconsider its decision not to refinance if you pay down your mortgage.
Mr. Steinberg suggested that buyers offer to finance the purchase themselves, or they can inquire whether the sale qualifies for a loan from the State of New York Mortgage Agency, known as Sonyma. The agency finances co-ops of 500 square feet and larger, depending on location and other factors.
 
Can a Landlord Shut Off Water at Night?
 
Q. Does the landlord in a rent-stabilized building have the right to turn off the water between midnight and 6 A.M.? Does the landlord have the right to keep the water pressure so low that when washing the dishes or taking a shower the water runs out with no force? Does the landlord have the right to turn down the water pressure between 9 A.M. and 5 P.M. on the assumption that no one is in residdence and doesn't need any water? . . . Jacqueline Edwards, Manhattan
 
A. No. According to Michael Finder, a lawyer in Manhattan, you should call the Department of Housing Preservation and Development at (212) 487-4545 and ask for code enforcement.

HEADLINE: Whitman Pins Hopes On Women

BYLINE: By JOSEPH F. SULLIVAN

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
CHRISTINE TODD WHITMAN, outfoxed at nearly every turn during the summer by the wily incumbent, hopes to unseat Gov. Jim Florio on Tuesday with a late appeal to female voters.
In the final week of campaigning, Mrs. Whitman's commercials prominently featured women, and the candidate began mentioning more often in debates and speeches that she is the mother of two teen-agers. 
 Early on, Mrs. Whitman's candidacy was expected to mobilize women interested in electing the state's first female chief executive. That posed a problem for Florio campaign strategists, who were worried about how forcefully they could run against a woman without appearing to beat up on her. But Mrs. Whitman relieved their anxiety by handing them a series of issues that they could easily exploit.
Among the first was her admission that she had paid more than $22,000 in back taxes for two undocumented immigrants who had worked for her. She was also fined $2,000 by the Immigration and Naturalization Service for hiring the pair. Every chance he got, Mr. Florio, a Democrat, said Mrs. Whitman, a Republican, had been "found guilty of violating the law" and "fined by the I.R.S." (instead of the I.N.S.) for the incident. His comments fed the belief by many women that Mrs. Whitman was a rich woman who could afford to pay for such things, while they could not.
By mid-October, the Florio camp had managed to usurp much of Mrs. Whitman's credibility on many issues important to voters, including violent crime, welfare reform and taxes.
"She failed early on to establish an identity for herself," said Neil Upmeyer, president of the Center for the Analysis of Public Issues, a Princeton-based policy study group. "And that brought about a second problem. When it came time to make her sales pitch for her economic recovery plan, nobody knew who she was and her message lacked credibility."
While Mrs. Whitman was conserving her campaign finances, the Florio campaign was filling in the blanks in the public's perception of the Republican challenger. Mr. Florio was able to picture Mrs. Whitman as a willing tool of the National Rifle Association when she criticized his ban on assault weapons, one of the most popular initiatives of Mr. Florio's term in office.
"The strongest image of the campaign was a Florio ad that had a picture of her in goggles and ear protectors that are used by people who are out shooting rifles," Mr. Upmeyer said. "Without having to say a word, it added credibility to the argument that she is in the pocket of the N.R.A."
Not surprisingly, independent public opinion surveys showed Mrs. Whitman trailing Mr. Florio among female voters, and her campaign aides said she must recapture their support if she is to have a chance of victory.
A recent Star-Ledger/Eagleton poll at Rutgers University found that Mrs. Whitman was favored by men of voting age by 49 to 43 percent. Mr. Florio was preferred by 61 to 31 percent among all women who could vote. His lead among Republican women was 28 percentage points over Mrs. Whitman and among independent female voters, 11 percentage points.
The poll used the responses of 601 likely voters in reporting Mr. Florio's margin among female voters. The margin of error is plus or minus 4.5 percent.
A New York Times/WCBS-TV News Poll a few days later reported that Mr. Florio was preferred by both male (51 to 35 percent) and female (47 to 34 percent) voters. The poll surveyed 1,153 people, of whom 925 said they were registered to vote. The margin of error is plus or minus 3 percent.
Of course, last-minute polls, not to mention the election itself, could still show a large enough shift by voters to give Mrs. Whitman all that she needs.
 
Legislative Seats at Stake
 New Jersey voting booths will be open from 7 A.M. to 8 P.M. Tuesday. Also up for election are all 120 seats in the Legislature. Republicans hold veto-proof majorities of 27 to 13 in the Senate and 58 to 22 in the Assembly, which they won in the 1991 election on a voter backlash against Governor Florio's $2.8 billion tax program.
Hazel Gluck, a co-chairwoman of the Whitman campaign, said no woman has won public office without winning the women's vote. The question in these closing days, is whether the renewed appeal to women and Mrs. Whitman's attempt to define herself in terms of some so-called women's issues come too late.
Susan Carroll, a senior research associate at the Center for the American Woman and Politics at Rutgers, said the Florio campaign's success in depicting Mrs. Whitman as someone whose wealth put her out of touch with the concerns of ordinary citizens kept her on the defensive almost until the end.
Ms. Carroll also said public opinion polls across the country have found that women are more willing to support female candidates for legislative seats than for the job of chief executive of a state. "The key problem seems to be older women voters," she said. "Perhaps that was because they were socialized in a very different era when there were limits on what a woman could and would do."
She said, however, that Mrs. Whitman "had a real chance to attract crossover votes from independent and Democratic women, particularly well-educated professional women." She said this group might have more strongly supported Mrs. Whitman because she is a moderate Republican, but said her proposal to cut state income taxes by 30 percent over three years damaged her appeal.
"When her tax plan seemed to be a holdover from the era of Reaganomics, it became a very difficult thing for progressive women, including some of those leaning toward the Democratic Party, to swallow," Ms. Carroll said.
 
Gubernatorial Races Reviewed
 Kathleen A. Frankovic, director of surveys for CBS News, said a review of five gubernatorial races in 1990 in which women were running showed that when the female candidate was a Democrat, the tendency of women to vote Democratic was enhanced, but when the female candidate was a Republican, there was no difference in the percentage of votes cast for either candidate by men or women.
For his part, Mr. Florio followed the traditional Democrats' end game of trying to energize elderly and minority voters. The appeal to black voters, which followed an endorsement by a statewide coalition of black members of the clergy, was channeled chiefly through urban churches.
Some black elected officials, though, including Mayor Sharpe James of Newark, have criticized both the Democratic and Republican campaigns, saying they have ignored the needs of the cities. "The words 'urban, disadvantaged or Afro-American' are missing from this year's campaign literature," Mr. James said.
He said he understood that in this state the campaign is largely waged for the suburban voter, but he said the decision not to campaign in urban neighborhoods and ask residents directly for their support could hold down voter turnout.
If the vote is light in the urban counties, and if Bret Schundler, Jersey City's Republican Mayor, is able to deliver his city's vote to Mrs. Whitman as he has promised, then the outcome could be much closer than expected.
 
Rehashing Tax Issues
 The candidates for the Legislature have been rehashing the 1990 tax increase and the Republican majority's 1992 rollback of the penny increase in the sale tax that was part of Mr. Florio's package.
Republicans argue that they redeemed their pledge to roll back the taxes through the elimination of the penny increase in the sales tax, which cut state revenue by about $600 million. The Democrats charge that the Republicans really accepted $2.2 billion of the increase by not following through and repealing the balance of Mr. Florio's program.
In the campaign's last full week, Republican leaders, including Assembly Speaker Garabed (Chuck) Haytaian of Independence Township, have tried to revive the public's fears about taxes by contending that the Democrats will again try to raise the sales tax to 7 cents from 6 cents if they regain strength in the Legislature.
The Senate minority leader, Senator John A. Lynch, Democrat of New Brunswick, denied any such plan to raise the sales tax and attributed Mr. Haytaian's comments to "runaway hysteria."
Mr. Lynch said Democrats should pick up several of the seats in nominally Democratic districts that were lost in 1991. Much of his optimism is based on surveys that indicate greater public acceptance of Mr. Florio's tax package, which the Governor said was needed to finance public schools and close a budget deficit. If Mr. Florio should win big, something that is far from certain, Mr. Lynch said Democrats could capture the eight seats needed to regain control of the Senate.
While Democrats are given an outside chance of winning the Senate even if there is a Florio landslide, it seems unlikely that the Assembly will change hands because of the top-heavy majority enjoyed by the Republicans.
 
Recall Amendment on Ballot
 In addition to deciding the races for Governor and the Legislature, voters have the chance to give themselves the ability to recall elected officials.
The recall amendment to the State Constitution is the only question on the statewide ballot. It would permit voters to recall officials from the municipal to the state level after they have served one year in office.
The referendum was a last-minute substitute for a proposed initiative and referendum amendment that would give the public a direct vote on setting state policy. After pledging to adopt such a system in the 1991 campaign, Republican leaders had second thoughts and placed the recall amendment on the ballot instead.
In the closing days of the campaign, the League of Women Voters and New Jersey Common Cause, the public interest organization, joined in opposing the referendum, saying it was probably unconstitutional, would increase the cost of campaigns since there is no limit on spending for public questions and would diminish the ability of officeholders to make difficult decisions.
"We already have the power to recall elected officials, on Election Day," said Lucy Heller, government director of the League of Women Voters. "It is ludicrous to suggest that voters should judge anyone's performance after one year in office."
Senator John H. Dorsey, Republican of Boonton, and a sponsor of the recall amendment, said 168 municipalities and 6 counties already had the right to recall their elected officials. "It's absurd to deny this right to the rest of the voters," he said. Residents of 15 other states also have the right to recall elected officials, he said.

HEADLINE: Bottle Collecting: Pieces of the Past Worth Digging Deep For

BYLINE: By HELEN PIKE

DATELINE: SOUTH AMBOY

 BODY:
SOMEWHERE under dense overgrowth in this industrial Raritan Bay town, Glenn Vogel and Glenn Harbour are digging through the ash, clay, sand and rusting metal of history.
For more than six hours, the slow, rhythmic work is punctuated by odd bits of nursery rhymes and nonsense songs that float up from a 15-foot hole in the abandoned dump. Mr. Vogel and Mr. Harbour estimate they are digging down as far as 1895. Scattered around the opening are the remains of leather boots, wooden buckets, porcelain bowls and eight-inch-long oyster shells -- all of which they have dug up and none of which interest them. 
 Only when the clink of metal against glass occurs does the site erupt with whoops of glee as they gently unearth what they are searching for: turn-of-the-century hand blown bottles.
Although Mr. Vogel's and Mr. Harbour's passion for glass may seem obsessive, they are not alone in collecting the old-fashioned containers that used to hold any liquid from bromides to beer.
 
Experiencing a Resurgence
 Every 10 years, often concurrent with a recession, bottle collecting experiences a resurgence, said Isabel Sherwood of Toms River, an officer of the national Federation of Historic Bottle Collectors and a one-time digger.
The current interest has been building for about three years, she said. The Jersey Shore Bottle Club, to which she, Mr. Vogel and Mr. Harbour belong, counts its membership in terms of families. Recently, it added 20 new members to its longstanding core of 70.
Mrs. Sherwood further estimates that the region has about 475 individual collectors who regularly get together to trade, buy, exhibit and share historical information.
 
'Often a Family Affair'
 There are three clubs in the state affiliated with the federation: the North Jersey Bottle Collectors Association in Oakland, the Heritage Club in Vineland and the Jersey Shore Club, which meets monthly in Belmar. There is also a nonfederation bottle collectors' club in New Egypt.
"Collecting, even digging for bottles, is often a family affair," said Mrs. Sherwood, who occasionally helps her husband, Stanley, with his collections of milk bottles and glass balls that were once used for target shooting.
One reason for the passion of collecting bottles is that they are relatively inexpensive. Some sell for as little as a few cents at a yard sale while others are found. These discoveries often lead the would-be collector to library talks given by established collectors, bottle shows and club meetings.
The most frequently asked questions are: What used to be in the bottles? Where were they made? Who made them? Why are they colored? How can I find more like this one?
That is usually how a collection gets started, said Mrs. Sherwood, who began with fruit jars and now also collects pickle jars. Style, color, maker, location and former content, like perfume, medicine, poison, bitters, soda and even olive oil, become the framework on which to build a collection.
Many collectors print handouts of the research that they have tirelessly gleaned from libraries, county historical archives and even courthouse records. Some collectors branch out, looking for vintage postcards and sepia-toned photographs in an effort to identify business locations and the faces of the entrepreneurs whose bottles they now own. Still others seek out advertising labels to match their bottles.
"It brings me back in time," said Bob Randolph, a Manasquan auctioneer who was one of 150 exhibitors at the recent fall show of the Jersey Shore Club. Back in time also means when he was a youngster growing up in Neptune and ignorantly smashing blob-topped and hutch-topped bottles he uncovered in a Shark River creek bed.
Today, those vintage bottles whose tops indicate how they were sealed fetch $45 and more, Mr. Randolph said with a shake of his head. He said bottles were once again setting records at auctions and private sales, as they did in the 1970's.
"Color is king," he said. "It's dictating the price." A bottle in amber or light yellow or greenish blue can, even if its shape is plain, command 10 times the price of a more ordinary bottle, he said.
After color comes age, Mr. Randolph added. Bottles made before 1870 are much sought after because few have survived and most were crudely made before manufacturing techniques evolved.
The last criterion is category, and, Mr. Randolph said, flasks head collectors' wish lists. They are followed by soda bottles and then colored pontils, named for the indentation in the base that is made when the blow pipe is removed from the finished glass container.
But even though prices at auction can climb as high as $42,000 for a single bottle, Mr. Randolph was quick to point out that fortunes are not made overnight.
"People spend years developing an eye for detail and color gradations," he said. "They start with dollar bottles and work their way up. If a category gets out of range for them, they try to develop another one that hasn't been discovered. It's really a form of treasure hunting. But nobody should quit their day job."
All of which is of little importance to Chris Tobin, 12, of Forked River.
"I like the odd stuff," Chris said, "different shapes, different colors."
Chris was one of the 500 individuals -- 100 more than last year -- who attended a bottle show at Toms River High School North. His purchase for the day was a 50-cent gum dish from Millville Art Glass, a cranberry-colored disk made by Ed Johnson, a retired glass-mold maker.
Chris's biggest investment to date is a $5 cranberry bottle he found during an antique furniture foray with his mother, Connie, who regularly attends shows and yard sales. Chris said he has yet to dig for bottles but may do so eventually.
Rita and Howard Morgan of Howell have stopped digging because they now have a 2 1/2-year-old son, Christopher. But they came to the bottle show looking for glass flasks, ice-cream soda bottles and mason jars. They have even begun a glass-train bottle collection for their son.
When he gets older, the couple may dig again, taking the child with them, Mrs. Morgan said. But if they do, it will be with caution. One outing netted them each $60 fines for trespassing, she said.
"I think it was a high amount to discourage us," Mrs. Morgan said. "But the farmer didn't even give us time to explain ourselves before he called the police."
 
Taking Inventory
 Meanwhile, back at the dump, Mr. Vogel and Mr. Harbour were taking inventory. Whenever they find a large, promising site, they observe the buddy system to avoid cave-in related accidents. They also rarely dig in the summer when it is the height of tick and chigger season. And they usually confine their digs to open land or abandoned sites, like former dumps.
"I'm looking for color to fill out my collection," said Mr. Harbour, a painting preservationist, who found a honey-amber shoe-polish bottle at the dig.
On the open market, the bottle would go for about $10, he said, but for this amateur archeologist, the value lies in having found a bottle that will now be part of his private collection.
Besides color, Mr. Harbour is also putting together a collection based on the Bayshore region from South Amboy to Highlands. To date, he has gathered a small -- 250-item -- but impressive variety of medicine, cider, milk, beer and soda bottles, along with broken stoneware crocks he has meticulously glued back together. He exhibits his findings at libraries and museums in the area.
 
A Journey Into the Past
 "Americans used to live out of glass," said Mr. Harbour, a Middletown resident who actively promotes glass-related events at the Spy House, a Revolutionary-era museum on the Raritan Bay in the township's Port Monmouth section. "Their medicine, their food, even their advertising used to be on glass."
Mr. Vogel, an Eatontown-area historian who works at Fort Monmouth, said, "Every bottle represents a little journey into the past."
At the dig he found little of interest other than a pale lavender machine-made Eiffel Tower Fruit Juice bottle. "It's the first time I've ever seen one of these," he said.
The bottle will probably go into his sale pile. Mr. Vogel, who owns more than 600 bottles, concentrates on handblown beers and sodas from the 1860's, even though he owns some machine-made bottles that first appeared in 1903.
"It's all about owning a little piece of history," he said.
His favorite part of his collection is the bottles of William A. French of Red Bank, a bottler who, Mr. Vogel discovered, was one of the five wealthiest men in New Jersey in 1900. His French collection features 47 different bottles so far.
"We're local historians who use bottles to tell the stories of an area," Mr. Vogel said. "If we don't do it now, soon it will be lost forever."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Glenn Vogel, below looking up, and Glenn Harbour in pit they dug while searching for turn-of-the-century handblown bottles, left. Isabel Sherwood, an officer of the national Federation of Historic Bottle Collectors and a one-time digger, with part of her collection. (Photographs by Sam D'Amico for The New York Times)

SECTION: Section 13WC; Page 1; Column 3; Westchester Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1477 words

HEADLINE: Last-Minute Look At the Candidates

BYLINE: By JAMES FERON

 BODY:
WESTCHESTER voters will participate in a major changing of the guard on Tuesday, Election Day, filling the soon-to-be-vacant posts of district attorney and county clerk. Voters will also decide whether to replace the incumbent County Executive.
After one of the most bitterly contested political campaigns in recent memory, voters will also select a new Board of Legislators as well as host of mayors, supervisors, council members, judges and other municipal officials. 
Polls will be open from 6 A.M. to 9 P.M. For information about voting, the number to call is 285-5700.
Rarely has there been so much uncertainty in so many top-level posts. The office of district attorney, occupied for 25 years by Carl A. Vergari, a Republican who is retiring, has attracted two qualified candidates who have conducted a harsh campaign, accusing each other, among other things, of accepting "mob-tainted" campaign funds.
The candidates are Jeanine Ferris Pirro, a 42-year-old Republican and former assistant district attorney who gave up a County Court judgeship to accept the nomination, and Michael G. Cherkasky, a Democrat, who rose in the office of Robert M. Morgenthau, the Manhattan District Attorney, to become one of his chief prosecutors.
Mrs. Pirro, who created and ran the Domestic Violence and Child Abuse Bureau under Mr. Vergari, has sought to persuade voters that Mr. Cherkasky, 43, is an outsider and therefore unfamiliar with law enforcement in the county. Mr. Cherkasky was born in White Plains and has lived in Larchmont for seven years. Mrs. Pirro is a lifelong resident of Westchester.
Mr. Cherkasky, who says the law is the same in Manhattan and Westchester, was chief of the investigation division in Mr. Morgenthau's office when he left, supervising 75 lawyers and 150 investigators and police officers. He had a budget of $14 million, which was $2 million larger than that of the entire Westchester District Attorney's office.
The Right to Life Party candidate is John J. Barry.
The office of county clerk, which is Westchester's records keeper, is becoming vacant because its four-term occupant, Andrew J. Spano, the first Democrat to hold the office in this century, decided after a decade of service to seek higher office. He was aiming for the county's top post, that of county executive, held for 11 years by Andrew P. O'Rourke, a Republican.
It was not Mr. Spano's first bid for this powerful office. He made a similar effort four years ago but was persuaded to back off in favor of Assemblyman Richard L. Brodsky, who was thought by county Democratic leaders to have a better chance to unseat the popular Mr. O'Rourke.
Mr. Brodksy ran and lost, by 6 percentage points, and Mr. Spano returned to his job, modernizing and computerizing the county clerk office. Then, last year, the two rivals began gearing up for another challenge.
 
No Debate in Clerk Race
 Mr. Spano thought he had an agreement with Mr. Brodsky that if the Assemblyman lost in the 1989 race, then it would be Mr. Spano's turn to be the candidate. Mr. Brodsky said no such pact existed. Defying Mr. Spano and the Democratic leadership, who endorsed the Spano candidacy, Mr. Brodsky forced a primary race and narrowly won.
Meanwhile, Mr. Spano's soon-to-be-vacant office has attracted two major candidates, the Eastchester town supervisor, James P. Doody, a Democrat, and County Legislator Leonard N. Spano, a Republican who is not related to Andrew Spano, the outgoing incumbent.
The race for county clerk has consisted of each candidate's putting forward proposals for how he would enhance Andrew Spano's improvements. The candidates have not engaged in any debates, however, largely because Leonard Spano has declined to do so. He says he has made more than 200 personal appearances, none with Mr. Doody.
The Right to Life Party candidate is Robert J. Walsh.
 
O'Rourke vs. Brodsky
 The major race is for county executive, with Mr. O'Rourke's saying that he continues to provide AAA-style government, a reference to the top bond rating that Westchester executives have long taken pride in maintaining.
He says Mr. Brodsky is unsuitable for the job and does not have the right temperament. Mr. Brodsky says Mr. O'Rourke has become weary in his 11 years in office and, in fact, had not expected to be running this year.
Mr. O'Rourke was nominated by President Bush for a Federal judgeship, but the Senate, which must confirm nominations, did not consider it before the 1992 Presidential election effectively doomed Republican nominees.
Mr. Brodsky has also campaigned strenuously on the ethics-in-government issue, picking up where he left off in the 1989 campaign when he sought, largely unsuccessfully, to associate Mr. O'Rourke with donations that concessionaires at Playland, the county-run amusement park in Rye, had to pay to the Republican Party to win contracts.
During this campaign, Mr. Brodsky has benefited from newspaper articles s about favors Mr. O'Rourke evidently sought to provide in the form of jobs and contracts to companies employing members of his family and close friends.
 
Unemployment a Key Issue
 Mr. Vergari, the Republican District Attorney, and a grand jury looked into the alleged misdeeds and failed to bring charges or to indict, saying no laws were broken. Mr. O'Rourke has cited these findings as evidence that he did nothing wrong. Mr. Brodsky says the standard of ethics in government should be higher than "I was not indicted."
Both candidates have put forward plans to help relieve unemployment problems, which Mr. O'Rourke minimizes by saying the unemployment rate in the county is 5.7 percent and Mr. Brodsky maximizes by noting that the county has lost jobs in 47 of the last 50 months -- 40,000 jobs in all, two-thirds of them white-collar.
Both have put forward plans for job training: Mr. Brodsky is proposing white-collar retraining and a small-business start-up center, and Mr. O'Rourke has recently opened a so-called one-stop shopping center, which offers outplacement assistance and a public-private partnership to provide a marketing plan.
They have disagreed over taxes, with Mr. O'Rourke seeking to associate Mr. Brodsky with "tax-and-spend liberals in Albany." He said his Democratic rival voted yes on 96 of 98 spending and tax bills and walked out of the chamber for the key vote on the budget to avoid being associated with its passage.
Mr. Brodsky said Mr. O'Rourke manipulated county taxes, producing three consecutive years of no increase, including next year's, to coincide with the election campaign. The premature release of the 1994 budget, before Election Day, an action the O'Rourke administration had previously resisted, was labeled by Mr. Brodsky as a political move.
The Right to Life candidate is Noreen E. Donohue.
 
17 Legislator Seats at Stake
 The entire Board of Legislators faces election this year, with the Republicans hoping to maintain their control and the Democrats seeking to regain the majority they say they won at the polls last time but lost in the legislative chamber.
When the votes were counted in the 1991 election, there were eight Democrats, eight Republicans and Suzanne R. Swanson, a Thornwood Conservative who won election running as a Democrat. After much pressure from both parties, she sided with the Republicans in selecting a chairman and organizing committees.
Since then, two Democratic legislators, Sandra R. Galef of Ossining and Audrey G. Hochberg of Scarsdale, have been elected to the State Assembly and have been replaced by Republicans by the Republican-controlled board.
Two races are being watched carefully. Vying for the Ossining seat are two former Ossining Supervisors, Richard G. Wishnie, a Democrat, and Barbara C. Fratianni, a Republican and Conservative. They are seeking to replace Mark S. Tulis, a Republican who had been appointed to replace Mrs. Galef but decided not to seek election. The Right to Life candidate is Paul Rossney.
 
Three-Way Mayoral Race
 The legislator selected to replace Mrs. Hochberg, Pearl C. Quarles, a Republican and former school board president in New Rochelle, is being challenged by Lois T. Bronz, a Democrat who served eight terms as a Greenburgh Council member before moving to White Plains. Charmaine R. Thomas of White Plains is the Conservative and Right to Life candidate.
One of the most interesting races is for mayor of White Plains, where the 18-year incumbent, Alfred Del Vecchio, is seeking re-election on the Conservative line, having lost the Republican primary, to his surprise, to Michael A. Coffey, an 18-year-member of the Common Council.
Likely to benefit from this split is the Democratic candidate, Sy J. Schulman, a one-term Councilman and former president of the Westchester County Association, which represents major employers.

PUB-DATE: October 31, 1993

SECTION: Section 9; Page 13; Column 3; Society Desk

LENGTH: 181 words

HEADLINE: WEDDINGS;
Anne Handwerger, Curtis Large

 BODY:
Anne Chapman Handwerger, a daughter of Gretchen M. Handwerger of Paris and Joseph Handwerger of Washington, was married in Washington yesterday to Curtis Eugene Large, a son of the Rev. and Mrs. Wayne T. Large of Ann Arbor, Mich. The bridegroom's father, a United Methodist minister, performed the ceremony at the Washington National Cathedral.
Mrs. Large, 25, and her husband, 23, Yale University graduates, work at Bain & Company, a management consulting firm in Boston, where she is a senior associate consultant and he is an associate consultant. 
 The bride's mother, a former deputy director of the Peace Corps, is with the World Bank as the special representative to the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development in Paris. The bride's father is the principal of Joseph Handwerger Architects, in Washington.
The bridegroom's mother, Joy Large, teaches history, music and German at the Central Middle School in Plymouth, Mich. His father is the director of the Wesley Foundation, the Methodist Church ministry at the University of Michigan at Ann Arbor.

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Betting On The Baby Boom Bull Market

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
"THE baby boomers are doing to the stock market what they did to the housing market."
That quote, from Claudia Mott, the small-capitalization stock guru at Prudential Securities, sums up the best long-term bullish case now available for the stock market. And it may prove to be right. 
 The baby boomers -- the children born in the years immediately following World War II -- have provided excessive demand all their lives. In the 1950's and 1960's, they forced construction of new schools, then colleges.
Then came the surge in demand for housing, pushing home prices to unprecedented levels. Value-sensitive investors, examining such things as ratios of housing prices to income, concluded prices were ridiculously high, and some decided to sell their homes and rent. But prices kept rising anyway as investors bet the trend would continue indefinitely. The value players appeared to have been dead wrong.
Eventually the housing market reversed, with the biggest declines in areas where the price inflation had been the greatest. Prices were too high, and there were fewer families being formed. Those who leaped in too late ended with big losses.
Now, the aging baby boomers are discovering that they must save for retirement, and at a time when interest rates are down. "We are pulling in a lot of inexperienced investors," says Christian Thwaites, a vice president of Aetna mutual funds, speaking of the firm's retirement account business, which is concentrated in such areas as hospital and local government employees. A year ago, he reports, about 30 percent of such retirement money went into stock funds. Now it is 50 percent. And more money is going into speculative stock funds.
At the same time, value investors are looking askance at the stock market, as they once did at the housing market. In housing they were eventually proved right, but only after the demographic trends were played out. If Ms. Mott is right, stock prices could levitate for years, until the baby boomers start trying to cash out. But the economy could stay weak, as demand is held down by a shift from consumption to saving. Rising prices would give American corporations access to cheaper capital, providing a competitive advantage for them.
It is easier to print new shares of stock than it is to build houses, so the rising supply of stock could create a digestion problem for the market at any time. And no doubt there will be scandals as some brokerage firms and money managers show more interest in their own wealth than in that of their customers.
If the baby-boom bull market theory proves false, its downfall will probably be traceable to the assumption that Americans will increase their savings for a prolonged period. But those -- including this columnist -- who tend to focus on value may be surprised by just how long it takes for the market to return to the old value benchmarks. If so, it will be fun while it lasts, and that could be for years.

HEADLINE: Business Diary: October 24 - 29

BYLINE: BY Hubert B. Herring

 BODY:

THE ECONOMY
 
A Plan Is Born. Now, Watch Out!
O.K., it's 1,342 pages, and it's so complicated that to try to understand it all could put you in need of some heavy-duty health care. But look at it this way, President Clinton said, as he plunked his mammoth health care plan in Congress' lap last week: "None of us could devise a system more complex, more burdensome, more administratively costly than the one we have now." He's got a point there. But then again, the present system wasn't built in a day, and he's trying to create a brand new labyrinth in a single stroke. The plan has evolved during its months of gestation, notably in new limits on Government subsidies -- an attempt to allay fears that Big Government will take on an unworkable burden. But for all the talk of his waffling, all the plan's revisions, he's stuck to one razor-sharp goal: "health care security for all Americans." 

Is It the Clinton Economy Yet?
Things economic take a while to sort themselves out, but at some point we'll have to start talking about the Clinton economy. Maybe it's time. President Clinton clearly thinks so, saying he sees sparks of a true rebound. He'd gladly take credit for last week's figures, data that showed quarterly growth at a strong 2.8 percent annual rate and inflation at just a 1.8 percent rate. Of course, if different numbers had appeared on the dice, someone might have blamed Bush-era policies.
 
The revival has skipped some places, like California. But, sadly, there will be a lot of rebuilding there soon, so if there's a silver lining to last week's horrifying fires, it's that construction could give the state a lift.
 
Not a Cruel Month at All
Well, the stock market survived October, and survived it in style. In the past, it's been a cruel month -- in 1987, say, and in 1989. So with prices already high, investors tiptoed into October, ready to head for the exit at the drop of a statistic. But the statistics were good, and the Dow industrials closed out the month with two records in a week. The Dow nudged another barrier, 3,700, before ending at 3,680.
 
COMPANIES
 
Paramount Isn't Sitting Still
Paramount isn't exactly sitting around eating popcorn while those folks with billions to burn decide who's going to buy the company. Quite the contrary. Last week it said it was teaming up with Chris-Craft to start a fifth television network. They plan to do it much the way Fox did in 1986: combine stations, buy some more, and get into more homes via cable. But here's where the whole interlocking mess gets tricky: If Paramount winds up with its chosen suitor, Viacom, then Tele-Communications, a prime backer of rival bidder QVC, will not be happy. So T.C.I., as the nation's biggest cable operator, could make life difficult for the infant network. Call in Captain Kirk and Dr. Spock! Which, in fact, is what Paramount plans to do: The cornerstone of the new network is another "Star Trek" series.
 
Speaking of that takeover battle, the billions keep flying, and now the lawsuits do too. After Viacom matched its $10 billion bid, QVC asked a court to block Paramount from merging with Viacom. Then, to add more spice, there was talk that BellSouth might jump into the bidding. Warp 3, here we come!
 
G.M. Coddles the Auto Workers
General Motors is selling more cars these days, but it had better hope sales get even better. It said last week that car sales rose a powerful 20 percent in the last 10 selling days. But this news was dwarfed by G.M.'s settlement with the U.A.W. The company badly needed union concessions -- especially on pensions, where it has dug itself into a hole, and on health care, where, amazingly, it still pays it all. But G.M., eager to be buddy-buddy with labor, walked away with no concessions.
 
Ford Sings, G.M. Doesn't
Last week was also earnings week, and the numbers served to underscore Detroit's new pecking order. Ford said it earned $463 million in the quarter, while G.M. posted a $113 million loss. The expectation gap was even wider, for there was immediate carping that Ford could earn even more if it weren't offering so many rebates. In contrast, at G.M. the expectations were so bleak Wall Street was actually impressed by such a small (small?) loss, and the company's stock rose a bit, ending the week at $47.625.
 
A Tough Choice for VW Workers
While the auto news is mixed in Detroit, it's just plain bad in Germany. Volkswagen is hurting -- sales are weak, German wages are high. Last week it tried a radical approach, giving workers a choice between a four-day week (with lower pay) and 30,000 lost jobs.
 
Motorola's Loss, Kodak's Gain
Old ways die hard, old corporate ways die even harder, but last week Kodak joined the venerable companies -- from I.B.M. on down -- that are shedding their inbred ways by looking outside for new bosses. Kodak's choice was a surprise: George M.C. Fisher, who has led Motorola to solid growth in recent years, and who has been called by one analyst "a hard-hitting, tough, cost-conscious executive." He replaces Kay R. Whitmore, dismissed because directors felt he wouldn't make the hard choices, and hard changes, needed. Mr. Fisher's electronics expertise is seen as a plus in this age of higher and higher tech, and Kodak stock leapt on the news of his selection, ending the week at $62.875. And what drew him away from Motorola, where there was no hint that he'd jump ship? No one would talk salary, but Mr. Fisher did say he got an "extraordinary offer."
 
Merck Tries to Buy Some Loyalty
Many employees are not feeling loyal these days. Why should they, with colleagues being laid off right and left? Drug makers are feeling special pressure as political scrutiny of drug prices mounts. So last week Merck tossed its workers a bone -- the ones who are left, anyway (Merck has done its fair share of job pruning). In a gesture Dr. P. Roy Vagelos, the chairman, said was meant to show "we are confident about our future," Merck is giving all 37,000 employees an option to buy 300 of its shares for $32.50 each. That would have been a good deal a year ago, when the stock was around $45, but it's now about $32.
 
A Lot of Takers at Prudential
A lot of people out there must feel that Prudential Securities owes them something. After Prudential settled charges of defrauding customers by setting aside $330 million to pay them back, callers last week flooded a toll-free line set up by regulators. It's a 24-hour line, so if you awake at 2 a.m. angry about the limited partnerships you bought, that's a good time to call. (The number: 1-800-220-9125.)
 
The scandal is reaching into Prudential's past.Word came last week that George L. Ball, who headed Prudential until 1991, was leaving his job at Smith Barney. Mr. Ball reportedly is being investigated for his role at Prudential, and Smith Barney apparently didn't want its own reputation tarnished.
 
Tokyo's Explosive New Stock
The Tokyo Stock Exchange has been battered for several years as the Japanese miracle has turned less miraculous, so Japan wants to restore investor confidence. It didn't do it last week. Newly listed shares of the Government railroad, the East Japan Railway, were meant to prove that the market was solid. They did just the opposite. First, so many investors wanted to buy the shares that it sucked money from other stocks and clogged the computers. When a new day dawned, everyone tried to grab quick profits. The stock plunged, with more disorder, more clogged computers. Trading was halted, and the market looks more than ever like a crapshoot.
 
R.S.I.? There's No Such Thing
Great news! Those of you who work on keyboards and have numbness or pain in hands or wrists can throw out those wrist braces and ice packs, and shelve the Advil. In a journalist's lawsuit, a British judge said last week that repetitive stress injuries do not exist.
 
INTERNATIONAL
 
Could Canada Sidetrack Nafta?
It'll be quite a feat if the North American Free Trade Agreement ever becomes reality, because it's at the mercy of three countries' political winds. It's as if you were sweeping up feathers, and just when you got most of them cornered, another would insolently flutter away. And so it must have seemed to Nafta's supporters in Washington last week when Canada's newly elected Prime Minister, Jean Chretien, said he wanted changes in the pact. So now everyone's watching the clock; if Mr. Chretien stirs things up before Congress votes in mid-November, the resulting uncertainty could be a real blow. If he holds off, the pact has a better chance.
 
Farm Ownership, Yeltsin's Way
With the legislature no longer blocking the way, Boris Yeltsin is moving to put his reformist stamp on Russia, and last week he put a big dent in a legacy from the Stalin era -- the vast network of collective farms. In signing a decree to let farm workers own and sell land, President Yeltsin challenged head-on a basic commandment of the Communist system: Citizens shall not own property.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Russians harvest wheat, circa 1947. (The New York Times); Jean Chretien of Canada. A G.M. plant in Fremont, Calif. (Associated Press)

LENGTH: 1890 words

HEADLINE: Corporate America's Diagnosis of the Clinton Health Plan

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM

 BODY:
A BIT like prophets whose message has finally been heeded after long years of being ignored, many employee benefits executives said "amen" when President Clinton trumpeted the need to bring health costs under control and make care universally available.
But many business people fear that the changes the President sent to Congress last week may increase their costs, which are still rising at more than double the general inflation rate. 
 It is too early to predict what will come out of Congress, of course, but many employers have jumped into the battle of competing interest groups lobbying to influence the outcome before they are forced to reshape their own health plans.
Many want to keep their carefully engineered managed care programs, which rely on health maintenance organizations and networks of "preferred" doctors and hospitals that agree to play by the employers' rules.
Under the Clinton proposals, companies with fewer than 5,000 employees would have to join regional health purchasing groups, which would be responsible for cutting deals with competing local managed care groups.
The President says the employers' costs would decline because they would no longer be paying indirectly for people without insurance. But many benefits executives doubt that the new buying groups could keep the lid on costs as tightly as they could.
"We've done a lot of innovative things that we would like to continue," said Kenneth J. Reifert, benefits director for the 40,000 employees at New York-based Merrill Lynch.
Some employers would lose and others could gain. Companies with young, healthy workers, for instance, might see their premiums rise if everyone moved to a community rate.
But those companies with older workers and heavy costs for retirees would gain as the regional groups took over responsibility for retirees under age 65.
Like many employers, Merrill Lynch has shifted a growing portion of its health care costs to its employees, while offering them a choice of benefits. Now they end up paying 31 percent of the costs. But under the Clinton plan, employees would pay 20 percent and employers 80 percent.
Mr. Reifert is also concerned about the proposal for a 1 percent surtax on payroll, to cover social costs like medical education, for large employers who retain their own health plans. "For Merrill Lynch, that could increase our costs by one-third, or about $40 million, depending on how they define payroll," he said.
Would "payroll" be defined to include the stock options and bonuses, which play a big role in Wall Street pay packages? he wonders.
Some employers are taking the proposal for a 1 percent payroll tax in stride.
Rick Dreyfuss, employee benefits director of the Hershey Foods Corporation, which has 10,000 employees and more down-to-earth pay scales than some Wall Street firms, said Hershey would support the 1 percent tax for medical education.
EVEN a company with more than 5,000 employees could join one of the new regional buying groups called alliances, if the group costs seemed lower.
Some companies, in industries that place a high value on attracting skilled employees and keeping them loyal, pay for procedures like in vitro fertilization and experimental AIDS treatments, which would not be in the Clinton basic package. They could continue to do so.
"Before taking any benefit away, we would evaluate whether there was a good business reason for keeping it," said Helen Darling, manager of health care strategy and benefits at the Xerox Corporation, which covers in vitro fertilization.
A number of benefits executives said they expected to see savings from the provisions for a standard benefits package and standard claims payment forms. Some also hoped to see reduced costs for their retirees, although they would have to return some of the savings to the Government.
On the down side, the employers worried about the prospect of new bureaucracies; being forced into poorly managed regional health alliances, and facing 50 sets of state laws if they lose the Federal exemption for self-insured companies.
But much remains unclear. Mr. Reifert at Merrill Lynch thinks the Clinton Administration is using the proposals "as a straw man" to get Congress moving on health care legislation. He welcomed the President's declared openness to negotiating and compromise.
"We are in that wait-and-see mode," said Linda Lafoon, benefits manager at Ukrops Supermarkets Inc., a small family-owned regional chain based in Richmond.
Dan Heslin, employee benefits director of Rockwell International, asked, "Do we have to go as just one corporate alliance, or could we join regional alliances on an optional basis?"
Rockwell, based in Seal Beach, Calif., has 60,000 employees in 45 states. It makes aerospace products, electronics, automotive components and printing presses.
Even small employers that are struggling with soaring costs worry about the possibility of losing control over benefits programs, which many feel are essential to the morale and stability of their work force.
"I wouldn't enjoy being dictated to and being told, 'You've got to do it this way and that way,' " said Alexandrine Doesberg, risk manager at Time Equities Inc., a Manhattan real estate management company with 68 employees. Premiums, reflecting the high costs in New York and New Jersey, are $612 a month for family coverage, she said.
"If you weren't giving the proper coverage," she said, the employees "would be up in arms in here." Obviously worried about the staff's care, she added, "I'm like the mother."
Benefits executives around the country discussed their myriad hopes and fears for the Clinton plan in telephone interviews.
 
Waldorf Corporation
 Thomas S. Kalbrener, benefits manager of the Waldorf Corporation, a self-insured maker of cartons and boxboards based in St. Paul, is cautiously optimistic that: "The Clinton plan is not going to cost anywhere near as much as the naysayers are saying."
Savings will result when buyers "can compare big masses of data," he predicted. For example, he added, "It changes the way doctors practice when they find everybody is looking at how many normal infant deliveries they have, as opposed to how many Caesarean sections."
Waldorf, which has 2,000 employees in 10 states, spends $3,000 per employee and provides benefits that Mr. Kalbrener said "probably exceed" those in the Administration plan.
The company "rents" networks of doctors and hospitals at negotiated prices from Blue Cross plans in Minneapolis and other cities. He worries that some employees may be "dumped into community rated pools and will have to pay more because the community is going to decide what the cost is."
Doctors in some parts of the country do not keep a lid on spending, he said, citing his company's experiences in Iowa and North Carolina.
Mr. Kalbrener had this message for Congress and the Administration: "If they perceive that they have a system and a market that works, like we have in Minnesota, let it work, rather than force it to change dramatically."
 
Hershey Foods
 Hershey Foods, which put together a network of nine hospitals selected from 23 and associated physicians around its headquarters in Hershey, Pa., would like to phase out its traditional fee-for-service health plan, Mr. Dreyfuss, the benefits director, said.
Under the Clinton plan, companies would have to continue to offer the traditional insurance through the regional alliances. But Mr. Dreyfuss said that this type of insurance is typically chosen by the people who need the most care, ballooning costs.
"The best way to shrink the health dollar is to buy care based on the data that's available on the quality of medical outcomes," he said. Pennsylvania publishes hospital charges and reports on deaths after surgery, which Hershey used in selecting its network.
"We propose that corporations be allowed to continue their own programs," he explained. "The state could set up pools on behalf of small businesses."
Mr. Dreyfuss also questioned whether the Clinton estimates of costs were realistic. The Administration says that an employer's costs would not exceed 7.9 percent of payroll. But Hershey is currently spending more than 10 percent of payroll, he said.
 
Ukrops Supermarkets
 Linda Lafoon, at Ukrops Supermarkets in Richmond, said the Clinton plan "as it now stands, would not have a great impact" on the company's costs. But she worries about complicated reporting requirements.
Ukrops has 1,400 full-time employees and 2,900 part-timers, including many students who get their coverage in their parents' plan, and retirees with benefits from a former employer. Turnover is low, she said, partly because the employees appreciate the benefits package.
Employees pay $32, $55 or $95 a month for family coverage, depending on which health-care package they select. Annual deductibles for hospital care under the three plans are $400, $300 or $100 if they use doctors and hospitals in a Virginia Blue Cross network. Dental, hearing and eye care are included, as well as total disability insurance and company-paid health club memberships.
Part-time workers who average 30 hours a week are eligible for the basic plan, except for the dental, hearing and eye care.
Ukrops "would like to keep those benefits in place," she said. "When the Government steps into a role that has been held by private enterprise, that's always a concern," she added.
 
Microsoft Corporation

Besides the usual types of medical coverage, the Microsoft Corporation, the computer software company based in Redmond, Wash., pays for some experimental cancer and AIDS treatments, which would not be included in the Clinton plan.
Microsoft's 10,000 predominantly young employees -- the average age is 32 -- are also allowed to select naturopathic treatment, which rejects the use of medicine.
A company spokeswoman said that Microsoft nurtures a culture that tries to "stay fresh. We have a very diverse employee base and we want to stay ahead of the curve," she said.
Most of the employees are in the Redmond-Bellevue, Washington area, Steve Winter, the company's senior benefits manager, said, but about 3,000 are scattered in 35 states. As a result, he is concerned that the company may have to deal with more than 30 local health alliances and 35 sets of state laws.
Mr. Winter said Microsoft was preparing for new Federal constraints by offering a new "flex benefits plan." Employees will be offered pre-tax credits, which can be "spent" for a choice of health plans, including traditional fee-for-service coverage, a doctor and hospital network, or an H.M.O., the Group Health Cooperative of Puget Sound. They can also use the credits to buy life insurance or disability coverage.
The company plans to keep the flexible benefits approach, he said, "even if we have to take it to an after-tax basis." The Clinton proposals would remove the tax break for health benefits in flex plans. In the long run, he added, Microsoft expects the flexible plan will reduce its per-employee costs, which are already "a little" lower than the $4,200 per family that the Clinton people are discussing.
"Our numbers are skewed because of our young population," Mr. Winter said. "Over time, as we get older, the costs will rise."

HEADLINE: At Work;
Want to Survive? Learn to Negotiate!

BYLINE: By Barbara Presley Noble

 BODY:
SO, Bunky, you're depressed because the economy's dragging, your company's melting away, you've got a hefty mortgage, a Volvo with a bad case of the Swedish flu and a gaggle of orthodontia-bait kids?
Meet Nicole Schapiro, who lived through World War II and somehow survived a firing squad during the Hungarian Revolution before coming to the United States and settling, eventually, in the San Francisco Bay Area. Ms. Schapiro, a consultant and trainer with her own firm, was in Bali in 1991 teaching women to be entrepreneurs when word reached her that she had lost her house and all her belongings in the fire that ravaged the Oakland hills. 
 Ms. Schapiro sometimes talks about her own experiences to give comfort to clients, many of whom are people in corporations coping with the reverberations of downsizing or re-engineering. Her specialty is negotiating -- but not the formal, ritualized sort that occurs between lawyers, labor mediators or captives of the Uruguay Round.
Negotiating, Ms. Schapiro theorizes in person and in her new book, "Negotiating for Your Life," is an existential philosophy, a Zen state, the motive force of human relationships, not just a means to an end. "It is the lifeblood of success," she said in a recent interview. "It is building bridges and breaking down walls." It is also the stuff of normal interaction. "The day starts with negotiating, when you and your partner decide what you are doing that day," she said. "Everything is negotiating."
What does this mean to someone perched insecurely in a job that may wisp away in the next ill wind? Ms. Schapiro's prime directive? "Shift happens," she said. "Anticipate it. Try to be part of a shift rather than falling away from it."
In bad times people often retreat into a please-God-let-me-just-make-it-through-this-crisis attitude, which is really no more than paralysis. Bad times can create opportunities as well as pain, a sentiment that may sound a little too haiku for anyone living east of the Sierra Nevadas. On the other hand, when "shift happens," optimism is cheaper than Prozac and you get roughly the same result: a good mood and control. "When there is a downsizing or 'melting down' of rules," Ms. Schapiro said, "if you have good ideas, you will get attention."
Ms. Schapiro coaches her clients not to remain passive in the face of change. She suggests, however, four steps to take before initiating any kind of negotiation:
* Audit your talent. What skills -- technical? interpersonal? -- do you have that would be of high value to your organization?
* Keep a list of what you can control and what you can't, personally and professionally. If there is something you can change that would benefit you and the company, make a plan to do it.
* Assess your networks. Keep track of the people you know and the information they may have about the company.
* Keep yourself focused on what you want and understand what distracts you from your focus. Don't, for example, get obsessed with meager budgets; instead, figure out a way around the low budgets.
Finding a way around obstacles features heavily in Ms. Schapiro's counsel. She urges clients to begin with basics.
"If you know your company is going to downsize 5,000 people, then you know it's not rational or objective," she said. "Be visible. Help them. Try to find out if there are criteria, like last hired, first fired. If there are no criteria, start with that, don't personalize it. Perhaps find someone who knows your work to speak for you."
RAISE your visibility by volunteering for projects important to the company's new direction. Or by learning new skills that will be needed. Or by offering to give up or share something you have -- a large, private office, for example -- for something your department needs, like more space.
One of Ms. Schapiro's female clients at a large, famously downsizing corporation offered to travel more and for longer periods, satisfying the company's need for traveling executives and its desire to have more women on the road. The quid pro quo? Some travel perks important to the employee -- and a second look from bosses who had been ready to put her on the redundant list.
In Ms. Schapiro's experience, women have more trouble with the idea of negotiating than men do. They may not view the daily negotiations of life -- getting children dressed in the morning, for example -- as negotiating. "They say, 'Negotiate! I can't negotiate,' " she said. So they back off, keeping their heads down, working harder, hoping someone will notice their contribution. They often regard what Ms. Schapiro calls positioning as manipulation. "In general" -- and she emphasizes she is speaking generally -- "women get socialized to be frightened or be nicer in competition," she said.
To anyone, male or female, Ms. Schapiro commends the lessons of her favorite games. From tennis: "I don't focus on competing against the other person, I focus on shots, where to put my feet, following through, where I'm hitting the ball," she said. Similarly, at work, depersonalize competition and forget about crushing a competitor. "Get the best visibility, get on the best teams," she said. And from chess? Cinchy: "Think four steps ahead and don't get frozen in your plan."
 
 
 
PRACTICAL TIPS TO HELP YOU THRIVE
 MORE guidance to the art of negotiating from Nicole Schapiro:
* Match your negotiating style to the style of the person with whom you negotiate. "Datacrats" want exact numbers, usually on paper. "Steamrollers" won't sit still for long presentations. Know in advance whom you are dealing with.
* In a new job, no matter how bad the economy is, try not to accept the low part of the salary range. You will never catch up. Women especially have to pull up their socks on this issue. "When the range is $36,000 to $47,000, women ask for $38,000 and men ask for $49,000," Ms. Schapiro said. Always leave room to come down.
* But in a downsizing environment, don't focus on salary alone, unless you can't pay your rent. Work on bonuses, perks or a better job title, which may create future options.
* Be patient. It takes longer to get jobs these days.
* If you can't get what you desire, be prepared to change your desires.
