HEADLINE: World Markets;
Nikkei Gets a Face Lift -- and Futures

BYLINE: By James Sterngold

DATELINE: TOKYO

 BODY:
WHEN the Japanese stock market began its long free fall in 1990, regulators thought they knew what the problem was: Those new-fangled stock index futures, and the American brokers who used computers to trade them.
So the regulators sought to dampen the market in Nikkei 225 futures by making them more expensive -- jacking up the margins, or the downpayment investors must make on their purchases. 
 But the market just kept falling.
Margins were raised further, the manner of settlement was changed and brokers were forced to disclose all of their futures trading, the equivalent of a poker player being forced to show his hand.
The so-called cash market, where plain stocks are traded, still kept falling.
Finally, the Finance Ministry decided that the stock index futures, which give investors the ability to bet on the overall direction of the market rather than on a single stock, needed an overhaul. After a long study, new rules for trading futures on the Nikkei index were formulated, and a new index was born -- the Nikkei 300.
Although the changes may seem highly technical, they represent some big shifts. They may also move the Finance Ministry from an attitude of hostility toward the futures market, dominated by American and European traders and their computers, to one at least of benign acceptance.
First, the new index contains 300 stocks, 75 more than the old one. A total of 155 shares from the old index will be carried over into the new one.
More important, it is calculated differently. It is a weighted index, meaning that the influence of each stock on the index is a product of the total value of the company's stock relative to the market. The old 225-stock index is a simple average, in which some smaller, relatively inactive stocks can have as much influence over index fluctuations as larger stocks.
The new futures contracts are expected to begin trading on the Osaka Stock Exchange early next year. At least initially, the old and new index futures will trade side by side. Eventually, the old futures may be phased out. Generally, experts in derivatives -- as futures and options contracts are known -- are pleased simply to have some certainty.
"We don't like question marks," said Rick Gould, a managing director in the equity derivatives department here of Morgan Stanley & Company in Tokyo. "The worry was we just wouldn't know what was going to happen."
The ministry has been explaining, brokers said, that the new Nikkei 300 futures will have four key features:
* The value of each new futures contract will be roughly half the value of the old one. That will put them more within the reach of individual investors. However, it also means large institutional investors, who deal in huge volumes, will have to buy or sell twice the number of contracts for an equivalent sized transaction. That will make the new futures slightly more cumbersome, traders said.
* Margins on the futures will come down to roughly half of where they are now over a two-year period. Currently, customers must make a down payment equal to 30 percent of the face value of the futures contracts they buy. A little over three years ago, the margin had been 9 percent, a figure that was more in line with international standards.
* On some occasions in the past, the authorities here have fought foreign exchanges listing futures contracts based on the Nikkei index, fearful that that put the trading out of the Finance Ministry's reach. But ministry officials have said they will not stand in the way of such trading on the new index.
* Rather than attempting to throttle futures trading whenever the market grows choppy, ministry officials say they will do more to actively promote the use of stock index futures as a way of drawing in more investors and giving them greater choice.
"Our view is it is still too early to judge whether this will be more acceptable to the market," said Ang Suee Tian, president of the Singapore International Monetary Exchange, which has an actively traded Nikkei 225 futures contract. "But if the market prefers it, we'll have to consider taking steps to respond to that."
The indexes are formulated by the Nihon Keizai Shimbun, Japan's leading business newspaper. (Nikkei is just a contraction of the paper's name.) The 225-stock index, introduced shortly after World War II, contains the stocks of many old-line industrial companies whose role in the economy have declined dramatically.
The new index reflects the new Japan. Companies like Japan Wool Textile and Sumitomo Coal Mining have been dropped, while Seven-Eleven Japan, the convenience store chain, and Nintendo, the electronic game maker, were added.
Clearly, the Finance Ministry hopes the new index will reduce volatility. But the first reaction to the release of the new list several weeks ago was a jolt of selling. Investors dumped some of the stocks left off the new list, while snapping up the newcomers. The Nikkei 225 tumbled 241.33 points to 20,137.31 on the first day after the announcement.
Still, traders were sanguine. In fact, few really accepted the idea that the index futures were behind the market's plunge, and so they do not expect the new index to change things much, except to calm the fidgety Finance Ministry.

LENGTH: 1539 words

HEADLINE: Mighty Brazil Rides A Free-Market Wave

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE

DATELINE: SAO PAULO

 BODY:
WHEN Paul Duval travels to the United States and Europe, all he hears are jokes about Brazil's 2,000 percent annual inflation and Brazil's "minister-of-the-month," the revolving door in the top levels of government.
That's fine for this American shopping center developer. Why invite competition when you are making money like never before? "Business is absolutely booming," said Mr. Duval, a managing partner in Shopinvest, based in Rio de Janeiro. "We are building three shopping centers, in Rio, Sao Paulo and Brasilia, for a total of $180 million." 
 Prices jumping by 37 percent a month don't faze another American, Peter Rodenbeck, a longtime Rio resident who administers about half of Brazil's 121 McDonald's.
"We are going to increase the number of our restaurants by 20 percent over the next 18 months," Mr. Rodenbeck said. "In the last year, there has been a veritable invasion of Brazil by American brand names -- Pizza Hut, Arby's, Kentucky Fried Chicken."
Indeed, American investors are increasingly taking a second look at Brazil, a nation with an economy slightly larger than Russia's and almost twice the size of Mexico's. With the bloom off of Eastern Europe as an investment opportunity, Brazil is emerging as the last major Latin American country heading toward a free market revival. The major threats continue to be a weak Government and high inflation.
Heavy American investment has helped fuel a stock market boom here. Rising faster than any other Latin American market this year, Sao Paulo's exchange has doubled in dollar value since January.
"Brazil is perceived to be the last to boom," Alden Brewster, a New York investor in emerging markets, said on a recent visit here. "Peru has boomed. Argentina has boomed. At worst, people think that Brazil will muddle through."
In the highest economic growth since 1986, Brazil's $450 billion economy is expected to expand by 5 percent this year. Referring to the size of Chile's economy, Sao Paulo's business press boasts that Brazil this year is "growing a Chile." But the growth is actually a recovery from several recessionary years.
Fueled by lower interest rates and pent up demand, residential construction has doubled in Sao Paulo, television sales have jumped nationwide by 30 percent to 3 million, and car makers believe they will build 1.3 million vehicles, another Brazilian record.
Import tariffs are now at less than half their 1990 levels, forcing manufacturers to tighten productivity to meet international competition. Since 1990, consumers have seen dollar prices drop in half for Brazilian-made stoves, bicycles, dishwashers, computers and video cassette recorders.
Since 1990, Sharp do Brasil, a subsidiary of the Japanese multinational, has cut by two thirds the time spent producing televisions and videocassettes in Brazil. Today, Sharp assembly lines produce a television in 25 minutes and a videocassette recorder in 41 minutes -- times equal to or better than the company's Japanese plants.
Some Brazilian economists complain of "jobless growth," but increased productivity means that exports surge alongside imports. During the first nine months of 1993, Brazil's foreign trade hit $48 billion, an 18 percent jump over the same period last year. Despite higher imports, Brazil's trade surplus is expected to be $15 billion -- 50 percent over the average level of the 1980's.
Following the rule that your neighbor knows you best, Argentine manufacturers are terrified that Sao Paulo will flood their markets once the Southern Cone Common Market implements zero tariffs between Argentina and Brazil at the end of next year.
"Argentina has grown in the last 14 months to become our third-largest export market, after the United States and Europe," said Lucas G. Godinez, president of Cummins do Brasil S.A., a major manufacturer of diesel engines here. "Our Argentine competitors are hanging on by their oxygen masks."
But the new self-confidence of Brazil's private sector could be betrayed by gross mismanagement in the public sector.
In a litany that sounds like a chamber of commerce Christmas carol, Brazil has had, since 1990, five central bank presidents, six treasury ministers and seven agriculture ministers. With the current President, Itamar Franco, a lame duck, the situation is not expected to improve. (A new president will be inaugurated Jan. 1, 1995.)
This month, Brazilian congressmen looked the nation's $25 billion budget deficit projected for 1994 squarely in the eye. Then they announced they would not cut spending and raise taxes in an election year. By December, inflation per month could easily be double the 19 percent yearly average for the rest of Latin America.
"Manageable levels of inflation are a requirement," said Sebastian Edwards, the World Bank's chief economist for Latin America. "Otherwise, this year's growth will be a blip. It will not be sustainable."
NEVERTHELESS, Brazil's ship of state seems to be slowly steaming down a new, free market course. A privatization program is methodically picking away at the edges of the mammoth state sector. Ending half a century of involvement in steel making, the Government this year sold all its steel companies to private investors. In the last two years, 24 of Brazil's 135 federally-owned companies have been sold, and 65 more are on the block. A final group of 45 state companies cannot be privatized without amending Brazil's Constitution.
"By the end of 1994, you are going to see the Brazilian state involved in a lot fewer activities," said Andre Franco Montoro Filho, president of the Government's Brazilian Privatization Program.
Faced with electricity blackouts looming in the late 1990's, the Government plans to auction off two regional electric companies and 19 unfinished hydroelectric dams. With the Ministry of Transport rating one third of Brazil's railway and Federal highway network as "bad" or "terrible," plans are being drawn up to lease railroads and to allow private construction companies to repair highways in return for collecting tolls.
Although $6.5 billion worth of sales make Brazil's privatization program the largest state selloff underway in Latin America, some critics say it is puny compared with the size of Brazil's state-owned industries.
"Russia in one year moved one quarter of the public sector into private hands, Mexico in four years has done well over 1,000 privatizations," said Rudiger Dornbusch, an economics professor at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. "Brazil's program is timid. It isn't major. It isn't impressive."
But the program is an achievement in a nation where the President views privatization as a necessary evil. And in November, Congress is to start a constitutional review, with business lobbying for it to open oil, mining and telecommunications to foreign investors.
Already, Petrobras, the state oil company, and Embratel, the state telephone company, are blanketing the nation with glossy magazine inserts designed to remind readers of their vital importance.
Petrobras neglects to note that, aside from Chile, Brazil is the only major Latin American nation that imports oil -- more than $100 billion worth over the last two decades, making oil the nation's largest single import. Similarly, the state telephone company neglects to note that in the last five years, Argentina, Chile, Mexico and Venezuela have privatized their national telephone companies.
THE price of protectionism is clear to Guillermo Acosta, managing director the A.T.&T. Corporation's Southern Cone division. "A.T.& T. started operations in Mexico and Brazil in the same month in 1987," he recalled. "Today in Mexico, we have close to 6,000 employees, nine factories and $1 billion in annual revenues. In Brazil, we have 155 employees and annual revenues of $100 million."
Brazil's President, arguing that state oil and telephone companies are strategic assets, has vowed not to ask Congress to allow foreign investment in these areas. Although unions are lobbying hard against privatization, business lobbyists believe that time is on their side. A recent survey of 436 Brazilian congressional representatives found that 68 percent favored allowing private companies to expand their activity in Brazil's telecommunications field.
"Either you open up, or you remain behind," Oscar Dias Correa Jr., a lobbyist for private telephone companies. "There is an increasing awareness that Brazil can't stay behind."
Meanwhile, Brazilian businessmen increasingly say they have learned to work around the chronic disarray reining in Brasilia. "The country depends a lot less on the state, the government or whatever you want to call that semi-bust organization based in Brasilia," Exame, Brazil's leading business magazine, wrote this month. "Brazil today can function despite the screwups, sometimes criminal, perpetrated in Brasilia."
Luiz Luciano Costa, a vice president for Cargill Agricola S.A., said his company (annual sales: $1 billion) is continuing with long-term investment plans despite the drumbeat of disorganization emanating from the capital. "Like all Brazilian companies," he said, "we are putting Brasilia aside and are going ahead with our lives."

HEADLINE: Term Limits Drive the Talented Out of Politics

 BODY:
To the Editor:
As chairman of the Committee on Elections and Reapportionment of the California State Senate since 1987, I have been directly involved in the state and national term-limit debate since its most recent incarnation as a popular political cure-all. In California, term limits for state officeholders were enacted in 1990; term limits for members of Congress were similarly enacted in 1992. 
 In "Term Limits Limit Voters' Rights" (editorial, Oct. 21), you point out many of the drawbacks California is facing as a result of term limits -- most notably the decline of invaluable legislative expertise and institutional memory, coupled with an increase in the power and influence of bureaucrats and lobbyists.
What voters may not realize, however, is the speed at which this brain drain will occur. As the California experience has proved, many of the best and brightest of New York's elected officials will not wait until term limits force them from office. They will resign midterm, as soon as they are offered other, usually private, positions in organizations that reward, rather than punish experience and dedication.
Early, midterm departures by several legislators have had a chaotic effect on state government and have resulted in multiple special elections to fill the vacancies. These special elections have already cost our state and counties millions of dollars at a time when California is suffering through its worst recession and budget crisis since the Depression.
If those who have been leaving public service were the untalented or the corrupt, then perhaps this enormous cost would be worth it, and the term limit proponents would be vindicated. But the untalented and the corrupt don't get attractive outside offers -- the innovative and the energetic do. Encouraging the kind of people that should be running for public office to do so is difficult enough; term limits will make it almost impossible.
As a result, the people of California and New York will be the ultimate losers.
 
MILTON MARKS
Sacramento, Calif., Oct. 22, 1993

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: LIGHTS! CAMERA!;
Clinton at Work: Is Motion the Same Thing as Action?

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
"REMEMBER," said the silver-haired Democratic elder, one of the members of the capital's permanent establishment that watches them come and watches them go, "remember that Clinton is trying to do a lot, and that what he's trying to do is very difficult, and that he starts from a position of weakness."
Except from the true believers, that is the best one hears about Bill Clinton in political Washington these days. Cut him some slack, people say. 
 And it is true enough: this is a President, elected by a minority, with only a narrow margin in the Senate, with no room for fiscal maneuvering at a time of paralyzing budget constraint, who is nonetheless trying to invent a foreign policy to deal with a not-so-brave new world, to enact a comprehensive health care plan (a prize that has eluded every president from Franklin Roosevelt on), to push through a potentially revolutionary trade agreement with Mexico and at the same time to nurse the economy back into robust good health.
But the prevailing impression is that of a President blown this way and that by a never-ceasing series of tempests -- homosexuals in the military, trouble in the travel office, Bosnia, the Waco debacle, Somalia, a failed job-stimulus proposal, a budget barely enacted, Haiti, a health-care plan delayed.
Mr. Clinton gets less credit than he deserves for his accomplishments, such as his deft handling of the crises in the former Soviet Union and the enactment of a popular family leave bill, because something else always goes wrong.
As a result, this has become an Administration of relaunches. Like a manufacturer whose product stays stubbornly on the shelves, the President is constantly changing the wrapper and the advertising, then gathering his advisers around him to make a fresh start. Another, and yet another.
So it was with the appointment of David R. Gergen, and the quartet of foreign-policy speeches late this summer, and a half-dozen other fresh initiatives.
So it was last week with the health-care legislation, which seemed to have so much momentum after Mr. Clinton's vivid speech on Capitol Hill a month ago, then faltered as its details were massaged in an effort to meet early criticism.
 
Starting Over
 By the time the President stood in the Capitol on Wednesday to press the cause of reform once again, with a fervent declaration that the nation was "choking on a health-care system that is not working," it was as if he were starting from scratch again. (Outline of the plan, page 3.) There was also a distinct if subtle change in the mood of the place. In September, there had been widespread praise, with calls from some quarters for modifications or fine-tuning; this time, there was widespread agreement that the final plan would be a good deal more modest than the one proposed by Mr. Clinton, and praise only for the general idea.
To some degree, the change in atmosphere resulted from the greater detail (and hence the clearer target) of the President's new proposal, but he also paid a price for delay. Counter-proposals have been worked up by Republicans and conservative Democrats, negative ads on television have eroded public support for the Administration's plans and the credibility of Mr. Clinton himself has been worn down by the controversies that have washed over him.
The Clinton Administration, the critics say from time to time, is becalmed. But that suggests a lack of motion or energy, and this is an Administration that never stops, led by a hyperthyroid President whose advisers concluded last week that one of his problems is that he's wearing out his welcome on television. Nothing is calm in the Presidential precinct these days; all is noise, dust, confusion and seemingly disjointed activity.
"God knows he's trying," said a Democratic Congressman who first came to Capitol Hill during the Eisenhower years. "I think he may be trying too hard. I'm reluctantly coming to the conclusion that he has the dreaded Carter disease -- no focus, no continuity, confusing motion with accomplishment."
In such an environment, accomplishments get scant attention, and little was paid last week to the suddenly improved prospects for the Brady bill, the long-debated restrictions on handguns. Likewise, two of Mr. Clinton's most applauded nominations, Janet Reno for Attorney General and Ruth Bader Ginsburg for Associate Justice of the Supreme Court, lost some political sheen because they came at the end of messy selection procedures.
One of the enduring questions amid these alarums and excursions, is whether Mr. Clinton has specific policy goals in which he is willing to invest his political capital.
The theme of the presentations on health care that he and Hillary Rodham Clinton made last week was that neither had any "pride of authorship" in the bill they were presenting. It is full of inconsistencies, ambiguities and compromises, with more surely to come. To some, those and the Clintons' appeals to bipartisanship reflect a canny awareness of the limits on Presidential leadership in this era of ever-weaker parties and ever-stronger pressure groups. To others, they represent a certain lack of steel.
 
Early Retreat
 On the budget, Mr. Clinton often seemed to retreat from a prepared defensive line -- the gasoline tax, for example -- even before the enemy attack reached him.
But the appearance of inconstancy has been most vivid in foreign policy. Last week, the subject was Haiti. Having signed an agreement under which President Jean-Bertrand Aristide was to be returned from exile by Saturday, and having been defied, the Administration had to watch, at least momentarily helpless, as the deadline slipped by with Father Aristide still in Washington.
Haiti, it can be argued, is small potatoes compared with Russia or China or even Bosnia. Critics argue that the United States should either send in the Marines or abandon the present plans for the restoration of democracy. But Mr. Clinton and his people respond, with some credibility, that tougher sanctions, rigorously enforced, stand at least as good a chance of eventual success.
In the end it may not be the policy that matters as much as the means of reaching it, the skill in articulating it and the steadiness in following it. On that score, the Administration is doing poorly. The rest of the world has been watching these last few months, and what it sees worries it.
"When you had an idea about what you wanted to do in Bosnia," said an ambassador from a western European country, "you sent Warren Christopher to us to say, not 'This is what we want, join us,' but 'Here is an idea, but if you don't like it, that's okay, too.' That was Clinton's fault, not Christopher's. We don't see isolationism in this, or in the inconsistencies in Somalia and Haiti, but I do see a President who is reluctant to reach out and shape the issues, in the way that only the United States can do it in today's world."
In the tartest comment to date from a senior Republican, Dick Cheney, a former defense secretary and a potential Presidential candidate in 1996, said on public television the other night: "If you're in North Korea today and you're told by the President of the United States that developing a nuclear weapon is unacceptable, and then you watch the performance in Haiti, you have to wonder whether or not you have to pay any attention to what he says."
This has domestic political consequences, too. A majority of voters may be happy that no Americans are in Bosnia, fewer are under fire in Somalia and none have landed in Haiti. They may be paying more attention to the economy's steady recovery and to the health care debate than to foreign policy questions; they usually do. But if the voters come to the conclusion that the man in the White House is unreliable, that he will make the wrong moves when the nation is backed into a corner, he will forfeit their confidence. Even before jobs, Americans want national security, and a President who dithers, even more than a President who blunders, will be made to pay. Ask Jimmy Carter.

HEADLINE: Conversations/Paul A. Samuelson;
The Man Who Wrote the Book Suggests Econ 101 for Presidents

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
ASK Paul A. Samuelson how he came to win a Nobel Memorial Prize in Economics, and he begins by talking about his teen-age years during the Great Depression, not far from the steel mills of Gary, Ind.
"One out of three Americans couldn't find a full-time job," said Mr. Samuelson, now a professor emeritus at M.I.T. "There was no money for relief. It was a poverty amidst plenty. If you lived in a middle-class neighborhood, little children came nightly with a note saying, 'We're starving, we're starving. Could you give us a potato or a dime?' " 
 He talked about failed banks, a brother whose savings were wiped out, and soon he was extolling his intellectual hero, John Maynard Keynes, the British economist whose revolutionary rev-up-the-economy theories helped pull the world out of depression. Then Mr. Samuelson described how, as a Harvard graduate student in the late 1930's, he wrote a dissertation full of economic equations that helped explain and buttress the Keynesian system. This work was instrumental in his winning the economics prize in 1970.
Although he is 78 years old, there is more than a touch of youthful optimism in Mr. Samuelson -- indeed, he is youthful enough to play tennis every day. As one would expect from someone who has written the world's most famous economics textbook, which has been translated into more than 20 languages, he is convinced that wise economics can make this world a better place.
And he isn't shy about giving advice. His speech resembles the plain talk of President Truman -- unless he is conversing in the intellectual stratosphere with other economists. His dress is as tweedy as the Hollywood image of a Cambridge professor, and he has a power of recall that has made decades of students marvel. He quotes Adam Smith, Voltaire and Cato the Elder as effortlessly as Jimmy Connors serves.
Mr. Samuelson has an intellectually straightforward prescription for easing America's economic ills: save more and invest more. If the United States only put more of its income into savings, he explained, it would have more money to invest in education, factories and new technologies, and this would make the nation's economy grow faster and richer.
But getting Americans to save more is easier said than done, he acknowledged. If Mr. Samuelson, who is as gentle as he is gentlemanly, gets angry about anything, it is the budgetary profligacy of the 1980's, a profligacy that favored consumption over saving. Although he describes himself as a "mainstream economist," not embarrassed about always saying on the one hand, this, and on the other hand, that, he sounds as unforgiving as any leftist radical in attacking President Reagan.
"American society was economically ill-run in the 1980's," he said, chopping his right hand through the air. "Our society has been on a consumption binge. If the American people had a town meeting and said, 'What do we care about posterity? Posterity hasn't done anything for us, we're going to whoop it up now,' that is a rational judgment.' But nobody ever did that. We just backed in by the back door."
"People have the wrong idea that God will forgive Reagan. They say he didn't know what he was doing. It's true he didn't know a lot of what was going on, but he was directly responsible."
In Mr. Samuelson's view, the American people were bamboozled by a President who persuaded them that tax cuts would lead to higher revenues without producing a Himalaya of debt. The deficits that are Mr. Reagan's legacy, he said, are eating away at the nation because they mean overconsumption and underinvestment, and ultimately less growth.
Speaking with the candor that is allowed tenured professors, but not politicians, he has a recommendation for cutting the deficit that Americans don't want to hear: higher taxes. "America is not remotely near the limits of taxation, and one more pfennig is not going to break the camel's back," he said.
Mr. Samuelson loves to talk politics, although, not surprisingly, he finds it inseparable from economics. Over the years, Mr. Samuelson has advised, usually informally, Presidents, senators and Federal agencies. During the 1960 presidential campaign, he was John F. Kennedy's chief economic adviser, although he did not have a formal position in the Kennedy Administration.
"People had J.F.K. all wrong," he said. "They thought of him as a dashing, deciding type. He was an extremely hesitant person who checked the ice in front of him all the time. He said it was vanity to use your political capital on lost causes."
Mr. Samuelson has an interesting take on last year's Presidential election, almost describing President Bush as a hapless victim of economic developments, including the Federal Reserve's failure to lower interest rates nearly as fast as Mr. Samuelson said was desirable.
"The Fed caused George Bush's defeat, in my interpretation of history. It did it by acting too little and too late, always behind the wave," he said, adding that other, more powerful forces were also at work.
"The deeper cause was good jobs are being lost in America, good jobs on Main Street, at I.B.M., Digital, Procter & Gamble." he said. "George Bush had one speech like Cato the Elder in Rome -- 'Carthage must be destroyed,' Bush always said, 'We must cut the capital gains tax, and that will turn around the economy.' The American public didn't buy it." (Mr. Samuelson supports a cut in capital gains taxes but doesn't see it as a panacea.)
Mr. Clinton could also become a victim of inexorable economic forces, Mr. Samuelson, a Democrat, fears. "There is a signficant chance that we're talking about a one-term President," he said. "We're in an age of anxiety and disillusionment. I honor him for his initiatives, but they may be too much for the political process to handle."
When he talks, Mr. Samuelson sometimes seems disappointed that the world's leaders did not learn enough from his textbook. That tome, which sells for about $50, was first published in 1948 and has sold more than 4 million copies, making its author a millionaire many times over. "I never dreamed that it would have such lasting power," he said. "But it became a chain around my neck -- it constantly demanded updating." It is now in its 14th edition.
In Mr. Samuelson's free time, he often scribbles down equations on scraps of paper, like Picasso doodling. When he was in Washington one recent day for a meeting at the National Academy of Sciences, his scrap filled up with econometric symbols.
As should be expected, Mr. Samuelson had an opinion on the most burning economic issue of the day, the North American Free Trade Agreement. He supports it, saying it will increase average incomes in the United States and Mexico. He admits that some jobs will be lost because of the accord, and understands that many Americans oppose it out of anxiety about their jobs.
"We've done a pretty good job creating jobs, better than in Europe, but they tend to be low-paying jobs," he said. "The vice president of a firm on Route 128 outside Boston gets laid off, and ends up with a job as a manager at Kentucky Fried Chicken. You can't be contemptuous of that if that's all there is. The best job is better than unemployment. We can't all be pitchers for the New York Yankees."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Economist Paul A. Samuelson in his Cambridge, Mass. office. (Evan Richman for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE NATION: Small Cities Lead an Economic Charge;
The South Renews Its Sense of Self

BYLINE: By PETER APPLEBOME

DATELINE: CHARLOTTE, N.C.

 BODY:
"IN the feeling of the South," W. J. Cash wrote a half century ago in his quirky classic "The Mind of the South," "Progress stood quite accurately for a sort of new charge at Gettysburg."
After decades of tectonic shifts in the balance of regional power in this country, the South's view of growth still seems to echo the notion that economic progress would give the Civil War's losers the last word. Now it is worth asking whether a modern version of Pickett's charge at Gettysburg, which doomed the Confederacy, is succeeding at last. 
 Last week, in what is becoming a familiar Southern ritual, this Piedmont commercial and banking center went happily berserk after beating out four competitors including St. Louis and Baltimore for the 29th National Football League franchise and the perceived imprimatur of big-time status that comes with it. The scene echoed similar celebrations, from Atlanta bringing home the 1996 Summer Olympics, to Greer, S.C., and Vance, Ala., being selected as sites for BMW and Mercedes plants in the most lucrative manufacturing site selections of the decade.
While Los Angeles is seen battling fires driven by the Santa Ana winds and New York is thrashing through a racially charged mayoral election, the image of the South is slowly being redefined, like a photograph in the process of being developed. Whether the picture will get it right or become a rosy new stereotype as simplistic as the one of the Old South remains to be seen.
It is probably no accident that Charlotte is the home of something called the Museum of the New South. More obsessed with growth than race, more rooted in quarterly reports than Faulkner or Welty, this relentlessly upbeat home to platoons of earnest young bankers out of the University of South Carolina and Wake Forest embodies the lament diehard Southerners often voice about Atlanta: a quarter-million Confederate soldiers died for this?
But if this city of 400,000 is not much like the mythic South, it is the newest version of the New South. Like the mainstream popularity of the suburbanized country music of Garth Brooks or Vince Gill, the appeal of places like Charlotte still hasn't registered with tastemakers on the two coasts. But the city's relaxed style, low living costs and relative distance from the "Bladerunner" image of urban America is increasingly what Americans seem to be searching for. A decade ago, the idea of Charlotte -- a city of just over 200,000 people in 1970 -- having an N.F.L. franchise would have been about as ridiculous as little Raleigh-Durham being picked the nation's best city for business by Fortune magazine. Last week both things happened.
Neither is an isolated incident. While the Northeast and West Coast have lost more than 450,000 manufacturing jobs in the last two years, the Southeast has gained 23,000. And from Nashville to Greenville, S.C., second- and third-tier Southern cities are growing like few places in America.
 
Not the Plantation South
 But, in a region that has always been more heterogeneous in its industry and agriculture than its stereotypes, prosperity exists not across the South, but in a certain conspicuous pockets. For the most part, it is limited to a corridor of cities running west from Richmond across the Carolina Piedmont to Atlanta, over to Birmingham and north to Nashville. Also thriving are coastal resort areas like Hilton Head, S.C., and North Carolina's Outer Banks and retirement centers in the Ozarks and Smokeys like Bentonville, Ark., or Asheville, N.C.
Culturally, what is succeeding is the South that was once small farms and textile plants, not the plantation South of the Cotton Belt. It is almost entirely a metropolitan prosperity, one largely of new cities, not old ports like New Orleans or Savannah. And the areas that are flourishing are almost invariably the ones that made their peace most amicably with integration three decades ago.
"My favorite statistic is that in the first half of the 80's, 90 percent of all new jobs in the South were in Texas and Florida and a dozen metropolitan areas elsewhere," said John Shelton Reed, a University of North Carolina sociologist. "So if you're looking for poor people, the South is still a good place to find them, and you find them geographically concentrated and ethnically distinct, whether it's poor white folks in the mountains and poor black folks in the Cotton Belt." An estimated 80 percent of the black children under 6 growing up in the rural South live below the poverty level, and from eastern North Carolina to the Alabama Black Belt to the Mississippi Delta, the worst stereotypes of what the South was -- poor, badly educated and racially divided -- still stubbornly persist.
Limited or not, there is a growing sense of vindication throughout the South. Every N.F.L. franchise or corporate relocation southward takes on the tint of metaphor and each success encourages something approaching hysteria. When the Charlotte Observer reported on the N.F.L.'s decision by proclaiming, "And forevermore, this single decision will likely change the way people across the country feel about what lies between Washington and Atlanta," the impression conveyed was one in which sweaty behemoths butting heads on Sunday were the least of the excitement.
"This kind of thing is what Henry Grady was promoting in the 1880's for Atlanta," said Professor Reed, referring to the editor of the Atlanta Constitution and the civic and regional promoter who helped champion the "New South." "Beating the North on their own terms."
The difference now is that the South is often doing it. Some critics from the South and elsewhere say success comes at too high a price, whether in industrial giveaways, a low-paid workforce or lax environmental enforcement that will come back to haunt the region. And the South still faces mammoth problems, from substandard education to the growing chasm between the haves and have-nots.
But, at a time when Los Angeles, New York and major cities elsewhere are grappling with an unrest born of economic uncertainty, joblessness and crime, there is a sense throughout the South that the tide of history is coming back its way.
"Our President is a Southerner, our Vice President is a Southerner, and I think there's a feeling regionally that we have finally put the monster to bed," said Dan Morrill, a history professor at the University of North Carolina here. "After all these years, the verdict of Gettysburg has finally been neutralized."

HEADLINE: THE WORLD: The Conservatives' Devastating Defeat;
Canada Joins the World in Dumping Incumbent Leaders

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH

DATELINE: MONTREAL

 BODY:
IN a sense, the Canadian elections last week constituted a revolution. With the Conservatives nearly obliterated, regional parties have been infused with sudden national power, and are threatening to dissolve the country into jealous fiefs. Only the Liberals, swept to victory by Ontario, the most populous and richest province, remain a force to hold things together.
The changes, which give the Liberals led by Jean Chretien a comfortable majority of 178 out of 295 House of Commons seats but have created hostile factions of western Reformers and Quebec separatists, took place for reasons that are both born of Canadian politics and are also international. Nine years ago the Conservatives, led by Brian Mulroney, came to power with the largest number of seats in the history of Canada. But his Government, and that of his successor Kim Campbell, could not control high joblessness, high budget deficits and debt. So a grassroots fury developed against Mr. Mulroney, and it was followed by a campaign of gaffes by Ms. Campbell. 
 The astonishing result last Monday was that their party was left with just two seats in Parliament and left to wonder whether it can ever rebuild.
At the same time, Canada is part of the global village, and it is not alone in giving the heave-ho to long-entrenched political parties. France, Italy, Japan and the United States all did the same in the last 12 months as voters did what they are supposed to do -- hold leaders responsible for bad times.
Peter C. Newman, economics columnist and a former managing editor of Maclean's, the Canadian newsweekly, put it succinctly: "Incumbents are toast."
Around the world, common problems have compounded the difficulties of leaders trying to keep the public's confidence. The sluggishness of most industrial economies has cut revenues and increased deficits and debts, threatening funding for welfare systems established in the good years. Even Canada's venerated national health insurance system has retrenched as it too comes under the fiscal guns. Global stagnation, moreover, has hit raw material prices, and this especially hurts Canada, where energy and mineral extraction still employ millions.
While external economics has worked against Canada and other countries, what has particularly aggravated voters here is the sense that politicians who have been in office too long lose touch with the people who elected them.
"There is the habit of politicians in most of these democracies to take on priestly attitudes, to believe they are demigods, to play in the sandbox of power and throw sand in the eyes of the citizens who put them there," says a Canadian broadcast executive, Keith Spicer.
Mr. Spicer detected the extent of the rage against Canadian politicians back in 1991 when he headed the Citizens Forum on Canada's Future, a Government-organized commission that assessed Canada's mood by contacting nearly one million citizens.
"Our report told politicians Canadians wanted them to listen to the people, to stop playing little parlor games in Ottawa, to do what they promised they would do and if they didn't the people said, 'We'll recall you.' "
What caused Mr. Mulroney's popularity to plummet to the lowest level for any Prime Minister this century was precisely that he didn't do what he had promised to do. He had, after all, won two elections by forging a coalition based on those promises.
To those concerned about Canada's debt, he had promised to cut the deficit. But he made too many compromises on spending, and while the deficit today is lower as a percentage of gross national product, it is still an astronomical $27 billion. Canada's national debt is so high that one-third of revenues goes to pay the interest.
Mr. Mulroney did introduce free trade with the United States, but here he over-promised on the benefits it would provide. And he did little to deal with the structural adjustments free trade imposed on the Canadian economy, especially during a time of recession when factories were shutting production lines anyway. As a result, tens of thousands of workers lost their jobs.
At the same time, many people in Quebec turned to his party during the 1984 and 1988 elections believing that he could deliver on promises of constitutional reform that would give Quebec the special status it had sought as a distinct society. He undertook national efforts in that direction, but could not get either of two proposed agreements adopted.
The succession of failures had devastating results for the Tories in both the west and Quebec. In the west, which has a long tradition of not running deficits, disillusioned Tories turned on Monday to Preston Manning's Reform party. And in Quebec, the erstwhile Tories were ripe for plucking by Lucien Bouchard's Bloc Quebecois, which wants Quebec out of the federation.
The Reform party speaks for many westerners who feel alienated from Ottawa because of their distance from the capital and sense that the power in Canada is exercised by Ontario and Quebec -- much as many Westerners in the United States feel alienated from Washington. For 24 of the last 25 years a Quebecer has been Prime Minister, a tradition now continued by Mr. Chretien, who is from Shawinigan, a town northeast of Montreal.
Mr. Manning, who represents a district in Calgary, Alberta, has developed a platform which would have a familiar ring to conservative western Americans in its demands for slashing budget deficits, and in its appeals for more direct democracy in the form of petitions to recall politicians and the use of more referendums.
Such parallels are more than a coincidence of geography. Alberta was deluged by Americans after 1896 when homesteading ended in the United States (in Alberta land was still free) and their descendants make up much of today's population.
 
A Rapid Slide
 As for the defeated Prime Minister, Ms. Campbell, who headed into the election campaign with a small lead over the Liberals, she will probably have to content herself only with the distinction of having been the first woman to become Canada's leader. When she took over, at a time when President Clinton and Hillary Clinton were still new in the White House, Ms. Campbell, youthful and sprightly, seemed a perfect choice for the times.
But the problems that had accumulated during the years of Conservative rule caught up with her, and were only compounded by the missteps of her campaign -- most notably, an "attack" ad that backfired because it ridiculed the way Mr. Chretien appears as the result of a partial facial paralysis. Now she seems headed for political oblivion.
In that, said Richard Gwyn, a columnist for the Toronto Star, she is "like some unknown pop singer, who lucks into one song that captures the public imagination, but who never gets back on the charts."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Jean Chretien, the new Prime Minister of Canada, near his hometown of Shawingigan, Quebec. (Associated Press)

LENGTH: 1317 words

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Worried Anew, Europe Asks How Best to Keep the Peace

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY

DATELINE: BRUSSELS

 BODY:
EUROPEAN nation states have always been each other's worst enemies.
After the convulsion of World War II the Western European nations agreed to start over. The European Community and the American-led NATO alliance guaranteed that they would never fight each other again and formed a bulwark against the new external threat from the Soviet Union. But that is now gone, and where Communism used to be, history has fallen back into the old fratricidal patterns. 
 To ward off disaster, Western leaders keep repeating incantations that worked during the postwar era, but they have been ineffective in the new world that opened with the end of the cold war. Now they are beginning to grope for new ones.
This month Secretary of State Warren M. Christopher shuttled between Moscow, Central Asia, Eastern Europe and the Baltic states trying to persuade them of the usefulness of a new stopgap American proposal to fill at least part of the vacuum. This "Partnership for Peace" would try to give Eastern Europe a new basis for security by offering limited cooperation with NATO at the military-to-military level to all who want it, including Russia.
But Russia and its neighbors have far from similar views of what they need for their own security. Russia seeks, as it did in centuries past, to insure its security by asserting influence over its near neighbors to the west and south. What these neighbors need most, however, is security from Russian domination.
There is perhaps an analogy here to what NATO and the European Community did for Germany and its neighbors during the cold war, pooling their security and protecting them not only from the Soviet threat but reassuring other Europeans that Germany was under control as well.
But security requires sacrifice, and today none of the rich Western nations, including the United States, seems willing to sacrifice for security against the new threat of militant nationalism and ethnic instability east of the German-Polish border.
What Western Europe wants is basically to go on living the prosperous sheltered life it led for 40 years, in part at American expense in the form of 314,000 G.I.'s and thousands of nuclear weapons on European soil.
 
What Everyone Wants
 What the United States wants is for Europeans to take on more of the military burden of their own security within NATO and the Western European Union, the European Community's embryonic military arm, but not if it means a loss of American political and strategic leverage in the alliance.
And what everybody wants is an opportunity not to worry about security -- to cut military budgets and get on with the good life.
Eastern European nations like the Czech Republic, Hungary and Poland know that as long as ethnic conflicts rage in Bosnia, Georgia, Azerbaijan and Armenia, and fester in Romania, Moldova and perhaps elsewhere, there is no safety for them. They fear, too, that nostalgia for the old imperial days -- whether czarist or Stalinist hardly matters -- will tempt Russia to exert its influence over them, just as they have finally become independent.
The United States and the Western European countries support President Boris N. Yeltsin as the best hope there is for democracy and a market economy in Russia, and he probably is. But Mr. Yeltsin and the military and security forces he needs to keep himself in power have hardly been reassuring to the Eastern Europeans.
One of them, a diplomat, put it this way in Moscow recently: "There are two souls fighting in Yeltsin's breast. One is a democrat, and one is a traditional Russian autocrat. Right now, unfortunately, keeping himself in power is more important to him than democratic principles."
But as the bloody showdown with the old parliament in Moscow early this month showed, the alternative to Mr. Yeltsin is not someone nicer; it's someone far worse.
 
An Active Russian Military
 Even so, he has allowed the Russian military and security services to do considerable mischief-making in Georgia, providing modern weapons and "volunteers" to rebels against President Eduard A. Shevardnadze's Government there and, after Mr. Shevardnadze was forced to crawl on his knees to Moscow for help, to him too.
They have also helped bring back a former Communist to power in Azerbaijan, given military support to Armenia in its battle with Azerbaijan over the Armenian enclave of Nagorno-Karabakh, and have refused to pledge complete withdrawal from Latvia and Estonia as long as ethnic Russians there suffer discrimination.
If all this looks to Americans like traditional imperial divide-and-conquer tactics, think how it looks to the citizens of Ukraine, Belarus, and the Eastern European democracies. Insecurity explains why Ukraine has delayed getting rid of the nuclear warheads it inherited. And it is the main reason why the Czechs, Poles and Hungarians want to join NATO as full members as quickly as possible. With it would come a precious guarantee of military aid if Russia should suddenly become a menace again, or fall apart into turmoil. But guarantees to get involved in war wherever it breaks out are risky indeed, and some countries feel that something less -- reliance on the 53-nation Conference on Security and Cooperation in Europe, for instance -- would be a better first step.
The Germans, for instance, know that Eastern Europeans worry not only about Russia but also about them. Chancellor Helmut Kohl and President Clinton also worry about making Russia, with its nuclear arsenal, feel threatened; NATO defense ministers (with France absent) agreed to adopt the Partnership for Peace proposal instead.
Mr. Yeltsin's reaction when Mr. Christopher explained it to him in Moscow was delight -- not about the Russian military being invited to play footsie with NATO military leaders, but about the fact that the Eastern Europeans wouldn't get into the alliance right away and bring the "West" almost to his doorstep.
The Russian military, for its part, showed in September that it still regards NATO more as a threat to its security than as a guarantor. After Mr. Yeltsin said in Warsaw that Russia had no objections to Poland and other countries joining NATO if they wanted, the military and the Foreign Ministry made him write to NATO leaders that extending NATO eastward could indeed be taken as a threat by Russia unless its right to an equal voice in the region's security was recognized.
The upshot was the Partnership for Peace proposal, which satisfied Western countries that didn't really know what else to come up with, but left the Eastern Europeans wanting more. "We don't want to be left in between the West and Russia again, the way we were for 40 years," one envoy in Moscow said.
 
Counting on Others
 The United States has so far resisted Mr. Yeltsin's calls for the West to recognize the Kremlin's right to play a peacekeeping role in the turbulent regions on Russia's southern edges, saying that the United Nations should do that. But so far it has left the job to the Russians.
Nor has NATO played such a role on its eastern doorstep, in Bosnia, even though the fighting there has generated instability and fear all over Eastern Europe, and has put legions of refugees on the road, creating a backlash in Germany, Denmark, and even Sweden. What good, many Europeans are beginning to ask, is an alliance that cannot deter this kind of conflict?
If NATO were simply a rich white men's club (Slavs need not apply), it would deserve to wither. And if it included the entire territory from San Francisco east to Vladivostok, it could become pointless.
All the same, a strategy to allay Europe's new security worries is needed. President Clinton and other leaders will be expected to come up with a sign that they recognize the need by the time they hold the NATO summit meeting here Jan. 10-11. Business as usual just won't work any more.

HEADLINE: Designers Turn New York Into a Fashion Big Top

BYLINE: By AMY M. SPINDLER

 BODY:
The New York fashion industry begins its week of runway shows today, but this time, along with the new clothes, there is a brand-new showcase.
For the first time, virtually the entire New York fashion establishment is presenting spring collections in big white tents in Bryant Park, behind the New York Public Library, under the auspices of the Council of Fashion Designers of America. A centralized approach, a longtime objective of Seventh Avenue, is standard in the two other fashion capitals, Paris and Milan. The hope is that slick, world-class presentations will attract more international media attention and more fashion buyers, spurring exports. 
 "Fashion is the largest manufacturing industry in New York and the largest employer" with 200,000 jobs, said Fern Mallis, executive director of the council. Wallace L. Ford 2d, the city's Commisisioner of Business Services, said a recent study for the Mayor's office put the economic impact of the industry at $22 billion a year.
Ms. Mallis added: "There are the fashion magazines, advertising, cosmetics, modeling agencies, shopping bag producers, trucking, hangers, thread makers, department stores, let alone the real estate business dependent on fashion retail. I shudder to think what would happen to the New York economy without Seventh Avenue."
The shudder along Seventh Avenue right now is one more akin to anticipation. Whenever a tent goes up, expectations seem to rise, too, as if the circus were coming to town. The fashion circus has been here all along; this is just the first time anyone has offered to wrap a tent, and a $1.5 million production, around it.
That figure represents contributions by corporate sponsors and the costs of designers for mounting this round of shows and the fall collections in April. The council held down costs for designers showing in the tents by soliciting the sponsors and standardizing production expenses.
Designers paid $24,000 to rent one tent for a rehearsal and a show, $13,500 for another tent and $10,000 for the Celeste Bartos Forum in the library. The $24,000 tent is known as the Gertrude, a reference to the statue of Gertrude Stein, and the $13,500 tent is the Josephine, after the fountain named for Josephine Shaw Lowell, a philanthropist. The statue and fountain are now inside the tents. Bids were taken for services, and the council bargained down models' fees, and the costs of lighting, sound systems, mirrors, chairs and rolling racks.
 
TV Brings Sponsors
 Television coverage of the flashy runway shows have made the productions, like sporting events, high-visibility opportunities for advertising tie-ins. This week, crews from CNN, CBS, NBC, the BBC, Comedy Central, VH-1 and MTV are expected.
Evian, Cadillac, Clairol, Godiva Liqueur, Pantone fabric color swatches, Prescriptives cosmetics, The New York Times, and Elle, Harper's Bazaar, Town & Country and Vogue magazines anted up more than $100,000 each to become sponsors. They are now entitled to tickets for some shows and to have their names listed on all promotional material for the event. This money went to the Seventh on Sixth Corporation, established by the council to defray costs of the shows.
The designer Donna Karan, who with Calvin Klein put her considerable clout behind the event early on, said that there has been increasing European interest in "reality based" American design. Ms. Karan's is the first offical show, with her lower-priced DKNY collection scheduled at 1 P.M. on Sunday; the day's schedule ends with Mr. Klein's CK collection, which is in the same price category. Both designers will show their higher-priced lines ($900 suits, say, versus $500 ones) on Friday, the final day of the showings.
"There's been some discussion of whether American designers should go to Paris to have people see what we do," Ms. Karan said. "But most people want to come here to see the collections, because they can feel New York, breathe it, and get the energy of the city."
 
Attending Was an Ordeal
 In the past, feeling and breathing New York has brought complaints from the foot soldiers of fashion week. The showings were in cramped Seventh Avenue showrooms, hotel ballrooms, SoHo lofts, night clubs, design-school auditoriums and other disparate locations, turning attendance into an ordeal. While a few designers will continue to use such places this week, most of the major players will appear in Bryant Park.
"It's really time that the American designers did it," Mr. Klein said. "We're all in this together and we're all going to gain. It's a win-win situation. The industry wins, New York wins, the city wins, the state wins. And the country wins, because the world will be looking at something we do very well."
Even if shows tie up traffic in the area a bit, cabdrivers still may want to add a little statue of their favorite Seventh Avenue designer to their dashboard icons. The Department of Transportation speeded up construction on the Avenue of the Americas near Bryant Park because of the fashion shows, and all four lanes are open.
"When we realized that Seventh on Sixth approached, we went into action to find out how construction could be rearranged and rescheduled to be out of their way so they could have a glorious celebration," said Lucius J. Riccio, the city's Commissioner of Transportation. "We've been working around the clock for the last month or so to try to expedite the job, not just for Seventh on Sixth, but for everybody."
 
A Chance to Ogle?
 Bryant Park has become a pastoral destination for the lunch-bag crowd, and not everyone will be happy to have paparazzi strolling by as they eat their ham sandwiches. But the patient park-bench spectator may get a glimpse of Todd Oldham, Isaac Mizrahi or Ralph Lauren, or of models and media stars like Cindy Crawford, Linda Evangelista, Tatjana Patitz and Christy Turlington. Or the crew of the director Robert Altman, researching the much-touted film on the fashion business, "Pret-a-Porter."
There was some unrest at lunchtime this week about the appropriation of what regulars proudly call "our" park.
"They put their runway over the fountain, and that's too much," said John Vasquez, an out-of-work architect who regularly eats lunch in the park with his 15-month-old daughter Jazzman. "And the fashion show isn't even open to the public. It's a public park, and if they're going to have a fashion show it should be public. I'm sure you can get into those shows if you have the right income bracket."
A New York University professor, Allan Kent Dart, who was pausing to smoke a cigarette on a lawn chair, said, "I suppose the controversy is, why should the fashion shows be allowed here when people who don't have a home are kicked out?"
 
Expansion Plans
 For the next round of showings, in April, the council hopes to placate these outsiders by broadcasting some shows on a large television screens outside the tents.
"We made sure 80 percent of the park was open to the public," said Dan Biederman, executive director of the Bryant Park Restoration Corporation, the private, nonprofit organization that runs the park. "A number of designers told us it's been such a boon in publicity that there are plans to possibly do a benefit for the public next time, making it inexpensive to see a sort of digest of the shows produced, and money going to the New York public parks."
Until the last model strolls off the runway Friday evening, New York designers will have a niggling feeling of disbelief that finally, after years of talk, they've bound the industry together.
"What I find significant is that it was the designers who were the catalysts for this," said Stan Herman, a designer who is the president of the council, made up of 185 designers. "Not the manufacturers, or the retailers or the mass merchants, or any of the people with the money. The designers have finally found a voice."

HEADLINE: Nuclear Era Is Departing By Same Door It Entered

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: AMARILLO, Tex., Oct. 29

 BODY:
Nuclear disarmament begins at a conference table in Moscow, Geneva or Washington. But it is carried out in places like a circular concrete cell buried in the dusty soil of the Texas Panhandle, where spheres of high explosives are pried from their cores of plutonium, rendering a bomb of almost unimaginable power into a collection of merely hazardous ingredients.
Dismantling nuclear warheads may be infinitely better than detonating them, but the job is tricky. In addition to being radioactive, plutonium is highly toxic and can burn on contact with air, and most of the explosives are old and prone to detonate with heat or shock. 
 The room is round so that if there is a nonnuclear explosion, the blast would be directed upward, where it would raise the roof, which is covered to a depth of 15 and a half feet with sand and gravel. Then the gravel would come back down and crash in, snuffing out the explosion, and incidentally, the 8 or 10 technicians in the room.
 
First Glimpse
 "It wouldn't matter to them," a plant official told a group of reporters on Thursday during a tour of two disassembly rooms at the Pantex plant 17 miles northeast of Amarillo. "They wouldn't be alive to notice."
Officials of the Department of Energy and its contractor here, Mason & Hanger-Silas Mason Company, refused to discuss a number of details about the work. But the department, perhaps anxious to show its competence in the new and critical task of dismantling 10,000 bombs and warheads in the next few years, offered people without security clearances the first glimpse of a long-classified world.
For example, in the disassembly cell, known as a "gravel gertie" for the tons of material above the workers' heads, engineers showed where they would wheel in the "physics package," or central explosives assembly, of a bomb, warhead or artillery shell. Rolled gently over a concrete floor with a nonskid coating, the assembly would be brought as close as possible to a waist-high metal table covered with a fabric cloth.
Removing the blanket of high explosives takes several hours to days, depending on the technology used when the weapon was assembled. A technician then lifts out the "pit," a sphere of plutonium about the size of a bowling ball, and puts it in a metal frame. A stainless steel jacket on the plutonium blocks some radiation. On the tour officials did not show a pit; in fact, the substitute they used was a bowling ball without holes.
Explosives attached to a core have never detonated at Pantex, although there have been various accidents at the plant over the years. Several workers were killed in the detonation of high explosives in which no plutonium was involved a few years ago, for example.
The Pantex plant, long known as the final assembly point for nuclear weapons, is perhaps better described as the disassembly point, at least for now. "We don't currently have any new production on line, but we have that responsibility," said Tom W. Walton, a spokesman.
The weapons displayed on the tour were the training versions without the plutonium core or high explosives and bearing data on weight, length and diameter, but not on the explosive power, or yield. The yields run from the equivalent of thousands of tons of TNT for artillery shells and small bombs dropped from airplanes, to the equivalent of millions of tons, for larger bombs and missiles.
With steady work and relatively good pay, the work force has low turnover, about 3 percent a year, and technicians say they get extensive training. Certainly there is something unusual about the physical settings, including revolving doors meant to contain blasts and windowless rooms where each wall is marked with a different color to help keep people properly oriented. But despite such constant reminders, the workers show little feeling about working on devices that can obliterate whole cities.
"If you get spooked, this is not the place to be," said Douglas Black, 60 years old and a 24-year veteran. His work is mostly mechanical, he said, but "it's not as greasy as working on your car."
Pantex, which stands for Panhandle of Texas, is a mixture of World War II-vintage buildings and modern, computerized systems, of cold war thinking and post-Soviet realignment. It covers 16,000 acres of the Texas plains, some of it a buffer zone defined by a few strands of barbed wire, and other parts defended by cascades of razor wire, huge infrared motion detectors and guards in desert camouflage driving armored Chevrolette Suburbans with holes cut in the roof and M-60 machine guns mounted on them.
Built early in World War II to handle conventional explosives and opened in 1951 as a nuclear weapons plant, Pantex is where plutonium was joined with the blankets of high explosives, detonators, timers, altimeters, parachutes, outer casings and other parts. Assembly is mostly nuts and bolts, but it is complicated, because a typical weapon can have 6,000 parts.
Pantex has been in the disassembly business for nearly as long as it has been in the assembly business, but mostly for servicing weapons or dismantling obsolete ones so some parts could be re-used.
Now amid its endless corridors, where technicians gently wheel bombs and warheads as if they were frail patients on hospital gurneys, Pantex is taking back about three dozen weapons a week, disassembling them, shredding some parts that are classified, sending others back to the weapons plants that made them, burning the high explosives and recovering the plutonium.
 
Russian Effort Is Uncertain
 Russian officials have given some indications that they, too, are dismantling weapons, but a report in September by the Office of Technology Assessment, a Congressional agency, said that information was "very limited, and sometimes conflicting or ambiguous."
"Although the Russians clearly possess the facilities for nuclear warhead dismantlement, it is not clear that their economy will be able to maintain the personnel, infrastructure, and financial resources required to operate these facilities," the report said.
Pantex has been storing plutonium in upright 30-gallon steel drums on the floors of 50-year-old concrete bunkers originally built for conventional explosives. But with more than 5,000 plutonium cores on hand and 10,000 more expected in the next few years, the plant will run out of space in the first three months of next year.
So with the help of the weapons-design laboratories, Pantex has devised a new system to stack barrels horizontally in the bunkers, in steel racks moved by a forklift shielded from radiation. Officials showed off a working model, minus the plutonium. It would allow storage of 672 cores in each bunker, which is a little bigger than a two-car garage; the current limit is 440. But the Energy Department is still reviewing the proposal.
One issue in dealing with plutonium is avoiding "criticality," or assembling enough material to sustain a chain reaction. Although this would produce a shower of heat and radiation, it would not produce a large blast because the plutonium would fly apart, unlike what happens in a weapon, where the high explosive contains the plutonium through several generations of chain reactions, producing a huge explosion. Pantex officials say, however, that becausethe placement of one pit per barrel surrounded by packing matter keeps the plutonium too far apart, a criticality is impossible.
Another issue is the possibility of a plane crash. Planes on instrument landings and takeoffs at Amarillo International Airport, which is nearby, fly over the plant. Ten yearsa ago the Energy Department asked the Federal Aviation Administration to order changes in flight patterns at the airport, but the agency refused. The Department of Energy believes that the probability of a crash in the area of the bunkers is slightly greater than one in a million per year, but the chance of a crash by a plane large enough to cause a release of plutonium is less than one in a million per year. The Energy Department is now considering approving the new storage plan without a change of flight pattern.
The new storage approach is described as an interim solution for plutonium, but there is no longer-term plan. The department is unwilling to accept the idea that the plutonium is now waste, too, but even if it did, it has no place for permanent disposal.
Pantex is even having trouble getting rid of the conventional explosives. For years it has burned them in the open, even though some are contaminated with radioactive material and chemical toxins. Now the neighbors are objecting.
A spokesman for the plant said Pantex had recently stopped burning explosives contaminated with radioactive material while it looked for another disposal method.
Other materials coming out of the weapons include hazards like asbestos and PCB's. And then there is gold used in electrical connections, which by some estimates is worth up to $50,000 per weapon.
But that is a pittance when contrasted with the cost of taking a weapon apart. Plant officials could not say what disassembly costs, pointing out that there are large fixed costs for security and other overhead items that are not dependent on the volume of work performed by about 300 technicians. For the fiscal year 1994 the budget runs to $210 million, plus $45 million for environmental restoration and waste management and another $40 million in capital improvements.
But the Office of Technology Assessment recently calculated that the Energy Department's total spending for dismantlement, here and at other sites to which Pantex sends components, runs from $500 million to $1 billion a year. That would put the cost per weapon at $350,000 to $700,000.
