HEADLINE: 10% of Fruit Juice Sold in U.S. Is Not All Juice, Regulators Say

BYLINE: By DIANA B. HENRIQUES

 BODY:
The clandestine production and sale of adulterated fruit juice is a widespread and highly profitable practice, a review of court cases filed across the country shows -- a practice that is costing American consumers an estimated $1.2 billion a year and exposing them to undisclosed and unapproved chemicals.
Regulators at the Food and Drug Administration, which oversees the food industry, had hoped that tainted juice would become less common after the Federal prosecution in 1987 of Beech-Nut Nutrition Corporation. Beech-Nut paid a $2 million fine for illegally selling "apple juice" that was nothing more than sweetened water and chemicals. But the recent cases all involved activities that continued long after that landmark case.
 
Enforcement Still Haphazard
 And while Federal officials say they are committed to pursuing adulteration cases, court exhibits and interviews with investigators and juice industry executives show that enforcement efforts are still haphazard, plagued by inadequate resources and an institutional tradition that has put a low priority on cases that, at least until recently, were not considered a threat to public safety. 
 David A. Kessler, commissioner of the F.D.A., said on Friday that his agency was determined to prosecute juice adulteration cases when it could muster evidence to support a criminal case. "And these are serious prosecutions," Dr. Kessler said. "People are going to jail."
But he conceded that despite past efforts, the juice industry remained a troubling exception to the agency's generally successful efforts to combat food adulteration. "We still have to be vigilant," he said. "In most cases of adulteration, it turns out to be just economic and nobody gets hurt -- but there is always that potential."
Chemists who specialize in testing food samples estimate that about 10 percent of the country's $12 billion pure fruit juice industry, about half of which is made up of orange juice, is adulterated in some way. The most common adulterants are sugar or watery orange byproducts, but in some recent cases, manufacturers have used preservatives not approved as safe for use in juice.
Many manufacturers cited in court cases since 1987, while not nationally known names like Beech-Nut, were important wholesalers to food producers like Borden and Land O'Lakes, and grocery chains like Kroger, Safeway Stores and Alpha-Beta markets. There have been no Federal claims that these retailers knew they were selling adulterated juice.
The recent defendants include:
*Peninsular Products of Warren, Mich., Flavor Fresh Food Corporation of Chicago, and eight individuals, who pleaded guilty after being indicted in February on Federal charges of conducting an eight-year conspiracy to add sugar and illegal preservatives to more than 32 million gallons of citrus juices sold under various private labels in 26 states. Court records show that the juice contained unapproved preservatives.
*Paramount Citrus Association of Mission Valley, Calif., a unit of the private investment partnership that also owns Teleflora Inc., the flower delivery service, and the Franklin Mint, the collectibles retailer. In April, Paramount and its plant manager pleaded guilty in Philadelphia to selling adulterated juice to the Federal Government since 1985. The defendants also paid nearly $7 million to settle civil claims by the Government.
*Seven executives of Sun Up Foods Inc. of Benton, Ky., which before its bankruptcy in September 1990 claimed to be one of the country's largest juice wholesalers. The defendants, who have denied the Government's allegations and are scheduled to go to trial in April, were accused of adding liquid sugar to their juice with equipment hidden in a secret room in their factory.
 
$2 Million in Settlements
 Meanwhile, Everfresh Juice Company, -- which from 1987 until 1992 was owned by the Canadian brewing giant John Labatt Ltd., the owner of the Toronto Blue Jays baseball team -- and the American Citrus Products Corporation, doing business as Home Juice in Melrose Park, Ill., have paid more than $2 million to settle lawsuits in Chicago accusing them of using sugar and illegal preservatives in their juices for more than a decade. The cases, filed in 1990 under a secrecy order, were settled in April.
The terms of the settlement are still being contested in court, said James Emmerton, the general counsel for Labatt, "so I cannot comment." He added, "In general, we have a policy that we comply with the law and try to enforce those policies throughout all our operations."
Steven M. Kowal, the attorney for American Citrus, said the company had instituted a program of independent testing for its juice. "Beyond that, I cannot comment because of the litigation," he said.
 
Cutting Corners
 Kristen Chadwell, who until July was the general counsel for the Florida Department of Citrus, said unscrupulous manufacturers had long cut corners by diluting juice with cheap but generally harmless ingredients like beet sugar and pulp wash, the watery residue obtained by soaking squeezed oranges in water and squeezing them a second time.
But diluted, sweetened juice is more prone to spoilage, and Ms. Chadwell said the industry's search for less detectible chemical preservatives may be outstripping the regulatory efforts to protect consumers. "These are complicated, white-collar crimes," Ms. Chadwell said, "and the potential for profit is enormous. So the analytical methods involved are on the cutting edge."
That cutting edge slices past such safe but easily detectable preservatives as sorbate and sodium benzoate, which can be used legally if they are listed on the labels, to focus on less detectable preservatives such as natamycin.
 
A Secret Solution
 Indeed, executives at Peninsular Products did not know exactly what they were adding to their juice when they were using an imported German preservative whose ingredients were kept secret by the supplier, said Assistant United States Attorney Brian K. Delaney, a prosecutor of that case.
In sworn statements filed in the Chicago litigation against Everfresh, some witnesses testified that they suspected the secret solution contained diethyl pyrocarbonate, or D.E.P.C., banned as hazardous by the F.D.A. in 1972. But that speculation has not been substantiated by laboratory tests.
Efforts by the F.D.A. to find and analyze samples of the compound have been unsuccessful. But Federal investigators in the Michigan case found a 1987 formula that listed the solution's active ingredients as natamycin and two enzymes called glucose oxidase and catalase. While not as dangerous as D.E.P.C., none is approved by the F.D.A. as safe for use in beverages.
 
Consultant Pleads Guilty
 According to documents filed in Federal District Court in Grand Rapids, Mich., last week, several defendants and other witnesses in the Michigan case have told prosecutors under oath that similar imported preservatives had been purchased in the past by Home Juice and Everfresh Juice, the defendants in the Chicago civil suits.
One of the Peninsular defendants is Friedrich R. Kohlbach, a juice industry consultant accused of supplying the illegal preservatives to Peninsular and Flavor Fresh. In early September, Mr. Kohlbach pleaded guilty to a single felony violation of Federal food purity laws. In a report prepared for his sentencing, which will take place in November, Federal prosecutors said he had also sold the preservatives to other companies.
The Government lawyers described the preservative Mr. Kohlbach supplied to Peninsular and Flavor Fresh as "a milky, white liquid" that "arrived in blue, 5-gallon jerry cans" bearing the following warning in German: "Contains dangerous goods of the highest classification."
That description exactly matches the one provided in November 1988 to the F.D.A.'s Detroit office by Duane Bosch, a former quality-control employee of Everfresh in Detroit. According to the employee's sworn affidavit, which is filed in the Seventh Circuit Court of Appeals in Chicago, Mr. Bosch gave samples of the fluid to the F.D.A., which tested them months later with inconclusive results.
On Jan. 11, 1989, Mr. Bosch sued Everfresh under Michigan's whistle-blower law, claiming he was fired for questioning the use of the mysterious preservative; the case was settled out of court nine months later, according to the affidavit.
A lawyer for Labatt, in a sworn affidavit, said his firm was called on Feb. 7, 1989 by in-house lawyers for Labatt, who said they had learned that "unlabeled ingredients" had been added to the company's orange juice. After an internal investigation, Labatt executives met with F.D.A. officials in May and June of 1989.
At the June meeting, according to an F.D.A. memorandum, the Labatt representatives reported that "at some unspecified point, they realized that Everfresh's orange juice was adulterated. This realization was apparently very recent and long after their purchase of Everfresh." Apparently, the F.D.A. accepted the company's assurances that the problem had been solved.
But court records in Chicago show that before Labatt purchased Everfresh Juice in late 1986, Everfresh had been sued six times for allegedly adulterating its products. One such case, by Purity Products of Baltimore, Md., was pending at the time of the sale and was settled out of court eight months later. A similar case, also by Purity Products, was filed in early 1988 against Holiday Juice, a subsidiary of Labatt until 1992, and was settled about six months later.
While Justice Department officials clearly hope that the recent prosecutions will be the curative that the Beech-Nut case was supposed to be, officials at both the F.D.A. and the Justice Department acknowledge privately that some cases still are not being pursued aggressively enough.
Some juice industry executives and advisers blame an overworked staff at the F.D.A., which must meet its vast mandate on a budget smaller than those of many big hospitals. Dr. Kessler, the head of the agency, said his agency lacked the statutory power to issue subpoenas or demand access to industry records to trace adulterated food, hampering its enforcement efforts.
But James Mundo, an outspoken F.D.A. juice industry investigator in Saginaw, Mich., said there had been occasions when good F.D.A. cases were dropped because Federal prosecutors in some jurisdictions were unwilling to take the cases to court.
And without meaningful punishment and stiff fines, the profit motive will always encourage cheating, said Alan Brause, the chief chemist and president of Analytical Chemical Services of Columbia, Md., a laboratory frequently used in Government investigations. "The money to be made is just stupendous," he said. "Sugar costs 30 cents a pound, and real juice solids costs between $1 and $2.50 a pound," he added. "That's the temptation right there."

HEADLINE: AMERICAN AIRLINES IS FACING WALKOUT

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: ARLINGTON, Tex., Oct. 30

 BODY:
The flight attendants' union broke off contract talks with American Airlines today after all-night negotiations and called for a nationwide boycott against the company.
"We are free to strike or to engage in other activities that would have an economic impact," said a union spokesman, Ray Abernathy, after a negotiating session of 25 1/2 hours with a mediator. "We have said we will strike on or before Nov. 22." 
 The contract covering the flight attendants did not expire but was opened for amendment on Dec. 31. A year of contract talks between the airline and the Association of Professional Flight Attendants came down to the exhaustive bargaining session because a 30-day "cooling-off" period set by the National Mediation Board expired at midnight Eastern time.
 
Planning Boycott
 The airline, based in Fort Worth, and the union had agreed to an indefinite extension, but talks ended about 9:30 this morning, Mr. Abernathy said.
"The nutshell is that the company was unwilling to compromise on concession demands for total control over staffing, cuts in vacation benefits, some radical changes in work rules and new employee contributions to pre-fund retiree health benefits," he said.
"We are going to begin our action immediately by launching a nationwide boycott of American Airlines to start drying up their business before we go on strike," he said.
A spokesman for the airline, Tim Smith, said: "We're just extremely disappointed, heartbroken even, that talks with the A.P.F.A. have broken off. We're studying our options.
"As of this moment, our employees are reporting, and operations continue on a normal schedule."
The union's 21,000 members had threatened to strike on or before Thanksgiving week, one of the year's busiest travel times.
Mr. Abernathy said the two sides were never close to settling.
"The company's final payoff was especially insulting," he said. "They offered us a raise of 7.5 percent over the life of the contract, along with a 4 percent signing bonus. That's not a pay raise. That's a bribe. It doesn't get added onto the scale. It's just a one-time bonus."
Mr. Smith said the airline had offered a contract with increases averaging 33 percent, or more than 7 percent a year for the average flight attendant.

HEADLINE: Glittering New Capital Belies Turmoil in Nigeria

BYLINE: By KENNETH B. NOBLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ABUJA, Nigeria

 BODY:
Amid a wooded plain and soaring granite hills, a new city is rising here, its splendor and vast scale in stark contrast to the pervasive rot and decline of much of Africa.
This is the site of Nigeria's new capital, a reflection of the country's sense of itself as black Africa's leader, and at the same time a symbol of a nation hoping to be one of the world's great powers in the next century. 
 Although hospitals in Nigeria are chronically short of medicine, schools are often closed because there is no money to pay teachers and many of the country's 90 million people are dirt poor, this glittering new settlement, still under construction, is being built at a reported cost of $10 billion. There has been no official public accounting of how much has been spent or exactly where the money has gone.
The original plan called for a national government modeled after the United States, with a two-chamber federal assembly, a supreme court and an elected civilian president. It was all to have been functioning smoothly by the early 1990's.
 
The Buildings Are Vacant
 But while the buildings to house those officials are finished, they either stand vacant or the officials working there are idle -- or, as in the case of the federal court officials, largely ignored and likely to remain so for the indefinite future.
The Nigerian Government has thrown a grand costume party, with drinks, food and entertainment, but the invited guests are stuck in a squabble at the front door.
Abuja illustrates what critics say is a dangerous vacuum of leadership, a growing absence of direction and common purpose that is threatening to tear the country apart.
"There is no real common denominator anymore, no real leadership at the top," said Nosa Igiebor, editor-in-chief of Tell, a weekly news magazine. "The country is sinking into a political void. We are on the verge of disintegrating."
The most apparent cause of Nigeria's latest political crisis is the decision by its former military leader, Gen. Ibrahim Babangida, to annul the multiparty presidential elections held last June 12.
But there are deeper causes: decades of mediocre government, pervasive corruption, a military leadership stubbornly clinging to power and ethnic and religious rivalries that have kept this country from establishing a social consensus more than 30 years after independence from Britain in 1960.
 
Two Rival Centers of Power
 Since September two rival power centers have claimed to rule this increasingly fragmented republic: an interim Government under the command of Ernest Shonekan, which was created by General Babangida, and a democratically elected one headed by Moshood Abiola, a wealthy industrialist who is widely believed to have won the elections in June.
The real power lies with the military. Soldiers have run the country for all but nine years since independence. And the armed forces appear in no hurry to give up the power to which they have become accustomed.
Until the leadership crisis is resolved, Abuja will probably remain an empty shell, a monument to Nigeria's hugely unfulfilled ambitions.
What is being lost, moreover, is the elaborate fiction that provided Nigerians with the idea that they were somehow different from the other countries that make up Africa; that this country, rich in oil, was not only more populous and wealthier than the others, but also that Nigerians were creating a thriving oasis of capitalist democracy that others would follow and emulate.
"Nigeria's dream is to become another America, to be respected and feared as Africa's unchallenged superpower," a Western diplomat said the other day. "But the truth is that is no different from the rest of the continent. It's an unworkable mess."
Gen. Olusegun Obansajo, a former head of state, said many Nigerians "feel a sense of shame, a sense of embarrassment about what has happened to our country. This is now what we have fought so long and hard for."
The impasse is all the more alarming because Nigeria has a history of violent upheaval. At least 500,000, and perhaps as many as one million people, are said to have died, mostly through starvation, in the Biafran secessionist war in the late 1960's. And thousands of people die each year in ethnic or religious-based conflicts.
Since overthrowing the authoritarian regime of Maj. Gen. Muhammed Buhari in 1985, Nigeria's military leadership has been trying to lay the foundations for a Western-style multiparty democracy that would have guarantees against a reversion to the corrupt and dictatorial rule that has existed in much of the continent.
But under General Babangida, the "transition," as this elusive blueprint for the future is called, seems to have lost its way. Leftists and human rights advocates complain about the belt tightening and unemployment caused by General Babangida's economic policies, and a relatively small but influential faction of the 80,000-strong armed forces grumbles that he is going to far in opening Nigeria's politics.
The economy has virtually collapsed. The inflation rate has soared from 13 percent in 1991 to more than 50 percent today. Compounding the sense of crisis are periodic fuel shortages, a shock in a country whose economy is based on petroleum production. The main cause for the shortages, Government officials say, is a surge in smuggling to other countries. Others blame corrupt, incompetent Government bureaucrats who, they say, have allowed supplies of refined petroleum to fall to perilously low levels. Oil accounts for more than 95 percent of export earnings.
 
Unquestionable Potential
 The future is not entirely bleak. With one of the largest gross national products in Africa and a population that includes one in four black Africans, Nigeria unquestionably has exceptional potential. Moreover, efforts to privitize and decentralize the economy in the late 1980's, while piecemeal, did contribute to a burst of growth, and the country has a considerable number of highly educated workers.
And despite recent crackdowns, Nigeria's press remains boisterously critical of both the Government and the opposition and is intent on discussing the issues that, for now, are paralyzing the nation.
But opposition leaders and political commentators say that the prospects Nigeria will recover its political equilibrium soon are bleak.
Ray Ekpu, editor of Newswatch, a weekly magazine, wrote recently that Nigerians "seem to have an obsessive sense of something, perhaps everything, going wrong. We wish we could wake up, scrub our eyes and tell ourselves it was a dream, for a dream would be a lot easier to deal with."
"But this is reality," he added, and "we are rapidly careering into a frightening state of ungovernability."
The current crisis began after General Babangida annulled last June's elections, saying there was evidence of fraud and vote-buying. Then, over the protests of the presumed winner, Mr. Abiola, the General promised to organize yet another election in late July.
Faced with an international outcry and growing domestic protests, General Babangida, along with the mostly hand-picked leaders of the Nigeria's two legal political parties, settled upon a compromise. The idea of holding new elections immediately was scrapped, and the General agreed to leave office and appoint an interim Government that would organize new elections and hand over to an elected President early next year. But Mr. Abiola, the Social Democratic party's candidate, and Bashir Tofa of the National Republican Convention were effectively barred from holding office.
The interim Government has proven unpopular, and its leader, Mr. Shonekan, has been widely ridiculed as a puppet for an unseen but powerful coterie of military leaders who are now the dominant force in the country.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Demonstrators, left, running through the streets of Lagos, Nigeria, in July in protests against the annulment of the presidential election held in June. Moshood Abiola, above, is believed to have won the vote. Although Nigeria has spent billions to create a new capital, its glitter contrasts with misery and turmoil elsewhere. HEADLINE: HAITIAN MILITARY CONDEMNED BY U.N.

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Oct 30

 BODY:
Still hoping that a solution can be brokered in Haiti, the Security Council condemned the Haitian military today for blocking the return of President Jean-Bertrand Aristide but stopped short of even repeating its warnings of a tighter trade embargo.
The statement was issued by the Security Council President, Ronaldo Mota Sardenberg of Brazil, on the day originally set for the exiled President to be restored to office under an agreement signed in July. 
 Diplomats here said the United Nations mediator in Haiti, Dante Caputo, was still appealing for talks with the Haitian military in the coming week to resolve the standoff. But there was no response from the army in Port-au-Prince, the Haitian capital.
In its statement, the Security Council condemned the military for failing to observe its obligations under an accord it signed in New York in July calling for Father Aristide's restoration to power by Oct. 30 and for the resignation of Lieut. Gen. Raoul Cedras, the head of the Haitian armed forces.
 
Oil and Arms Banned
 The Council hinted pointedly that the military still had time to make good on its pledges, reaffirming that the agreement remains "fully in force as the only valid framework for the solution of the crisis in Haiti."
The current United Nations embargo against Haiti bans shipments of oil and arms. Content to bide its time in case a settlement can be mediated, the Council did not repeat recent warnings by diplomats that the embargo may be extended to all commercial goods.
"Let's see what comes out of these talks," Mr. Sardenberg said.
Even the measure condemning the military was held up for 24 hours by China as it sought to extract an apology of sorts from President Aristide, who infuriated Beijing by calling for Taiwan's admission to the United Nations when he addressed the General Assembly last week.
Today Father Aristide in effect capitulated to that demand. In a letter to the entire United Nations membership, his Government said that President Aristide had assured China's representative at a meeting on Friday that his country wanted good relations with China and that those ties should be governed by "relevant" United Nations resolutions. The United Nations voted in 1971 to expel Taiwan, which had previously occupied China's seat.

HEADLINE: Amid Strife, Haiti Parliament Struggles With Itself

BYLINE: By GARRY PIERRE-PIERRE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Senator Julio Larosiliere the most senior member of the Haitian legislature, says the return of democratic rule in the country rests in the hands of its exiled President's allies in Parliament.
Like many others in the parliamentary bloc that for two years has resisted attempts to restore the elected President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, Mr. Larosiliere said Father Aristide's legislative supporters lacked the courage of their convictions. 
 Mr. Larosiliere said that instead of turning out last week to pass legislation that diplomats said could have paved the way for Father Aristide's return from exile, deputies in the constitutional bloc, which supports Father Aristide, stayed in hiding or exile, preventing the quorum needed for a vote.
"Therein lies the big problem," said Mr. Larosiliere. "Instead of trusting our initiative, they are creating problems."
 
Legislators Silent
 Although Mr. Larosiliere is a member of the alliance bloc of legislators, which opposes Father Aristide, he is the architect of an 11-point plan to restore him to power and end the international embargo against Haiti.
But for Patrick Norzeus, a deputy in the constitutional bloc who has continued to support Father Aristide openly, despite a spate of political killings targeting people like himself, it is Mr. Larosiliere who is not working in good faith but is instead using his seeming support for Father Aristide's return as a ruse to delay the restoration of democracy.
On Wednesday, as the lower house failed to reach a quorum for a second day, journalists covering the session from the gallery of the chamber, saw assembled outside many alliance block deputies who declined to respond when their names were called. If they had answered, it would have given the lower house the quorum it needed to enact legislation that the military insists must be passed before Father Aristide returns.
The alliance "has always found a way to block any real push to bring Aristide back," Mr. Norzeus said. "It is the game that they play."
The contradictory views in the Haitian Parliament underscore the depths of the problems in the struggling institution as it tries to acquire some level of legitimacy in the country's political life after decades of being nothing more than a rubber-stamp body.
Among members of the alliance, which has backed a role for the opposition party in the new government, the issues appear to be more about power, privilege and jobs for allies than political ideology.
At the crux of the plan to bring Father Aristide back is an amnesty for military officers involved in the September 1991 coup that drove him into exile. Under terms of an agreement signed by Father Aristide and the country's military leader, Lieut. Gen. Raoul Cedras, in New York in July, Father Aristide agreed to such an amnesty. But the military leaders want the Parliament to enact legislation guaranteeing it before they resign and allow Father Aristide back.
Facing the threat of a complete economic isolation by the international community, the Haitian lawmakers last week drafted the 11-point proposal that they hoped would be acceptable to Father Aristide and Gen. Cedras.
Mr. Larosiliere and members of his coalition are widely believed to have support among the army's high command, but he is trying to paint himself as a moderate in a sea of hard-liners, at great personal risk to himself.
"I am not isolated," Mr. Larosiliere said. "It's simply we find that the country is going down the tubes without Aristide and we want to do something about it."
Mr. Larosiliere, elected to represent Cavaillon in the Chamber of Deputies in 1984 and now one of two senators from the southern department, is a member of a landed family in the Les Cayes area. He spent three years in New York in the 1970's studying business administration and political science. When he returned to Haiti he became a coffee speculator and shortly afterwards went into politics.
He said that before taking up any measures he consulted with the military high command "to get a better understanding of the situation."
"True there are some among us who have friends in the military just like there are others" who have friends in the military, he said.
Beyond the ideological differences between the two opposing blocs, many Haitians cite the maneuvers like the quorum ploy as the kind of behavior that prevents Haiti's Parliament from becoming a responsible political entity.
While the alliance, which has a majority in the lower house, blames Father Aristide's party for failing to make a quorum in the chamber of deputies, Mr. Norzeus points out that the alliance has had no problem rounding up enough legislators to pass business it wants approved.
Mr. Norzeus has been one of the few supporters of Father Aristide to attend Parliament recently and has spoken out openly against the attack on the exiled President's party. "I went because the country is facing a difficult situation," said Mr. Norzeus, a soft-spoken man who is under the protection of a foreign embassy. "I am a political man and as such I have to take my responsibilities."
Mr. Norzeus, a 30-year-old lawyer who represents the southern region of Les Baraderes, has been waging a campaign to shed light on the seriousness of the threat of violence that Father Aristide's supporters face.
He met Father Aristide in 1987 and has been a close friend of his ever since. An organizer of grass-roots organizations, he was elected for the first time in 1990 in what is regarded by many as the only democratic election in the country's history.
 
Declining Ranks
 In principle there are 27 Haitian senators and 79 members of the Chamber of Deputies. But because of deaths, death threats and a bogus election in January there are now only 17 senators and 61 deputies. The Senate is regarded as narrowly in favor of Father Aristide; the Chambers of Deputies is controlled by his opponents.
In the country's struggling democracy, the Parliament is relatively young. Francois "Papa Doc" Duvalier, dissolved it a few years after he took office in 1957, when he declared himself President for Life, and the Parliament only resumed any useful function after the departure of his son, Jean-Claude Duvalier, in 1986.
Mr. Lasrosiliere, who describes himself as an independent, said that 29 years of rule by the Duvaliers, characterized by social and economic injustices, paved the way for a radical leader like Father Aristide and that after a failed experiment to the left the president should be more of a moderate.
During the eight months Father Aristide served in office before he was overthrown, the Parliament tried to flex its muscles only once, by summoning the Prime Minister. Some members were intimidated by mobs, and others were beaten. More recently, guns are drawn almost as often as words are exchanged.
Parliament is "not a Haitian invention," Mr. Larosiliere said. "It's human nature when you live with a brother he is the first one you fight with."

HEADLINE: Train to the Plane: Will It Ever Fly?

BYLINE: By LISA W. FODERARO

 BODY:
When the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey first proposed a light rail system that would finally link New York City's airports to Manhattan by sleek, quiet electric trains, people cheered. But now that the plans are nearing completion -- with a proposal to put the Manhattan terminal in one of the worst traffic spots in the city -- rumblings of opposition are surfacing.
Just about everyone recognizes the need for better access to Kennedy International and La Guardia Airports, where congested highways are the principal transportation connection to the rest of the city. 
 But the proposed location of the Manhattan terminal, on East 59th Street near Third Avenue, has made many people queasy. The neighborhood is already a nightmare for motorists, with traffic from the Queensboro Bridge flowing into an area teeming with office workers and shoppers on the edge of the residential Upper East Side.
Accompanying that fear of congestion is a rising chorus of support for extending the rail line to Grand Central Terminal, even if it means spending more money on the project and taking longer to build it. Grand Central is a more convenient location, the argument goes, and can better handle hordes of new commuters.
 
A 100-Year Plan
 "If this system has to last for 100 years, what's another $500 million?" said Brenda Levin, a member of the New York City Planning Commission, referring to the additional amount that Port Authority officials say it would cost to run the train all the way to Grand Central.
The Port Authority has held 122 community meetings to explain the $2 billion undertaking, which is scheduled to be completed in 2003 and is one of the largest public works projects in a half-century. Officials painstakingly defend what they say is the internal logic of the proposed route that wends 20 miles from Kennedy Airport to 59th Street almost entirely along public rights of way.
The present proposal enjoys major political backing from Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, Mayor David N. Dinkins and others. But three community boards whose districts touch on East 59th Street, as well as the Manhattan Borough President, Ruth W. Messinger, have also voiced concern about the proposed terminal site.
Originally, the Port Authority was going to end the rail line in Long Island City, Queens, but critics immediately dubbed any system that stopped short of Manhattan a road to nowhere.
Last year, when the Port Authority realized it could use the lower outer roadways on the Queensboro Bridge for the railway, planners began examining sites on 59th Street for the terminal. They seem to be zeroing in on the old Alexander's department store on Third Avenue between 58th and 59th Streets, which is empty. But two other sites east of Third Avenue are under consideration.
A Third Avenue terminal could neatly connect the rail line underground with the the Nos. 4, 5 and 6 and the N and R subway lines, and would make it more accessible to workers in midtown, officials say.
While studies indicate that taxi traffic in the terminal area would grow by 410 vehicles from 4 to 5 P.M. alone each day, the Port Authority has proposed steps to mitigate traffic. Running a jitney from major hotels to the terminal, rerouting traffic onto the Franklin D. Roosevelt Drive and creating two new lanes that would bisect the terminal building's ground floor for taxi use would reduce congestion, said Edward J. O'Sullivan, director of the Port Authority's airport access program.
In addition, the Port Authority estimates that there would be 4,300 fewer vehicles on the streets of Manhattan each day as a result of a new airport rail system, 760 fewer from 4 to 5 P.M. alone.
 
Money's Not Everything
 Even if there were additional money above the airport user fees dedicated to the project to pay for a Grand Central link, the Port Authority would not automatically shift gears. Studies show that there are the same number of potential airport commuters within a mile of each site.
Extending the line from 59th Street to Grand Central would also require condemning property, Mr. O'Sullivan said, and likely entail tunneling through schist under Madison Avenue and ripping up the road to relocate utility lines and pipes. A terminal on East 59th Street, however, would be relatively simple to construct and could be achieved without condemnations.
And while advocates of a Grand Central link note that it would allow easy access to Metro-North commuter lines, focus groups have indicated that suburban passengers would continue to drive to the airports regardless of where the terminal was situated. "Psychologically, people think of Grand Central as a major transportation hub, which it is, but it's not necessarily where the airport passengers are coming to and from," Mr. O'Sullivan said.
Once environmental impact studies are finished, the Federal Aviation Administration ultimately has to sign off on the project, which would be financed by a $3 fee that has been collected since last summer on departing passengers at Kennedy, La Guardia and Newark airports. If approved, the rail system could be under construction by 1996.
 
A Sure Thing: Complaints
 As the rail link moves from concept to concrete plan, opposition is certain to mount. "Motorists are going to complain like crazy about losing capacity on the Queensboro Bridge, and there'll be opposition from residents over traffic congestion," said Jeffrey M. Zupan, of the Regional Plan Association, an independent research group.
Mr. O'Sullivan calls the 59th Street terminal location the "$64 question" and says that if it can't be resolved, the train won't come to Manhattan. That makes some critics wonder just how open the process is.
"We hear a lot about how alternatives are going to be considered in the environmental impact statement," said Jess A. Velona, chairman of the airport access subcommittee of Community Board Six "But all the presentations focus on a single route and terminus."
And Ms. Messinger, the Borough President, asserted this week that East 59th Street "was not a part of town that could easily adapt to 400 to 600 taxis an hour" and predicted "severe economic impacts."
But none of the criticism has yet to faze Stanley Brezenoff, who as head of the Port Authority, considers the airport link crucial to the city's welfare. "This is the one megaproject that perhaps can be built," he said, "and it's not unreasonable that people press us on some of these critical issues."

HEADLINE: THE 1993 CAMPAIGN: Campaign Watch;
Dinkins Draws a Crowd; Giuliani Plays Solitaire

BYLINE: By DEBORAH SONTAG

 BODY:
In the final campaign week as seen on television, Mayor David N. Dinkins wrapped himself in the cloak of his incumbency, buoyed by an all-star cast of Hollywood and Washington celebrities. Rudolph W. Giuliani, in contrast, played the Lone Ranger, poised to avenge four years of municipal evil with no big guns by his side, no fancy munitions at all beyond faith, family and a handful of turncoat Democrats.
As Mayor Dinkins leaned into the televised embrace of Barbra Streisand, the Rev. Jesse Jackson and Tipper Gore, his Republican-Liberal challenger appeared proudly on the evening news beside his latest supporter, Andrew S. Fisher of the Brooklyn Economic Development Corporation. As Mr. Dinkins clasped hands with President Clinton ("a friend of mine from Hope, Arkansas"), Mr. Giuliani stood alone at a podium before four questioners on WCBS-TV in a hand of political solitaire that was supposed to have been a debate. 
 The Mayor aired aggressive new commercials that sought to portray his opponent as hot-headed and cold-hearted. Mr. Giuliani introduced a warmer side of himself in ads and interviews, crinkling his eyes and laughing frequently in what seemed to be either the giddiness of genuine confidence or the last-minute determination to lift his mask of stiffness.
 
Increased Visibility
 After a month of spotty coverage, this was the week to flip the dial and find the candidates everywhere, talking back-to-back if never face-to-face. Still, on Thursday, a peak news day, when Mr. Clinton came to town and Mr. Giuliani participated in his one-man debate, the mayoral campaign did not even lead the 11 P.M. newscasts -- losing out to an abducted child struck by a train and to the raging fires in Southern California.
Despite professing their desire to keep above the fray, the candidates, yet again, performed for the cameras with a take-this, take-that childishness. "Frankly, I don't think he likes New York City very much," Mr. Dinkins said in a WABC-TV interview, the campaign equivalent of insulting his opponent's mother. Mr. Giuliani, however, had spoken with exuberant hyperbole about the city in a New York 1 News interview Wednesday: "To the extent that human civilization in 1993 has a central place, that's New York City," he said.
When Mr. Dinkins explained the city's loss of jobs as the legacy of 12 years of Republican rule, it was Mr. Giuliani's turn to be petulant. "That's the kind of time warp he's in," he said of the Mayor on WNBC-TV. "He probably thinks that Ronald Reagan is still the President."
As the incumbent, Mr. Dinkins was able to use television to greater advantage than his opponent. He could make news simply by fulfilling the duties of his office, as when he rushed to Jamaica Hospital on Wednesday to check on police officers who had been shot. He could surround himself with the trappings of his position, granting breakfast interviews on the porch of Gracie Mansion.
Mr. Giuliani, in contrast, was always portrayed in television images as a candidate, shaking hands at Queens diners, donning a yarmulke at Brooklyn synagogues, dancing with elderly women at senior centers in the Bronx. In interviews, Mr. Dinkins sought to cast the pure candidate's role as something crass, as self-interest rather than political purpose. Talking to Gabe Pressman on WNBC-TV, Mr. Dinkins said that his own economic plans were "a product of more than a year's work" rather than "dreamed up by three people in a back room at election time."
 
'Not Managing the City'
 But incumbency is a double-edged sword. And Mr. Giuliani has used it to jab the Mayor again and again. "He's just not managing the city," the challenger said often. "I'm going to have to clean up the mess he's made."
For weeks the Giuliani campaign has used surrogates to sell the candidate on television, from his wife and his mother to a taxi driver, deli owner and butcher. But this week, the candidate moved front and center, both in the first commercial to feature him talking directly to the camera and in innumerable interviews.
He took particular advantage of the one hour of prime-time television given him by WCBS-TV when Mr. Dinkins refused to participate in a one-on-one debate, managing to massage all the sound bites of his campaign into a fluid, coherent presentation.
The adrenaline of the final stretch that pumped Mr. Giuliani into higher spirits than previously seen proved puzzling to some reporters. WNBC-TV's Ti-Hua Chang, for one, could not help but comment on his peculiar ebullience. "Rudolph Giuliani," he noted, "seems tickled pink to be behind by 9 points" in the News 4/Daily News poll.
But that giddy good humor was fading by week's end when Mr. Dinkins launched a harsh commercial that included a clip of Mr. Giuliani's using an expletive in addressing a rowdy police rally. For the next few days, Mr. Giuliani promised, he would give as good as he got. And television is sure to be a principal arena for the final rounds.

HEADLINE: THE 1993 CAMPAIGN: New Jersey;
X Marks the Spot the Candidates Will Watch on Election Day

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: EAST BRUNSWICK, N.J., Oct. 29

 BODY:
There is a political map of New Jersey that some politicians carry around in their minds. It shows the state's political landscape as an enormous, misshapen X.
In this map, the line that starts at top right and drops to the bottom left is the New Jersey Turnpike, connecting the state's big cities -- Newark, Elizabeth, Trenton, Camden -- as it runs through the Democratic Party's old urban power base. 
 The other line is the Garden State Parkway. Everything from its name to its ban on trucks speaks of a suburban, white-collar Republicanism. Its path meanders from the old North Jersey suburbs of Bergen County through Monmouth and Ocean Counties to the new, tax-shy settlements of the shore.
 
Balance of Power Shifts
 Over the years, the balance of power in the state has been shifting from the turnpike to the parkway, said Stephen Salmore, a professor of political science at Rutgers and an occasional Republican consultant who has transformed the highways into political metaphor.
"In the 30's, 40's and 50's, when the turnpike vote turned out to vote, the Democrats won the state," he said. "Now the axiom is that the candidates who win the Garden State Parkway win the election."
Where the two roads cross here in central Jersey also happens to be where the state's political crosscurrents are most turbulent, a quality that was vividly illustrated in the 1991 legislative races, the election in which Gov. Jim Florio's $2.8 billion tax increase was just about the only topic. In the anti-tax backlash, Republicans swept all the seats in 6 of the state's 40 legislative districts. Three of those districts -- the 14th, 18th and 19th, which had been controlled by Democrats -- lie here at the state's waist.
Mr. Florio and his Republican opponent, Christine Todd Whitman, will be watching this area closely on Tuesday. The will look at exit polls and early returns to see whether one-time Florio supporters will return to the Democrats, both in the governor's race and legislative contests, either because they have decided to lay aside their anger with him or because they find some of Mrs. Whitman's positions unpalatable.
 
Voters' Ambivalence
 If voters like Richard Toth are any indication, the message may take a while to sort itself out.
Mr. Toth, a health safety auditor from Milltown, in the 18th District, voted for Mr. Florio in 1989, but on a recent sunny afternoon spent not catching any fish from Lake Farrington, he gave the undecided voter's refrain: Whitman. No, maybe Florio after all. No, Whitman.
"Florio won't run on his record and give people a reason to try him again," Mr. Toth said. "If he won't run on his record, all people have are their memories, and in his case, the memories are all bad."
Mr. Toth says he will make the decision Election Day. "It'll probably be Whitman, but it could be Florio. It's going to be how I feel Tuesday morning."
 
Anyone but Florio
 Not so Diane Rossiter, an Edison housewife who does volunteer work for the P.T.O., the Altar Guild and the Girl Scouts. She too is a one-time Florio supporter, but the tax increase has come between her and her history of voting Democratic.
"My personal choice would be for anyone who isn't Florio, and I don't care who knows it," she said. "It doesn't matter what his or her name is, as long as it isn't Florio and as long as she isn't a convict, I'll vote for her."
Mrs. Rossiter and her husband, who transports toll booth receipts for the Triborough Bridge and Tunnel Authority, fall into what Dr. Salmore says is the "middle-middle-middle" category: middle class, middle aged, middle income. These voters predominate here in Middlesex County, he said. They registered the biggest defection from Florio and the Democrats in 1991, he said, and are vital to Mrs. Whitman if she is to offset Mr. Florio's inroads into some communities farther up the Garden State Parkway in Morris County.
 
A Republican Loyalist
 William Haacker, an accountant who lives in East Brunswick, said he would support the Republican because he has always been a Republican.
It was true, he volunteered, that the Republican-run legislature wiped out his homestead rebate and doubled the cost of his elderly mother's prescriptions under the state health plan by cutting the sales tax. Governor Florio has made much of the fallout from the rollback, but Mr. Haacker holds all politicians equally at fault.
"It's a choice between two evils," he said cheerfully. "They're all gutless wonders."
In Trenton, Drew Mueller, a carpenter, spoke up for Governor Florio.
"On the taxes, we're always going to have them," he said over the hubbub of the after-work crowd at Larry's Corner Tavern on Mulberry Street, where a bumper sticker on the wall reads, "If Florio's the Answer, Then It's a Stupid Question."
"I was against him when the taxes went through, but the main thing for me is, we're starting to get the housing construction business back, so I guess the taxes aren't hurting business that much anymore. So yeah, I'm for him."
 
Florio's Base
 When he represented Camden in Congress, Mr. Florio considered interior South Jersey, in Camden and Gloucester Counties, his base, and always counted on a heavy turnout for him below Trenton to offset the Republican strength in the north.
Yet in the last decade, the landscape has changed. Thousands of professionals have been drawn to new housing developments in the scrublands west of Atlantic City. From the north, moving south along the Garden State Parkway, other subdivisions have sprung up in Ocean and eastern Monmouth Counties to house a new generation of young commuters and elderly, tax-averse retirees.
Environmental issues, a strong Florio suit, do not loom as large in the more open reaches of the south as they do in the congested north, Dr. Salmore said. At the same time, hunting and gun ownership is more common in the south down to Cape May, whose southern tip, South Jerseyans are proud to point out, reaches below the Mason-Dixon line.
Without those two elements of Mr. Florio's appeal to hold them, South Jersey's voters are becoming less predictably Democratic.
Tom Dresch, a 35-year old security consultant, is one of these newcomers. As a young father and a first-time homeowner, his chief worries are about the economy.
"My parents were Democrats right down the line because they think Republicans are unfair to the little guy," he said. "I'm more interested in seeing the business climate be strong, and I think it's obvious that Whitman would be better for that."

GRAPHIC: Photos: "My personal choice would be for anyone who isn't Florio," said Diane Rossiter, a longtime Democrat who lives in Edison. Sauro/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Votes in Congress

 BODY:

Tally Last Week in Connecticut, New Jersey and New York
Senate
1. Taxes: Procedural vote that attempted to roll back the retroactive tax increases included in President Clinton's deficit reduction bill. Failed, with 50 in favor and 44 opposed (10 short of teh 60 needed), Oct. 26.
 2. Jobless Aid: Vote on an amendment to a bill providing emergency unemployment benefits that would deny additional benefits to any jobless workers who had reported a taxable income in 1992 of more than $120,000. Approved 52 to 43, Oct. 27. 
 3. Jobless Aid: Vote on an amendment to a bill providing emergency unemployment benefits that would carve into law a White House proposal to reduce the Federal workforce by 252,000 employees. Approved 82 to 14, Oct. 28.
4. Jobless Aid: Vote on passage of a bill to renew a two-year-old program that grants up to 13 extra weeks of benefits to jobless workers who exhausted their normal unemployment benefits. Approved 76 to 20, Oct. 28.
5. Filibuster: Vote to cut off a filibuster blocking the Administration's plan for major changes on federally owned range land, including nearly doubling grazing fees. Failed, with 54 in favor and 44 opposed (six votes short of the 60 needed), Oct. 28.

HEADLINE: On Bid Rigging and Price Fixing

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Michael Sproul ("Before Setting Antitrust Policy, Study It," Viewpoints, Oct. 10) asserts that the Department of Justice "has never examined" the price effects of the price-fixing and bid-rigging schemes it prosecutes. (He examined 25 cases and found that prices did not fall following indictments or convictions.) On the contrary, the department commonly studies the price effects of conspiracies. These studies typically are not placed in the trial record, however, and therefore normally cannot be made public. 
 In a soon-to-be published paper, two of my colleagues and I report on the effects of a typical recent bid-rigging scheme. We found that the scheme raised prices of frozen seafood purchased by the Department of Defense by 23 percent to 30 percent for more than four years. Prices fell precipitously when the conspirators discovered that they were under investigation.
A major flaw in Mr. Sproul's study is that he examined the behavior of prices following indictments and convictions. As the frozen seafood conspiracy illustrates, prices are likely to have already fallen from conspiracy levels by the time of indictment.
 
GREGORY J. WERDEN
Washington, Oct. 18
 
The writer is director of research at the Justice Department antitrust division's economic analysis group.

HEADLINE: Alfie, Oh Alfie, What Can We Do With Ye?

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In "For Best Results, Forget the Bonus" (Viewpoints, Oct. 17), Alfie Kohn suggests that rewards are dangerous. Extrinsic rewards such as money, praise and recognition will, according to him, undermine intrinsic interest in rewarded activity. If this is so, it is strange that his own attacks on rewards persist, since they have surely earned him money, praise and recognition. 
 Fortunately, his thesis is false. I say "fortunately" since this country is a meritocracy -- a social and economic system that values talent and hard work and has generally rewarded them. Where the system has been most faithfully implemented, America is a world leader. Where rewards have not been closely related to performance, as in much of education, America has fallen behind.
Do rewards really undermine interest? Rupture relationships? Deter risk taking? There are studies that show negative effects from rewards, just as there are studies that show negative effects from aspirin. But the vast majority of studies show that rewards, properly used, result in better performance, more productivity and even more creativity -- without adverse side effects.
Mr. Kohn argues that our society should abolish the reward system that recognizes sustained effort and the productive use of knowledge. Ironically, one can obtain money, praise and recognition for arguing against those rewards for others.
 
SIGRID S. GLENN
Denton, Tex., Oct. 27
 
The writer, an associate professor at the State University of North Texas, heads the Association for Behavior Analysis.

HEADLINE: Bruce Babbitt's Landscape, at Risk

 BODY:
For months, Interior Secretary Bruce Babbitt has been trying to persuade Congress to accept a small increase in grazing fees and new regulations to halt the deterioration of 260 million acres of public rangeland. And for months, he has been frustrated by Republicans determined to embarrass President Clinton and by a small but ornery group of Western Democrats determined to preserve a way of life that exists mainly in their minds.
An exasperated Mr. Babbitt now says he will impose the fees and regulations by administrative fiat, which is his right under the Federal rule-making process. But that is not the outcome he had hoped for. 
 A legislative solution is far preferable to administrative fiat, if only because Congress is less likely to tinker with it later. On a deeper level, the Senate's refusal to enact this modest reform spells huge trouble for Mr. Babbitt's more sweeping ambition to reverse not only 12 years of Reagan-Bush indifference to the environment but an entire century of federally sponsored exploitation of the public lands.
The Administration's environmental agenda includes proposals to charge royalties on gold and other minerals now mined on Federal land at no cost, reauthorization of the 1972 Clean Water Act and, most contentious of all, renewal of the Endangered Species Act.
If the Senate's behavior on grazing fees is any guide, these and other environmental initiatives are now seriously at risk. There's not much Mr. Clinton can do about the Republicans, but he can whip the dissidents in his own party into line. Unless he does so, his pledge to become the "environment President" George Bush promised to be but wasn't is almost sure to go unfulfilled.
Mr. Clinton originally included the higher grazing fees in his budget but caved in when a bipartisan cadre of Western senators threatened to torpedo his entire economic package unless the fees were removed. Mr. Babbitt then said he would raise the fees on his own authority, at which point the House and Senate agreed to a compromise -- now blocked by a Senate filibuster led by a few self-appointed Sons of the Prairie who have invoked everything but John Wayne to make their case that higher fees will destroy a vanishing way of life.
The fees will "do those old cowboys in," warns Senator Alan Simpson. "We are defending a Western life style [against] this Administration's war on the West." For sheer hyperbole, few can match the Wyoming Republican, but his views are shared by Democrats like Max Baucus of Montana, Jeff Bingaman of New Mexico, Ben Nighthorse Campbell of Colorado, and Byron Dorgan and Kent Conrad of North Dakota.
Their arguments are nonsense. Under present law, ranchers are charged $1.86 to graze one cow and one calf for a month. That's one-fifth the going market rate on private land. The compromise bill would raise that fee, on average, to $3.45, yielding a meager $20 million extra over four years to help restore damaged lands. There's no evidence so far that the fees will drive even smaller ranchers off the land. In New Mexico, where state fees have doubled to $3.41 a month, no ranchers have quit and people are still lining up for permits.
Moreover, as The Times's Timothy Egan pointed out in an article on Friday, many of the ranchers who would pay this fee are hardly marginal dirt farmers. Mr. Simpson's "old cowboys" include the Metropolitan Life Insurance Company, with 800,000 acres under its control; the Mormon Church; a Japanese conglomerate, and some of the wealthiest families in the nation. In all, 28,000 ranchers hold permits to graze their livestock on 260 million acres of public land. That comes to just under 10,000 acres per rancher, and most of them profit hugely from one of the great bargains in the Federal store.
One Western Senator with a firmer grip on reality is Harry Reid, a Democrat from Searchlight, Nev. Mr. Reid, who helped write the Senate compromise, agrees that below-market fees are an indefensible public subsidy. But what really offends him is the Simpson-Baucus fantasy that the West's future lies entirely in the hands of 28,000 ranchers who hold permits to graze on public land, not the 50 million others who also live there.
"I think it is important that we reject gridlock and do it now," Mr. Reid said on the Senate floor when debate began. "I ask [my colleagues] to reject the demagoguery that comes from those who represent a West that no longer exists."
Mr. Reid and the pro-Babbitt forces will try one more time to break the filibuster, possibly early this week. Perhaps by then the opponents will have come to their senses, realizing that their narrow allegiance to a small interest group, not to mention their misplaced romanticism, threatens more than Mr. Babbitt's effort to bring responsible stewardship to the public lands. It also emboldens all those who wish to undermine the rest of the Administration's environmental agenda.

HEADLINE: Iraq Raid Stirs Doubts on Clinton Policy

 BODY:
To the Editor
An article by Seymour M. Hersh in The New Yorker suggests that the Clinton Administration had inconclusive evidence for its charge that Saddam Hussein was responsible for an attempt to assassinate former President Bush in Kuwait (news article, Oct. 25) -- and thus for the bombing of Baghdad. This raises important questions, which require Congressional investigation. 
 The bombing won almost unanimous Congressional support and gave President Clinton a crucial lift in public opinion polls. Nevertheless, critics at the time -- including you in "Was This Strike Necessary?" (editorial, June 28) -- questioned the factual basis of President Clinton's act, its international legality and its wisdom as foreign policy. The critics were dismissed, as usual in the euphoria following a military strike against our archetypal enemy. But the facts and questions must now be openly aired and fully explored.
In examining the June 26 raid against Iraq, Congress must address broader issues: the honesty and judgment of President Clinton's national security staff, as well as the soundness of major elements of the Administration's policy. Political risks seem to have driven the Administration into extreme positions that are politically safe, but objectively dangerous.
This is true not only of the President's policy toward Iraq (where we insist on sanctions that are hurting civilians, and where even allies such as Turkey are opposed), but also toward Russia (where the rhetoric of democracy masks President Boris N. Yeltsin's authoritarian nationalism, and where we are responsible for blocking any moderation of economic shock therapy).
Representative Frank McCloskey's call for Secretary of State Warren Christopher's resignation (Op-Ed, Oct. 24) is particularly valuable in that Mr. McCloskey goes beyond easy criticisms of the "effectiveness" of our policies on Somalia and Haiti. He takes on the far more important question of our policy toward Russia. It is an index of the deficiency of conventional wisdom that the Russian policy is often reckoned the Administration's one success, along with the raid on Baghdad.
Congress must now take an unusually independent look at both the issues and the officials who are responsible for them. President Clinton's quest to protect himself from the Republican right on foreign policy will not protect the nation from the enduring costs of the errors to which it leads.
 
DAVID KEPPEL
Essex, Conn. Oct. 26, 1993

HEADLINE: THE 1993 CAMPAIGN: Campaign Trail;
Hillary Clinton Praises Florio's Opposition to Assault Weapons

BYLINE: By ROBERT HANLEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: FAIRFIELD, N.J., Oct. 30

 BODY:
Hillary Rodham Clinton flew to New Jersey for the second time in 10 days today to stand by Gov. Jim Florio, endorse his ban on assault weapons, and then extol him as the candidate for women.
Mrs. Clinton's 20-minute speech in a damp and drafty hangar at the Essex County Airport was the highlight of a busy Saturday of campaigning in which Mr. Florio attended rallies in Atlantic City, Vineland and Turnersville in southern New Jersey, flew here by helicopter, and then went to Camden to attend a children's Halloween party and meet with black and Hispanic officials. 
 Mrs. Clinton praised Mr. Florio for resisting the pressures of the National Rifle Association in his attacks on assault weapons.
"He stood up to the forces that have profited from the violence that has stalked our streets too long," she said. "What you do Tuesday will send a huge signal across the country. I cannot overstate how important this is, to stand by a Governor who has not flinched. This is a battle for the future of our children. This is a battle for the safety of women."
The theme of the rally was "Women for Florio." The Governor stood on a podium in a hangar with 25 women. Besides Mrs. Clinton, they included his wife, Lucinda; Mae Smith, a Newark police detective; Marianne Espinosa Murphy, a former State Family Court Judge denied renomination to the bench, and 21 women running for the State Legislature and for county and local offices.
Mrs. Clinton chided the Governor's opponent, Christine Todd Whitman, about her frequent bus trips in the waning days of the campaign.
"Anybody can get on a bus," Mrs. Clinton said, without naming Mrs. Whitman. "The trick is to figure out which direction you're going."
Mr. Florio, she said, was going in the right direction. She appealed to women as well as men to vote for him.
"The voters of New Jersey can vote for one New Jersey woman or four million New Jersey women," Mrs. Clinton said.
 
'On the Fence'
 Jo Ann Chittenden of Delaware County shook Christine Todd Whitman's hand at T. J.'s Pancake House in Flemington and said: "I'm a registered Democrat. Why should I vote for you?"
Miss Chittenden and her husband, John Pulia, who describe themselves as "on the fence" in Tuesday's election, had come to the restaurant specifically to meet the Republican gubernatorial candidate during her Saturday morning campaign swing down Route 31 to Trenton. If Mrs. Whitman hopes to travel all the way to the State House, they are the kind of voters she will need.
Mrs. Whitman spent several minutes telling the couple why she felt she deserved their votes. She stressed economic issues.
"We've got to bring business back to the state," Mrs. Whitman said. "We've got to stimulate growth to create jobs." In answer to a question, she reaffirmed her stand favoring abortion rights.
Miss Chittenden said later that she remained uncommitted but had been "very impressed" with Mrs. Whitman. Mr. Pulia said that his discomfort with the Democratic incumbent, Gov. Jim Florio, was not over increased taxes but his feeling that Mr. Florio had been "untruthful" in his approach to them.
This was echoed by Joe Pierson of Hopewell, a Whitman supporter in the crowd at her next stop, addressing the Pennington first-aid squad. Mr. Pierson said that "the economic and credibility" issues decided him for the Republican.
Addressing rescue workers from volunteer squads, Mrs. Whitman praised volunteerism and promised to limit state government's local role.
"Obviously the government should set the standards," she said, "but after that it should get out of the way and let you do your job." She pledged a "state mandate, state pay" philosophy guaranteeing that programs created by the state would be optional unless money was provided for them.
She defended her promises on taxes: "Lowering taxes is not lowering taxes for a political campaign or a political speech. It's a philosophy of government."

GRAPHIC: Photo: "Obviously the government should set the standards," Christine Todd Whitman said to the first-aid squad in Pennington, N.J. "But after that it should get out of the way and let you do your job." She visited with the squad and their families yesterday and urged them to vote on Tuesday. (Joyce Dopkeen/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE 1993 CAMPAIGN: Housing -- The Agenda;
Housing's Waiting List Includes Policy for Growth

SERIES: Mayoral Agenda: A periodic report on major issues facing the election winner - 7. Housing

BYLINE: By SHAWN G. KENNEDY

 BODY:
Choosing to focus on one of the more visible aspects of New York City's chronic housing shortage -- homelessness -- the mayoral candidates have given scant campaign attention to housing preservation and redevelopment issues.
But the challenge to the city's next mayor -- the Democratic incumbent, David N. Dinkins, or Rudolph W. Giuliani, a Republican-Liberal -- will remain how to best use city housing resources in the face of shrinking revenues, and how to balance complex redevelopment needs with conflicting social and political interests. 
 There are 270,000 families on the waiting list for public housing. Many now wait 10 years or more to be placed. Yet Housing Authority officials say there are worrisome signs that neighborhood opposition to new public housing will jeopardize Federal financing for some of the 2,000 units in development.
Federal rules are similar to the "fair share" provision in the new City Charter, which says public housing and similar services should be spread throughout the city, rather than concentrated only in low-income areas. That policy, however, is generating political opposition in middle-class neighborhoods where some of the new housing is planned.
 
Landlord by Default
 On the fiscal side, the city is losing an estimated $130 million a year in tax revenues because of landlords who have lost their buildings in property-tax foreclosures. While officials say the city's portfolio of these units has been reduced by nearly 10,000 in the past four years, New York remains the landlord to the tenants in 30,000 such apartments.
Moreover, housing experts say a sharp rise in the number of apartment buildings in default raises the prospect of a new wave of foreclosures and abandonments. Statistics show that the number of residential properties in tax default more than doubled in 1992, to 8,428 from 3,999.
Most housing advocates give Mayor Dinkins credit for maintaining the city's housing initiatives despite fiscal constraints. This week, he unveiled a new package of programs intended to build on current initiatives. The most innovative among them are plans to turn unsold luxury co-op and condominium units into apartments for middle-income families and to put public housing residents into homes of their own.
Even as a worsening economy has tightened the city's finances, New York has stayed ahead of a 1986 pledge to create at least 47,000 new apartments before the mid-1990's.
The commitment, made by former Mayor Edward I. Koch, remains the city's most ambitious housing plan. During the Dinkins administration, the Department of Housing Preservation and Development has created 22,000 apartments in formerly vacant buildings, much of it as permanent housing for the homeless; helped finance the rehabilitation of 21,000 units in privately owned buildings, and paid for some of the construction of 5,000 new homes for low- and moderate-income first-time buyers.
These and other home-ownership initiatives have helped revitalize neighborhoods throughout the South Bronx, Central and East Harlem and Brooklyn.
But Mr. Giuliani argues that the Dinkins administration has concentrated on programs that perpetuate the city's involvement in housing, rather than private participation. Most specifically, he has criticized the administration for holding too long to to buildings taken in taken in property-tax foreclosures. Mr. Dinkins does agree with Mr. Giuliani that more private participation is essential to any long-term solutions to the city's affordable housing crisis.
Except for remarks made during a televised debate with Mayor Dinkins and two other candidates in early August, Mr. Giuliani has not set a housing agenda or given a speech on housing matters. Mr. Giuliani has acknowledged the city's achievements. But the former Federal prosecutor, who was born in the Flatbush section of Brooklyn, has said he still sees neighborhoods suffering from the effects of neglect. He sees landlords teetering on he brink of financial ruin as they struggle to pay taxes and maintenance bills on meager rent receipts.
Mr. Giuliani's advisers say his main goals would be to rid the city of the buildings that were taken over in tax foreclosures, greatly expand opportunities to own homes and use more resources to help owners of the crumbling buildings where low- and moderate-income renters live.
 
Focusing on Private Owners
 William J. Grinker, a Democrat and former head of the city's Human Resources Administration, is Mr. Giuliani's social policy adviser. In trying to reduce the buildings for which the city acts as landlord, he said, Mr. Giuliani would first restore a program that put these buildings in the hands of private owners.
"There were some abuses in POMP," he said, referring to the Private Ownership and Management Program, which was scrapped by the city during the Dinkins administration after complaints from housing advocates that some new landlords were not meeting their obligations. "But the city can't afford to ignore this source of housing managers."
Mr. Grinker said abuse could be prevented by increasing oversight, tightening the requirements and encouraging small, neighborhood owners to take the buildings rather than focusing on attracting large housing management companies.
Mr. Grinker said that under a Giuliani administration, more buildings would be released by encouraging residents to take advantage of the Tenant Interim Lease program, which sells buildings to tenants as low-income co-ops.
 
Incentives for Tenants
 "If you lifted some restrictions -- like the limit on the profit from resale -- more tenants would probably be interested," Mr. Grinker said.
To avert another wave of apartment building abandonment, Mr. Giuliani would carry out an emergency hardship program to help owners on the brink of default. It would use city resources and private lenders.
A centerpiece of Mr. Giuliani's housing policy would be the expansion of home ownership opportunities, Mr. Grinker said, adding that less would be spent on the development of rental housing and more on building new homes. Using the model of Shore Bank in Chicago, a private bank set up in low-income communities, Mr. Giuliani would stimulate neighborhood rebirth by financing both housing and business projects.
Mr. Grinker said that while Mr. Giuliani supported the "fair share" provisions of the new city charter, the candidate believed that Federal Department of Housing and Urban Development site and neighborhood regulations are unrealistic for New York.
 
Opportunity, not 'Nimby'
 H.U.D. requires new developments to be in in areas that are not already heavily populated by minorities.
"The issue is not Nimby," said Mr. Grinker, "but more a matter of opportunity and availability. The sites that are available tend not to be in neighborhoods that meet the H.U.D. requirements."
Felice L. Michetti, Commissioner of the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development, defended the city's housing policies. In past four years, the city has sold 12,400 renovated apartments to their occupants, to community organizations and to some private management firms, Ms. Michetti said. In the next four years, she said, the administration will try to speed up efforts to turn buildings over to nonprofit organizations, or private owners, including the tenants themselves.
"The city has put $2 billion into housing in the past four years," she said, "but what is significant is that we have gotten much more than the dollar-for-dollar value out of it because we have leveraged that money. We've become partners of nonprofit organizations and community redevelopment groups, lenders and the Federal government. And our goal is to do more of this public/private redevelopment in the future."
 
People Who Need a Boost
 She said the city would commit another $1.8 billion to produce and preserve an additional 48,000 units, but would also emphasize programs for people who need a boost to lift them out of public housing or rentals.
One such program, Project Choice, will offer Housing Authority residents chances to be owners through nonprofit housing corporations that build for moderate-income buyers.
Another program would make use of the thousands of vacant, unsold co-op and condominium units throughout the city. Using the resources of the New York City Residential Mortgage Insurance Corporation, the Federal Home Mortgage Administration and private banks, moderate-income families would be offered mortgages so they could buy these apartments.
 
 
The Series

This is the final installment of the Mayoral Agenda series. Earlier articles examined the city's impending budget crisis, Sept. 27; social services issues, Oct. 2; crime, Oct. 11; the economy, Oct. 18; the city's infrastructure needs on Thursday and education yesterday.

CORRECTION-DATE: November 1, 1993, Monday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about the New York mayoral campaign misstated the amount of a new Department of Housing and Urban Development grant. The city is to receive $37 million over the next year, and plans to use $20 million to hire 400 additional Housing Authority police officers. While the grant is projected to continue at the same level over the next five years, providing a total of $100 million to finance police hiring, that amount is not guaranteed.

GRAPHIC: Chart: "THE ISSUES: The Candidates On Housing"
Here are the candidates' positions on major housing and community development issues:
 
 RUDOLPH W. GIULIANI, REPUBLICAN/LIBERAL
 -- Would vastly increase home ownership opportunities.
-- Would move the city more quickly out of managing apartment buildings whose owners have forfeited for failure to pay taxes.
-- Wants to revitalize decaying neighborhoods through housing and commercial redevelopment.
 
 MAYOR DAVID N. DINKINS, DEMOCRAT
 -- Plans to create new ownership opportunities by converting failed co-op and condominum buildings to affordable housing.
-- Wants to create ownership opportunities for public housing residents.
-- Would more aggressively seek to have private corporations take over buildings managed by the city because of owners' failure to pay taxes.

SECTION: Section 13; Page 4; Column 1; The City Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 432 words

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.

 BODY:

Farewell to Macy's Sign: 'World's Largest Store'
 The sign on Macy's in Herald Square, proclaiming it "The World's Largest Store," has come down. An electronic replacement is to go up on Nov. 26 and start operating after Jan. 1. What kind of messages will the new version broadcast? Laura Melillo, a spokeswoman for the store, would say only that the sign "says that we are a retailer, and says something about shopping." 
 The sign will be 70 feet tall and 52 feet wide and sit 30 feet above the street. Macy's has had a "world's largest" sign in the space since 1932.
 
Former Helmsley Palace Going for $202 Million
 The New York Palace Hotel, once known as the Helmsley Palace, is about to be sold for $202 million to the offshore partnership Amedeo Hotels. The hotel, at 455 Madison Avenue (50th Street), came under the control of a court-appointed receiver, W. Thomas Parrington Jr., last April after a long dispute between Harry and Leona Helmsley and a group of limited partners who contended that mismanagement had led to losses of $10 million in 1991.
Thomas McConnell, senior manager at Kenneth Leventhal & Company, a New York consulting group that has been employed by the Helmsleys, said the price was high given the depressed real-estate market. "It's a one-of-a-kind situation," he explained. "The Palace is a big property and extremely well located."
But don't look for the signs on the Palace to change next week. "This is a very large transaction for a hotel," Mr. McConnell said. "It doesn't get done overnight."
 
Two Olive Gardens Will Open in Midtown
 General Mills will open two Olive Garden restaurants in Manhattan next year, joining an outlet in Kew Gardens that appeared in April. The chain, which offers Italian fare, family style, has 407 restaurants nationwide. The new ones will be at 47th Street and Broadway and at 34th Street and Eighth Avenue.
"It's not like we said in 1994 we are going into New York City," said Cathie Weinberg, a spokeswoman for the chain. "We are constantly looking for available property at the right price. Those areas happened to open up, and the timing and price were right."
 
Fewer Businesses Fail,But Total Is Still High
 Fewer New York City businesses failed in the first nine months of 1993 than in the same period last year, Dun & Bradstreet reports.
The number of business failures in the five boroughs dropped 9.4 percent. The construction industry fared worst with a 3 percent increase in failures; the number of failing companies in finance, insurance and real estate dropped by 15 percent.
