HEADLINE: SOAPBOX;
On the City Politic: Voters' Round Table

 BODY:
Four New Yorkers came together for three hours last Sunday with Felicia R. Lee and Larry Olmstead of The New York Times to discuss the candidates and the issues in Tuesday's mayoral election and their feelings about life in New York. The participants were Melissa Woronoff, 20, a college student who divides her time between the Bronx and Manhattan; Ken Shung, 39, a free-lance photographer who lives in Manhattan; Jerolyn J. Minter, a retired college professor who lives in St. Albans, Queens, and Roger D. Fields, a "40ish" Harlem resident and the senior vice president of the United Block Front Street Association, a program in which ex-offenders build housing.
What follows are excerpts from their discussion.

What things are most important to you when you look at the quality of life in the city?
 ROGER FIELDS: No. 1 is crime, which, as an African-American, is very, very on my mind. And No. 2 is affordable housing. When one talks about upgrading and upper mobility in any community, it must be a safe community. And once you build housing, that housing, which may be comfortable and affordable, has to be in a safe neighborhood.
JEROLYN MINTER: I agree with much of what you're saying, but I think when you put things in order of priority, it's not crime first. Crime is an offshoot of something that I think is more deeply rooted. Attention to education and educational standards, economics, housing -- all are built upon the first fundamental, and that's learning how to understand someone who is different from you that you have to live next door to or in the community right adjacent to yours.
FIELDS: Well, I don't tend to agree with you. When one talks about empowering different ethnic groups, those communities must first have safe streets and must have affordable housing before one can no longer feel stressed. And one gets angry because one sees that one group is getting more than another group, which also causes division.
No. 2, if we talk about education standards, when you develop affordable housing and affordable home ownership, then those communities become mixed and those educational standards become more active, because the community is not monolithic nor mono-economic.
Melissa, what's your idea of the key areas?
MELISSA WORONOFF: Well, I would agree with Jerolyn. I would also add not only race but also class and religion. And I think that's what's great about New York City, but it makes things hard at times. I mean, to get those things to work out, that's really the starting point and then other things will follow.
KEN SHUNG: My concerns with the city have to do with crime. I think the real issue for me is, like, who's raising our kids? When there are two functional parents in the house, then you can maybe have the next generation of people that would want to not resort to crime. I just feel that you've got to start at home. If parents don't spend time with their children, then there's no love. When there's no love, the children don't grow up with any kind of sense of responsibility and moral obligation.
MINTER: I have a concern when I read statistics that come past my desk, that eight out of 10 African-American children are going to be brought up in single-parent families. What is going to happen to that poor mother who's going out working, trying to support that child. And she has to put that child in a daycare center if she doesn't get home until 5, and that daycare center or that school program is not going to be able to give them the kind of structure that they need. When they look and see Mama struggling to pay the rent and buy food, and on the corner is this drug dealer who has diamonds and is driving cars, what's going to happen? What happens is that person becomes the role model.
WORONOFF: I think it's interesting that there certainly are a lot more single-parent families today. But I think what needs to happen is that both our city and the country need to acknowledge that families are changing and need to accommodate for that, rather than try to change it.
What can the mayor of this city, whoever is elected, do about any of these issues? What are they really responsible for?
MINTER: They're responsible for selecting the people who are going to take care of the various city agencies and monitoring them. I would make sure these people are knowledgeable, decisive, have some humanist to them and are in contact with the needs of the people.
FIELDS: I would fire every single commissioner in the City of New York; that's the first thing I would do. The second thing, I would go to people who have grown up in this city, understand its problems, are not political. I would want my commissioners to be responsible to me, not to the Democratic or Republican or whatever leader placed them there.
MINTER: I have come to realize that in our society we aren't willing to give people time enough to bring about change. You can't come in and change things in four years that have been messed up for the last 12.
FIELDS: Wait a minute, Jerolyn, are you trying to tell me things were messed up in the last 12 years before David Dinkins became Mayor? This Mayor, he has more nepotism than any other mayor in the history of this city. He appoints Joyce Brown deputy mayor -- who happens to be married to Carl McCall, the person the Mayor appointed to the Board of Ed. He appoints Victor Gottbaum to the Board of Ed, and his wife to being Parks Commissioner. I can go on and on and on. I think that this Mayor has shown no leadership. I think this Mayor has gone to traditional clubhouse politics and has not reached out to the community. I think the city was much better four years ago than it is right now.
Who else wants to jump into that?
WORONOFF: I think the country was better off four years ago than it is now. I mean, the economy was better. When the economy goes down, you know, crime does go up. We have more homeless people. Fewer people have jobs; more people are being laid off. I wouldn't place that blame on Mayor Dinkins.
Ken, are you better off than you were four years ago?
SHUNG: Oh, what a question. I guess -- I don't know, I haven't been that alert in the last four years. Do I feel more fear when I walk down the street? I do.

The accusation has been that the Mayor panders too much to blacks. And that Mr. Giuliani has pandered too much to whites. Then the President came in and said that there are too many people in this city who are unwilling to vote for people who are different from themselves, be it because of race or religion.
SHUNG: Anyone who suffers from any kind of discrimination might find truth in that kind of statement. I think people go into the voting booths after all is said and done, and get very tribal and start to, like, push the lever they feel they can identify with.
FIELDS: I think that everywhere you go there are going to be people who are racist. But I think people are concerned about issues. And President Clinton, I thought, made an inappropriate statement which caused the campaign to become even more racially oriented than it is.
What should the Mayor do to build bridges to racial harmony?
MINTER: I believe that Mayor Dinkins has been systematically trying to build those bridges here in New York, initiating programs all the way toward bringing this into fruition.
FIELDS: In reference to Mayor Dinkins's leadership in building bridges, I did not approve of the way he handled Crown Heights. I did not approve the way he handled the Korean boycott. I did not approve of the way he dealt with the Washington Heights situation with the drug dealer, in which my tax dollars were used to bury him. I think when you build bridges, you build them in an objective and corrective manner. I think, unfortunately, in this campaign, no one is building any bridges.
MINTER: In terms of Giuliani, I don't think that he would be building bridges. He has set a tone with his activities that, to me, is negative and divisive. I will vote for David Dinkins, because I believe in his dream.
FIELDS: Well, let me tell you something. I'm not going to vote for David Dinkins. I'm going to vote, this ex-offender, this African-American, is going to vote for Rudolph Giuliani. I think his record in reference to civil rights when he was United States Attorney shows his concern. I don't see Rudy Giuliani as a police monster.
Tell me about welfare.
MINTER: To eliminate the high numbers of people on welfare, you have to improve the economic situation. You have to provide training for people so that they can fit into the economic arena for jobs. For mothers who have to work, you have to provide the services that will care for the child while the mother is in training on a new job. You have pockets of this happening. It's not happening across the board in large numbers because you're looking at money. Where do you get the funding for this?
FIELDS: You've been doing it for 30 years, and it hasn't worked, the social scientist's philosophy. This is this old liberal philosophy that has kept our community behind. We need to inform people very candidly. If you have children out of wedlock continuously, you are going to be punished. We must stop people from having six children from six different fathers.
No. 2, we cannot reward people because of the fact that they do not wish to work. I agree with you, we must provide jobs. But those jobs must be provided with the partnership between private industry and the government. The government cannot continue to absorb and create these make-believe jobs and cost taxpayers more money. Now the welfare system rewards you.
What is it that the mayor can and should do?
FIELDS: He should have an aggressive, aggressive program of finding fathers who are not paying. We need to have these people who are on temporary income maintenance, these Aid to Dependent Children, and these Workfare people actually participate in training and working and placed in private enterprises. We need an aggressive program like that. And Mayor Dinkins doesn't have that program.
MINTER: He has started some of these programs.
FIELDS: He hasn't started -- the only thing he has started is sitting there and playing tennis at Gracie Mansion.
WORONOFF: I would say that as far as welfare is concerned, we need to have programs that fit the people's needs, rather than programs that lead people into who we want them to be. If there are single mothers with children, then we need some sort of daycare for these children while the mothers learn the skills that they need to go out and work. We need support for our community members out there.
We're talking about ethics and values. What can any city administration do to affect values -- let's say, the homeless problem?
SHUNG: Well, those policies should not polarize people and put them on the margins of society. I think people tend to think: "Well, not in my neighborhood. Or not in my backyard. Or I'll just retreat and it won't happen." What happens is, they'll be sitting in a public school, or even a private school. And the child that grew up on welfare, that value system will be in the same classroom as your child who had maybe a nice upbringing, and those values will cross. This is a problem that America has to confront, that our value systems, whether you're rich or poor, and those class distinctions, are eventually going to cross paths.
MINTER: You know, another concern is health care. I think that a lot of women, particularly pro-choice women, may not be inclined to vote for Giuliani, because he has gone back and forth on the issue of abortion. I think that taking a stance on abortion is really taking a stance on women and how highly you value them.
FIELDS: Well, people are going to vote for David Dinkins, who at one time was not pro-sexual preference, and that's become pro-sexual preference.
WORONOFF: I'd also say that Giuliani's issues on homelessness and his 90-day policy is something that people are going to be looking at.
FIELDS: I think what he was talking about was that bureaucracies tend to drag their feet. And what he was saying was, "Within that 90 days, I want that person placed." I think he was using the concept that the Rev. Calvin Butts and the Abyssinian church do in Harlem, that you come to the shelter for a short period of time. And the first day you're there, you begin to look for employment and permanent housing.
Is the city doing enough?
SHUNG: To deal with homeless? The city is not doing enough. I think I don't even look for Mayor Dinkins to solve the world's problems. I look for him to start moving in the right direction, to build the bridge instead of break it down. . . .
MINTER: I'd like to tell you, I have not voted for Mayor Dinkins because he was black. I voted for him because I thought he was the best person to do the job. I'm proud of him because he happens to be an African-American. I feel that he sets a good role model for African-American children, who can look and say: "I can aspire, too. And doors will not necessarily always be closed to me." But I think he is a man, a human being, trying to do what I would like to see done in this city.
FIELDS: Well, I will tell you, it took a lot for me not to vote for him this time.
Because of race?
FIELDS: Because of race. It was one of the most difficult decisions I've ever made in my life. . . . You know, it's interesting, because you have such diverse people here. Here I am, supporting a relatively conservative candidate. And here are some of the traditional people who you would think would support a more conservative candidate, supporting a liberal candidate.
And what I'm hearing over and over is that we're all saying the same thing. Who would be the best mayor to take care of the quality of life and show compassion? We're all saying, who's going to show more compassion? All of us sitting here who are employed or have some income are saying we're concerned about those people who can't take care of themselves.

HEADLINE: Good Eating;
Ethnic Pleasures, Elmhurst to Astoria

 BODY:

ELIAS CORNER (Diner's Journal)
(718) 932-1510; 31st Street and 24th Avenue, Astoria;  no credit cards accepted
This popular, tiny neighborhood spot serves some of the freshest seafood in New York City. There is a glass fish counter at the entrance and a tiny square kitchen behind it. The small dining room with a cooler for beer and wine, a plastic shark mounted on the wall, and the pale fluorescent lighting is convivial. There are no menus. Regulars walk over to the counter and pick their seafood. 
 If available, recommended dishes include charred fresh octopus drizzled with strong olive oil; deep-fried, pinkie-size Mediterranean fish called aherino; grilled porgies and red snappers; excellent taramosalata, the lemony fish roe puree; and tsatsiki: yogurt, mint and cucumber. (Diner's Journal: 7/26/91; article: 2/9/92)
 
LA FUSTA (Article)
(718) 429-8222; 80-32 Baxter Avenue, Elmhurst; $ no credit cards accepted
The atmosphere at this popular Argentine restaurant is casual and congenial. Tablecloths are checkered, and a South American mood is achieved with stucco and timber walls festooned with horse-racing memorabilia.
Parrillada is the Spanish term for a mixed grill of T-bone steak, marinated skirt steak, morcilla (blood sausage), chorizo (Spanish sausage), sweetbreads, kidneys, calf's liver and sausage. La Fusta's mixed grill comes with a jar of chimichurri, a piquant sauce made with garlic, parsley and olive oil.
Other dishes are empanadas de carne, redolent of garlic and minced olives; grilled chicken; filet mignon, and short ribs. (Article: 2/9/92)
 
PARK SIDE (Diner's Journal)
(718) 271-9274; 107-01 Corona Avenue (108th and 51st Avenue), Corona; all major credit cards
The sprawling, fern-festooned establishment has all the familiar trappings of a traditional Italian restaurant: brick arches, smoked glass panels, waiters in black tie and photographs of prominent Italians on the wall.
It differs from so many other old-fashioned red-sauce Italian restaurants in that the kitchen cares about textures and flavors, not just volume. Among dishes to look for are tiny clams oreganata; shrimp in garlic sauce; fried calamari; chicken parmigiano, and chicken scarpariello. Pastas are uniformly pleasing. (Diner's Journal: 5/28/93)
 
O LAVRADOR ($25 and Under)
(718) 526-1526; 138-40 101st Avenue, Richmond Hill;  all major credit cards
This rustic Portuguese restaurant and tavern attracts a steady mixed crowd for its homey fare and ethnic ambiance. The setting is different depending on which door you enter. On the left is a sometimes raucous tavern, to the right a more sedate informal dining room with oak floors, brick walls and dim lights.
Appetizers include broiled shrimp, and cherrystone clams in a broth flavored with Portuguese ham and chorizo, or with a garlic sauce. Entrees are gargantuan, served with heaping mounds of yellow rice and crisp french fries.
Best offerings are barbecued chicken marinated in hot sauce, pork and clams in a lemony brown sauce, and fried veal with white wine and lemon. ($25 and Under: 8/28/92)
 
TELLY'S TAVERNA (Diner's Journal)
(718) 728-9194; 28-13 23d Avenue, Astoria;  no credit cards accepted
Some of the best and most economical seafood buys can be found in the Greek neighborhoods of Astoria. Telly's Taverna is such a place, indistinguishable in its decor from many similar restaurants: whitewashed walls, a tiny glass-enclosed kitchen up front, maybe some photos of the old country, simply appointed rows of tables and a cooler for wine and beer.
The food is fresh and invigorating. For starters, sprightly Greek salads and purees can be recommended. Also good are taramosalata, a creamy and saline fish-roe spread, and skordalia, a garlic-powered puree. The best way to choose a main course is to inspect the fresh fish on display at the entrance. Look for the grilled octopus, grilled porgies, red snapper, striped bass and swordfish.
(Diner's Journal: 2/12/93)
 
UNCLE GEORGE'S ($25 and Under)
(718) 626-0593; 33-19 Broadway, Astoria;  no credit cards accepted
Open 24 hours a day, this bright, sprawling neighborhood place has a steady stream of patrons. Waiters orchestrate seating at family-style tables, meant to accommodate as many diners as possible.
The enticing food is what draws people in: leg of lamb, chicken and suckling pig slowly turning on spits near the window. Other recommended entrees are sauteed octopus in a lemony sauce, stewed rabbit, barbecued pork and lamb burgers. ($25 and Under: 1/15/93)
 
WATER'S EDGE **
(718) 482-0033; East River Yacht Club, 44th Drive at East River, Long Island City; $$ all major credit cards
This romantic spot with sweeping views of midtown Manhattan in a commodious setting attracts a dressy local crowd, including many tables of celebrants. The beguiling main dining room has dark wood panels, scalloped sconces, a fireplace and a piano bar.
Enticing starters on the current menu include pan-seared house-smoked salmon with a sweet onion-caper condiment; wild mushrooms fricassee in pastry with smoked mozzarella, and ragu of baby clams with cannellini beans in a broth of parsley and garlic.
Entrees include roasted cod with sauteed escarole and garlic; roast loin of venison with a sour cherry sauce and chestnut blinis, and braised filet mignon in chardonnay with cepes (wild mushrooms) and truffled mashed potatoes. (Review: 2/21/92)
 
ROUMELI TAVERNA *
(718) 278-7533; 33-04 Broadway, Astoria;  AE
This is one of the more reliable and diverting spots for distinctive Greek cuisine, and the stucco walls are hidden behind leafy vines ablaze with tiny bulbs. The casual dining room is packed nightly with a cheerful neighborhood crowd. At the entrance, a little glass counter displays the fresh fish and meat that will be the night's offerings.
The menu carries the familiar Greek standards: fat and flaky spinach pie; stuffed grape leaves; crisply fried eggplant, and a vibrant hummus (a chickpea and garlic puree).
Other recommendations are deep-fried calamari; grilled porgies, snapper or mullets, and a special of baby leg of lamb with potatoes served at room temperature. (Review: 11/20/ 87; article: 7/8/92)

HEADLINE: Neighborhood Report: Flushing;
Downtown Expansion Plan: Westward Ho!

BYLINE: By David W. Dunlap

 BODY:
Saying that downtown Flushing is bursting with pent-up demand for commercial and residential space, city planners last week recommended measures to encourage development in the central retail hub and channel future construction westward.
"This area has experienced a tremendous influx of new population, economic activity and investment," said Richard L. Schaffer, director of the City Planning Department. "It's a very vibrant business district that is literally bursting at its seams." 
 To open opportunities nearby, planners proposed rezoning a manufacturing district on either side of Prince Street, more than tripling the allowable commercial density and permitting residential development for the first time, Mr. Schaffer said.
The district manager of Community Board 7, Regina Colletta, said the movement toward Prince Street had started already. "It used to be only up to Main Street and Roosevelt Avenue where all the activity was," she said. "Now if you go on Prince Street, you'll see a lot of Asian restaurants and tourists visiting shops. So it's logical that we should go westward."
Before this or any other rezoning takes effect, it must go through environmental and land-use review, involving Community Boards, the Borough President, the City Planning Commission and the City Council, among others.
City planners also envision rezoning a roughly triangular 44-acre manufacturing district, bounded by the Flushing River, College Point Boulevard and the Long Island Rail Road, for commercial and residential uses.
A 14-acre parcel in this district, at College Point Boulevard and Roosevelt Avenue, is owned by the Muss Development Corporation of Forest Hills. "Our intentions generally for the last 10 years have been to build a mixed-use development combining residential, commercial and retail," said Joshua Muss, president of the company.
When he learned that the rezoning was tied to the cleanup of the heavily polluted Flushing River, Mr. Muss expressed skepticism. "They're talking about 2010 and then they'll start the environmental review," he said, adding that city planners "don't have the mindset to plan the boroughs."
Mr. Schaffer said the rezoning along the Flushing River would begin "as resources allow over the next few years." This week, the City Planning Commission is to vote on a proposed 40-million-gallon sewer overflow tank on the river, which might be operating by the year 2001.
For the near future, city planners also recommended easing the parking requirements in the 13-block central retail area of Flushing, through which Main Street and Roosevelt Avenue both run. Under current rules, one parking space must be provided for every 300 square feet of floor space for general retail uses.
City planners acknowledged that "it is difficult to comply" with this rule on the moderately sized lots that predominate. Mr. Schaffer said the requirements were "unreasonably high" for a downtown that is well served by public transportation. The proposal seeks a parking space for every 400 square feet of floor space.
In a separate report, planners recommended rezoning Long Island City with a view to creating New York's fourth central business district. (Midtown Manhattan, lower Manhattan and downtown Brooklyn are the others.) A 36-block, arrowhead-shaped area west of the Sunnyside Yards, between Court Square and Queens Plaza, could become a "well-defined, pedestrian-oriented, high-density 24-hour urban center," the report said. HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.;
In Times Square, Turning Profits From 'Gin Mills'

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS MARTIN

 BODY:
AT 9 A.M. in Rudy's Bar, Ernest Schroeder sits on a stool counting money. There are many $100 bills, rolls and rolls of quarters, and everything in between. The cash, around $12,000, represents the weekend take from his four Times Square bars.
At a time when saloons everywhere are struggling, Mr. Schroeder says that his establishments -- Rudy's, the Full Moon Saloon, the Holland Bar and the Savoy -- are doing well. The bars are loud, the beer cheap, the food almost free -- and the clientele of a class that would not be terribly affected if the stock market crashed. 
 "These, for the most part, are not dirt-poor people, but most of them are one paycheck away from being on the street," said Mr. Schroeder, who has two co-owners.
So he sells people who can't afford much else a product they either don't need or need too much. Indeed, he calls alcohol "gasoline," for its potential to burn up individuals and their families. He rationalizes that if he didn't do it, somebody else would. He has no trouble articulating a larger purpose.
"Nobody wants to cater to the guy who wants to have a couple beers, read a couple newspapers and pick out a horse," he said. "Everybody in this whole world is trying to be high class, and the truth is we're all descendants of peasants."
It all rings at the old-fashioned cash registers, though it is not clear how much. "We're not on C.O.D., and that tells you something," said Jack Ertl, a co-owner, noting that many competitors now must immediately pay bills to get deliveries.
According to the Alcohol Research Information Service, the number of bars in the nation declined from 158,492 in 1986 to 149,516 in 1992, in New York State from 23,672 to 20,861. Local bar owners say the recession has made the decline much more precipitous in New York City, where rents are higher, though statistics aren't available.
Mr. Schroeder's strategy in bringing in customers is low prices: Draft Bud goes for $1.50 a pint, imported beers for $2.25, standard cocktails $2. "The key to our entire thing is price," he explained.
Mr. Schroeder, 50, rises at 3:30 A.M. so he can be around each bar just after the 4 A.M. closing and handle the money. This done, he takes a nap before the 8 A.M. opening. He then prowls his empire, starting with the Full Moon at 735 Eighth Avenue, which has a 50-foot-long bar, flashing red Christmas tree lights everywhere and, he says, not as many prostitutes as people think. Then there's the Savoy at 355 West 41st Street, a gay bar. And the Holland, at 532 Ninth Avenue, which is less than 10 feet wide, with just 14 stools and much conversation.
At Rudy's, 627 Ninth Avenue, where the trail starts and ends, there is the feeling of a time warp. The little round tables and banquettes date to 1934, and the ceiling fans rotate endlessly. Musicians are big customers, partly because Thelonious Monk and John Coltrane dominate the jukebox.
"The jukebox is a very powerful thing," Mr. Schroeder said. "It controls what kind of crowd you get."
As Mr. Schroeder goes from place to place, his ample belly leads the way and a graying ponytail waves behind. From his belt dangle dozens of keys and a jagged-edged can opener, too big to open a beer but just right to give a mugger pause. He lights a Pall Mall. On Ninth Avenue, he sees Frank, a numbers runner, and wordlessly hands him the usual $20.
"If I get lucky, I get $9,000," he said. "But I ain't gonna get lucky."
If Mr. Schroeder's life seems to contain all the elements of a country song, let it be mentioned that he ran away from his Orange, N.J., home at 14 with his friend Eddie Rabbit, who went on to become a country-singing star. Not that he had an uncomfortable youth as one of seven children of a chemical engineer who designed whisky distilleries.
An uncle owned a bar, and Ernest Schroeder loved going there even as a youngster. "Owning a bar is the only thing I ever wanted to do," he said almost wistfully.
But there were other things he did: Working as an orderly, putting in time in a factory. Earning a degree in business administration from Marist College in Poughkeepsie, N.Y., while working in the Post Office. Buying a Poughkeepsie tavern, the Shamrock.
Then his marriage collapsed and he returned to New Jersey. After stumbling around a bit, he and his brother began buying go-go clubs, ultimately owning six. But they didn't get along, and Mr. Schroeder left his brother with the bars. "Nobody wants to shoot his brother," he explained.
Mr. Schroeder found his way to Times Square and began bartending. Among his stops were the Savoy and Rudy's, under earlier ownership. But it was in a little bar in the same building as the Holland Hotel, then populated by welfare families, that he first became an owner and made his reputation.
At the Holland, he created a neighborhood feeling in an establishment inhabited by people with nowhere else to go. He had free Easter bunnies, cheap Christmas toys and other elements of home. Hot dogs were a nickel, beer a dollar and the boneless chicken dinner, a boiled egg, 9 cents. The pattern was established.
When the bar closed along with the hotel, Mr. Schroeder with his new partners opened the second Holland Bar a few blocks south. A flock of dipsomaniacal disciples followed.
Along the way Mr. Schroeder has learned a thing or two. He trusts no bartender. "There are no honest ones," he said, "only relatively honest ones." He caters to the customer: at the Full Moon, he has written out signs honoring the birth of a nephew of a regular customer. He has learned to guess customers' ages, partly as a result of being closed for 15 days last summer for serving an undercover, under-age policeman.
He has learned that the way to diminish the presence of prostitutes is to require that they pay for drinks and not let them approach customers. He has learned not to have television sets because they diminish conversation -- the best use of a bar, in his experience.
It all works with customers. "The days of the wonderful old local gin mills like this are gone," said Richard Sperle, a Long Island resident who stopped at Rudy's on his way to the American Museum of Natural History.
At a nearby stool, Carol Lopez added, "Beautiful people hang out here, really -- we're a family."
 
BAR HOPPING
How to Make Ends Meet
 
Here is how Ernest Schroeder, co-owner of four bars, keeps his stools full and costs down.

MARKUP: Saloons usually price their drinks 4:1, meaning a bottle of beer purchased wholesale for $1 is sold to the public at $4. Mr. Schroeder's markup is 3:1.

LABOR: To minimize costs, only part-time employees are hired. There are 20, making $32 to $50 for an eight-hour shift, not counting tips.

RENT: The main expense. At the Holland Bar, rent is $1,700 a month, with weekly sales of $2,800; at Rudy's, $3,000 with weekly sales of $5,000; at Full Moon Saloon, $6,000 with $6,500 in sales. Mr. Schroeder declined to reveal sales for the Savoy, bought last March in financial distress; rent is $3,200.

HEADLINE: THE NATION;
Health Plan: Out of the Gate

 BODY:
The Clinton health care plan, at a hefty 1,342 pages and 5 1/2 pounds, thumped into congressional in-baskets on Wednesday. This version differed from the one leaked last month, with revisions designed to increase the odds of the bill's passage. Among them: abortion coverage with a "conscience clause" that would exuse people with objections from having to perform the procedure; subsidies for small businesses with with as many as 75 workers, up from 50; the right to choose a doctor, even one outside the patient's plan. 
 Hilary Rodham Clinton told Congress that the Administration had no "no pride of authorship" and that some details were open to negotiation. These are highlights of the opening round of bidding.
 
UNIVERSAL COVERAGE
All Americans will be covered by health insurance by 1998, regardless of their medical or employment status.
 
People will be able to choose from three types of plans: traditional fee-for-service, health maintenance organizations or a combination of the two. H.M.O.'s will have to offer patients the option to seek care from a physician outside the organization, but this could carry an additional cost.
 
HEALTH ALLIANCES
Insurance will be administered through health alliances, which will pool the purchasing power of customers.
 
Generally, one alliance will cover a state or region. Corporations with more than 5,000 employees will be allowed to create their own alliances.
 
Alliances will negotiate with health providers and offer consumers a choice of plans, with details on each plan's cost and quality. Alliances will be allowed to reject only plans that do not meet state standards or ones with budgets that are 20 percent higher than the average of other plans in the area.
 
COST OF COVERAGE
Businesses will pay at least 80 percent of the cost of premiums for employees.
 
Employees will pay the portion of premiums that employers don't cover. The cost formula will be based on how expensive a plan they choose, with out-of-pocket expenses higher for those who are not in an H.M.O.
 
Costs for small businesses will be subsidized by the Federal Government. Caps on premium costs will range from 3.5 percent of total payroll for small businesses to 7.9 percent for large one's.
 
COST CONTROL
Insurance premiums will be controlled by law over five years to bring growth in health costs in line with general inflation rates.
 
A natioanl health board will set national and regional budgets.

HEADLINE: An Emergency Unit With Children in Mind

BYLINE: By JULIE MILLER

 BODY:
MARQUIS JONES'S mouth drooped as he lay on an examining table in the emergency room of the children's unit of Yale-New Haven Hospital.
 "Show me where it hurts," the doctor said to the 7-year-old as her hands gently probed his belly. 
Marquis's mother, Valerie Jones, brought her son to the emergency room after he began vomiting and complaining of fatigue and headaches. "You're in a lot quicker than you would be if children were mixed with adults," she said, referring to the children's unit emergency room. "And there's less commotion for the kids to have to go through."
 
Designed for Small Patients
In other ways, too, the emergency room is equipped with children in mind. Blood-pressure cuffs, breathing tubes and other medical devices are available in various sizes to fit children of different ages. Similarly, the monitoring equipment is fitted with cables appropriate for small patients.
 But in many places across the nation children's needs in emergency care have been overlooked, while services for injured or seriously ill adults have improved, a professor of pediatrics at Harvard Medical School, Dr. Donald N. Medearis Jr., said in a report on emergency medical services for children. The report, published last July by the Institute of Medicine, a unit of the National Academy of Sciences, called for widespread improvements in children's emergency care. The report also recommended that Congress appropriate $30 million a year for five years to support state and Federal emergency service programs for children.
 Dr. Medearis is chairman of a 19-member committee that studied pediatric emergency medical services for two years and prepared the report. Noting that "children are not simply little adults," he explained that a child's normal respiration rate, heart rate and blood pressure are different from an adult's. Changes in those vital signs that indicate deterioration in adults may not be present in children. For this reason, treating children requires instruments of different sizes, different types and doses of medicine and psychological support appropriate for their age.
 
'Difficult to Evaluate'
 Caring for seriously ill or injured children also demands special diagnostic skills, said the director of pediatric emergency services at the children's unit, Dr. David Bachman. A 6-month-old with abdominal pain, for instance, could be suffering from any of a number of problems. "It's difficult to evaluate, because the child can't communicate, and some professionals get nervous about treating a child," Dr. Bachman said.
 Although ambulance services and hospital emergency rooms are assumed to be equally capable of caring for children and adults, it is not true, the report stated. In addition to better training for medical professionals who treat children, the report recommended that emergency rooms and ambulances be equipped with supplies and medical devices for children. Cervical collars used for patients with neck injuries, for instance, are not always available in children's sizes and can interfere with breathing.
 In Connecticut, state regulations require all ambulances to carry equipment in children's sizes, and the vehicles are inspected annually, said the chief of field services in the State Office of Emergency Medical Services, Marie Wilson. Emergency medical technicians must take a course that includes pediatric emergency care. "But it's not as extensive as most of us would like to see," Ms. Wilson said.
 
Regulations Under Consideration
 All hospital emergency rooms in the state are prepared to treat children, said a spokeswoman for the Connecticut Hospital Association, Julie Cox. She added that the State Office of Emergency Medical Services is preparing regulations under which a critically injured child would be considered for transfer from a local hospital to one with a pediatric intensive-care unit.
 Information on the number of children treated each year in emergency rooms across the nation is not available, Dr. Medearis said, although the report by the Institute of Medicine stated that children account for an estimated 25 to 35 percent of all emergency room visits.
 "What we do know is that some 21,000 children and young people under age 20 died in 1988 alone as a result of injuries," Dr. Medearis said. "That same year, nearly 21,000 more deaths occurred because of illness and other disorders."
 Injury is the leading cause of death in children more than a year old; for younger children, the major causes are developmental disabilities, birth defects, genetic problems and prematurity, said the chief and chairman of pediatrics at Yale-New Haven Hospital and the Yale School of Medicine, Dr. Joseph B. Warshaw. Since the children's unit opened last July, Dr. Bachman said, about 50 youngsters a day have been treated in the emergency room for head injuries, burns, lacerations, broken bones, asthma and flu. Automobile and bicycle accidents, drownings and poisonings are high on the list of the most common emergencies. In addition, increases in child abuse and the number of shootings and assaults among young people have placed greater demands on emergency services in general.
 
Few Training Programs
 Despite the growing need, there are few training programs in pediatric emergency care because it is an area of medicine that is still developing, Dr. Warshaw said. A survey of 85 percent of the emergency medicine residency programs in the United States in 1980 showed that an average of four months was dedicated to pediatrics, Drs. Stephen Ludwig and Gary R. Fleisher, authors of "Textbook of Pediatric Emergency Medicine," reported. Residents trained in the programs often had difficulty distinguishing a child with meningitis, or inflammation of the membranes surrounding the brain and spinal cord, from one with a high fever caused by an ear infection, they added.
 Now, however, pediatric emergency medicine is a recognized sub-specialty of pediatrics and emergency medicine, Dr. Ludwig and Dr. Fleisher wrote.
 In the future, hospitals that serve as regional centers for pediatric emergency services may be the most economical way of providing better care for Connecticut's children, said James L. Paturas, the director of emergency services at Bridgeport Hospital and a member of the committee that prepared the Institute of Medicine report.
 
Preventing Injuries
 Mr. Paturas and Dr. Bachman emphasized the importance of teaching children and their families to prevent injuries. Keeping poisons out of reach and insisting that children wear helmets while they're riding their bicycles are two of the most important precautions, Dr. Bachman said.
 Mr. Paturas expressed the hope that safety procedures will be passed down from one generation to the next. "You can't say enough about it," he said. "There's nothing we would like more than to be put out of business. The greatest asset we have as a nation is our young people."

CORRECTION-DATE: October 31, 1993, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
Because of an editing error, an article on Oct. 31 about the treatment of children in hospital emergency rooms misattributed a statement that Connecticut was preparing regulations on transferring injured children to pediatric intensive-care units. Marie Wilson of the State Office of Emergency Medical Services made the statement, not Julie Cox of the Connecticut Hospital Association.

LENGTH: 1836 words

HEADLINE: Upstart Airline Staying on Course

BYLINE: By BARBARA STURKEN PETERSON

DATELINE: NEWARK

 BODY:
IT is half past noon on a busy Monday at Newark International Airport. From his office across the road from the runway, Robert Iverson surveys the jets lining up for takeoff and consults his watch. It is almost departure time for Flight 9 to Orlando, Fla., on Kiwi International Airlines, the upstart airline he founded.
What Mr. Iverson, Kiwi's chairman, sees from his window, though, is a sobering view of his competition. In the last year, he has witnessed other newcomers open for business across the way, only to close down shortly thereafter. And the dozens of Continental Airlines jets parked on the tarmac at Newark are more evidence of the odds against Kiwi. 
 Earlier this year, Mr. Iverson accused the much larger Continental of trying to drive his airline out of business with predatory, or below-cost, prices. He did not file formal charges against Continental, however, because, he said, hefelt that such a case would be too difficult and costly to prove. Continental denied the accusations.
But even though skeptics predicted that Kiwi could not last against bigger and more experienced rivals, especially in a highly competitive market like Newark Airport, the airline recently marked its first anniversary and recorded a modest profit during the months of July and August.
"We are here because we've learned the lessons of those who have failed," said Mr. Iverson, who worked as a commercial airline pilot for nearly 20 years. "Those airlines that strayed from their original niche are no longer here."
 
The Budget Travel Market
 By his reckoning, Kiwi's niche is serving the budget travel market; it has lower costs than its larger rivals and can afford to charge lower prices. Kiwi also has at least one advantage over other fledgling airlines: financial support from the State of New Jersey.
Kiwi received a $1.7 million loan guarantee from the state's Economic Development Authority, which it redeemed at the Summit Trust Company. The airline has already repaid $700,000 of the loan and recently renegotiated a revolving line of credit with the bank so it can draw on the full $1.7 million amount.
"That's a unique angle," said John Eichner, chairman of Simat, Helliesen & Eichner, a New York aviation consulting concern, and a longtime airline analyst. "I don't know of any state that has done that with a start up airline."
Mr. Eichner said that while the odds against a small airline are daunting, Kiwi "is doing as well as can be expected." He added, "They've got the initial growing pains that any start-up airline might have."
Among the problems any new airline can face is cutthroat price competition from established lines. Kiwi operates 12 departures daily out of Newark to six cities, a tiny fraction of the 550 flights that depart the airport on various airlines each day. Even so, when a new airline cuts fares, competing airlines usually lower theirs to match the newcomer's prices and protect their share of the market.
When Kiwi started flying to Atlanta and Orlando, for example, Delta Air Lines lowered fares to match the newcomer's prices of about $100 one way. Continental did the same when Kiwi entered its turf. The difference, Mr. Iverson said, is that Kiwi charges low fares for all its seats, while the larger airlines tend to restrict the number of seats offered at a discount.
 
'He's Got a Chance'
 "I think he's got a chance to get the larger carriers to back off and to at least survive," Mr. Eichner said. "He seems to be getting by, and has at least enough cash to have his planes in the air."
Several other start-up lines were less fortunate. They either proposed or actually started service to Newark, but expired in the last year, said Vincent Bonaventura, general manager of the airport.
Skybus, a low-fare airline to Florida, and Ultrair, a luxury Houston-based line, both suspended service after a few months in business. Family Airlines, formed by a group of former Pan Am workers, announced a start-up date for coast-to-coast budget flights but failed to pass muster with the Federal Government. The latest casualty was a low-fare upstart named Jet U.S., which collapsed after seven days of service between Detroit and Newark last August.
"Ever since deregulation, it has been very difficult for new airlines attempting to enter the business," Mr. Bonaventura said. Many of the recent failures were victims of a tough economic climate, which has battered airlines both large and small.
Kiwi "seems to have the right balance between caution and aggressiveness," he said. "And it has an organization that is made up of people with many years of experience."
 
A Lot of Experience
 Kiwi's 620 employees possess an unusual amount of airline experience for a young company, averaging 20 years' worth. Virtually all them hail from three defunct airlines: Pan Am, Eastern and Midway. In part to spoof their grounded status, the founding employees chose to name the airline for the flightless Kiwi bird.
Many of those idled by the airline bankruptcies had few options for other employment; in recent years even financially healthy airlines have laid off workers. Mr. Iverson said he had tried to hire the most senior employees he could find; four pilots have already retired at the federally mandated age of 60 since the airline's startup.
For most of the workers, accustomed to large bureaucratic organizations, Kiwi is a sharp contrast. Paul Clements, director of sales development, and a resident of River Vale, worked at Trans World Airlines for 30 years before joining Kiwi in July.
Although T.W.A. is still flying, until recently it, too, was embroiled in lengthy bankruptcy proceedings, and Mr. Clements said he had left because of the uncertain atmosphere and plummeting morale there. Although a new airline is risky, he said, he was encouraged by the fact that many of Kiwi's workers volunteered their time for tasks that were not in their job descriptions.
 
Other Roles for Pilots
 "Here you have a very involved group trying to make this thing work," he said. He compared it to the early days of aviation, when airline executives were more likely to be pilots than financial experts.
He is often accompanied by a group of pilots when making sales calls at large companies, he said, which would rarely occur at a more traditionally structured airline.
Seasoned pilots can be found at Kiwi performing such unlikely chores as cleaning the interiors of the aircraft they fly. They also earn far less: Kiwi pays a captain $60,000 a year, versus more than $100,000 at big airlines.
The company was also able to keep costs down by securing aircraft leases at favorable rates because of a surplus of planes. That means it can make money by filling just over half of its seats, a threshold many larger airlines would envy.
It was because of such efficiencies that the airline was able to report its first operating profit in July, after nine months of service. By the third week of September, it had carried a half-million passengers, and was taking in more than $7.5 million in sales on a monthly basis, up from $1 million at its startup. Its fleet has grown, from two to eight jets, and its work force has tripled.
The airline serves Atlanta; Chicago; Tampa, Fla.; San Juan, P.R., and Orlando, Fla. It is scheduled to add twice-daily service between West Palm Beach, Fla., and Newark tomorrow.
 
Taking a Cautious Approach
 Kiwi will add to its fleet and route map cautiously, Mr. Iverson said. "Growth, that's a scary word," he said, citing the grim record for airlines like People Express that collapsed after wildly expanding in the mid-1980's.
Mr. Iverson witnessed firsthand the upheaval in the business as a commercial pilot for Eastern Air Lines for 18 years. He walked off his job along with other striking pilots and mechanics at the troubled airline in March 1989.
After working briefly for Pan American World Airways, which, like Eastern, expired in 1991, Mr. Iverson began to explore starting up an airline from scratch with a group of fellow pilots who had worked at Eastern.
Banks were reluctant to lend money to a new airline, given the unencouraging track records of such ventures. Kiwi's main source of capital came from 100 pilots who each put up $50,000 for a stake in the new company on top of the $2 million it privately raised from employees plus an additional $2 million in seed money from outside investors and the aid from New Jersey.
Kiwi, in fact, says it is the only jet airline to be incorporated and have its headquarters in New Jersey. (People Express was based at Newark but was incorporated in Delaware.)
Newark Airport is also within easy driving range of one of the largest airline market in the country; it serves some 25 million passengers a year. While the airport is close to New York City, Kiwi estimates that 80 percent of its customers who originate their trips at Newark are New Jersey residents.
On most flights, Kiwi has two fares; there are as many as a dozen on other airlines' flights. The lowest price is for a restricted ticket requiring a round-trip purchase; the higher fare carries no limits. For example, the cheapest ticket on Kiwi to Chicago is $104 one way, with restrictions, and $119 unrestricted.
 
'They Like the Low Fares'
 Joyce Liebelt, a travel agent with VTS Travel Enterprises in Secaucus, said a number of her clients had been pleased with Kiwi. "They like the low fares," she said. "I haven't heard any raves about their service, but I haven't heard any complaints either."
Bill Mosley, a spokesman for the United States Department of Transportation in Washington, said the agency had no record of complaints against Kiwi. The agency releases statistics on complaints against a given airline only when there are more than five recorded in any one-month period.
Although comparisons with discount airlines like the ill-fated People Express are inevitable, Mr. Iverson pointed out that unlike his no-frills predecessors, Kiwi offered full meal and beverage service.
Kiwi's office could fairly be described as no-frills, however. The Newark-based reservations and management staff occupies part of a floor of the Hemisphere Center office building on Route 9 overlooking the airport. In a large room, desks are jammed next to each other, and private offices seem to be nonexistent.
Mr. Iverson works in a corner space; his daughter, Kelly, who works as his secretary, sits nearby. Reservations agents work in a smaller room down the hall.
A papier-mache kiwi bird, fashioned by a pilot who moonlights as an artist, greets visitors to the headquarters. With the staff ever mindful of the fate of so many similar airline ventures, this bit of self-deprecating humor seems to set the tone for the operation.
And although the airline has passed the one-year mark, employees still gather at their windows when a colleague shouts out, "Kiwi alert." It means one of their planes has been spotted in the skies above.

HEADLINE: New Jersey Q & A: John J. O'Brien;
Staying on Top of Newspaper Trends

BYLINE: By ARTHUR Z. KAMIN

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
JOHN J. O'BRIEN, the executive director of the New Jersey Press Association, has come a long way since his days of sweeping the floors in the press room at the weekly Hunterdon County Democrat in Flemington 28 years ago.
Though it seems odd now, he thought he had "reached the pinnacle" of his career when the paper quickly promoted him to Linotype operator in the composing room, setting news articles and advertisements in hot metal. 
 "I was happy," said Mr. O'Brien, 46. "But I knew, deep down, that Linotype wasn't going to be around too much longer. Times were starting to change."
He was right, of course. The line-casting machine became a relic as new technology slowly found its way into newspapers. Mr. O'Brien moved into other areas of the business -- circulation, editorial and advertising.
Late in 1975, he joined the Somerset Press newspapers in Somerville, where eventually he became publisher. Malcolm Forbes bought the operation in 1987 -- and two years later Mr. O'Brien was promoted to president of what became known as Forbes Newspapers.
For the last two years, he has headed the Trenton-based Press Association, founded in 1857, a trade organization of New Jersey's 20 daily and 105 weekly paid-circulation newspapers and 3 out-of-state affiliated members.
It has not been an entirely happy time. With the woeful state economy, the newspaper business has seen ad revenue decline even as the industry has been undergoing ever greater technological changes and sharp competition, often from sources other than rival newspapers. Mr. O'Brien has emerged as the state newspaper industry's chief spokesman.
"Sometimes I feel like a point man, a Green Beret," Mr. O'Brien said. "It's my job to move out to see what lies ahead and to cover our flanks at all times. It's not easy, but we're doing the job."
The association says it has already won a compromise victory by obtaining the establishment of ground rules for New Jersey Bell's participation in electronic publishing in the state.
The situation today makes Mr. O'Brien think often of that hulking Linotype -- and how newspapers must again change their ways not only to survive but to march into the next century.
That's one reason, he said, that the Press Association set up a "Future of Newspapering in New Jersey" committee, headed by E. Donald Lass, publisher and editor of The Asbury Park Press. And on the schedule for the association's annual convention last week in Cherry Hill were experts from all over the country with such topics as "The New Media Landscape," "Entering the Brave New World" and "Information Services on a Shoestring."
Despite the recession gloom, a new newspaper came on the scene on Sept. 24 -- the West Long Branch-based New Jersey Times, a tabloid weekly seeking a statewide circulation.
Mr. O'Brien lives in Kingwood Township with his wife, Chris. They have two grown children.
Though Mr. O'Brien's job involves frequent travel up and down the state, he tries to find time to ride motorcycles in the summer and snowmobiles in the winter -- and, as he puts it, to "recuperate from both in the spring and fall."
Q. The New Jersey economy continues to struggle. What does that mean for New Jersey newspapers in terms of advertising?
A. New Jersey is probably the most competitive newspaper market in America. Because 98 percent of the electronic media serving the state are based outside New Jersey, New Jersey newspapers are virtually the only providers of statewide and local news. Thus, intense readership occurs, and also intense competition for those readers. A down economy that affects ad budgets hits hard in this competitive climate.
Q. How about circulation? Is newspaper readership in the state on an upward or a downward trend?
A. I suspect there are as many or more people reading newspapers in New Jersey than there were 10 or 15 years ago. Our numbers have not kept pace with population growth, but remember, a large percent of our population does not read and speak English.
Q. How many daily newspapers were there in the state 10 years ago? How many are there today?
A. There are four fewer daily newspapers today than 10 years ago. Today's number stands at 20.
Q. Weekly newspapers? Is the total up or down?
A. There are more weekly newspapers than 10 years ago but fewer owners. A total of 105 paid circulation weeklies belong to our association.
Q. What is the circulation of your smallest member and your largest? Do they have very different needs and interests?
A. The Star-Ledger Sunday circulation is approximately 725,000. The weekly Vailsburg Leader serves about 300 households. There are more problems and obstacles that face large and small papers collectively than there are those that affect them individually. Things like First Amendment infringements, recycling issues and government regulations impact all New Jersey newspapers.
Q. There is a perception that the day of newspapers, as we know them today, may be coming to an end. Is there any truth to that?
A. Our industry is going through what I call "mediamorphosis." We're not dying. We're changing.
Q. We hear talk about electronic newspapers. What is the impact for New Jersey?
A. This change we're about to undergo is both exciting and frightening. The new media landscape is being created before our very eyes. Newspaper publishers must remember to protect their franchise as the electronic world develops. That franchise is news.
No one does news better than newspapers. All the other players out there -- cable companies, telephone companies, wireless companies -- are all building highways, but highways need traffic. We own the traffic.
Q. There's a perception that investigative journalism has become a casualty of the economy. Many newspapers do not seem to want to dig as deeply as they once did to get the story and to expose wrongdoing. Investigative journalism is costly and many newspapers do not want to offend readers. Is that true?
A. The lousy economy in New Jersey has claimed many victims. One of them has been editorial department budgets. To do true investigative pieces, you may have to commit one or two reporters for months. This is expensive.
But it is still going on, especially at the large newspapers. I don't think offending the readers enters into the equation. Offending advertisers might be a better question.
Q. Politicians like to get their stories into newspapers -- but when it comes time to advertise their election campaigns, they choose radio and television. Why?
A. I'm dumbfounded as to why anyone would spend $5 million on New York and Philadelphia TV when almost 70 percent of the people viewing the commercials can't vote in New Jersey.
The problem is the so-called "media advisers" are all ex-TV producers. They'll talk about gross rating points and frequency and impact. All nebulous terms. The real problem is that they are image builders. Politicians today worry about image -- not substance. Newspapers are media of substance.
Q. It appears that the day of the teen-age carrier, who delivered newspapers to your doorstep or sold them on the streets in all kinds of weather, is gone. What happened?
A. When I delivered The Newark News in 1957, I could save my pennies and buy a new pair of Keds after a few weeks. If a kid wants to buy $120 Nikes, he'll have to sell a lot of newspapers to make that purchase. Economics, coupled with papers switching to A.M. delivery, have pretty much cut kids out of the loop.
Q. What efforts are being made in New Jersey to get more minorities into journalism?
A. There are several excellent programs. Dow Jones has a national minorities program based in Princeton. Rider College runs an excellent Minorities Journalism Workshop each summer for high school journalists. We're not doing enough. Part of N.J.P.A.'s agenda is to do more.
Q. The New Jersey Press Association is also a lobbying organization, especially in dealing with the Governor and legislators. Is this a role you feel comfortable with?
A. Senator Alan Cranston said it best: "When it comes to lobbying -- take part or get taken apart." My responsibility at N.J.P.A. includes lobbying on the state level. With the average state legislator being exposed to 4,000 or more bills in a session, the only way they can possibly know the pros and cons of each is to rely on lobbyists to educate them.
My job is not the easiest in Trenton. While most lobbyists represent firms that contribute to legislators' war chests and wine and dine them, the businesses I represent write editorials telling them they are idiots. Then I lobby them. Not an advantageous position.
Q. What do you see as the biggest problem facing New Jersey newspapers today?
A. There are many problems: Government infringement, mandated recycling, changing reading habits. But by far the black cloud on the horizon is the way people are changing their buying habits.
Since 85 to 90 percent of a newspaper's revenue comes from advertising and that field may change dramatically in the coming years, there's cause for concern. Interactivity -- being able to bypass the traditional retailer and deal directly electronically with the manufacturer -- will change the face of marketing.

GRAPHIC: Photo: John J. O'Brien, head of the New Jersey Press Association. (Frank C. Dougherty for The New York Times)

LENGTH: 1788 words

HEADLINE: Choosing Sides In the Way We Shop

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER

 BODY:
IN Westchester, where crowds of savvy consumers have increasingly been drawn to a growing lineup of sleek regional discount stores along the highways, some shoppers still hew to the old ways, saying they prefer the quiet, albeit dowdier, aisles of their local Woolworth store.
"Coming here is a lot like coming home," said Adele Piacquadio, a 72-year-old resident of the Bronx who was buying a lampshade at the Woolworth store in Eastchester recently. "Maybe it's force of habit, but I grew up with Woolworth, and I like growing old with it." 
 But Woolworth's sales receipts earlier this year showed that the American retail icon founded more than 100 years ago was not growing old gracefully. In the second quarter of this year, net income was $2 million compared with net income of $33 million in the same period last year. Revenues, however, for the second quarter actually increased slightly, to $2.28 billion from $2.23 billion. An overall increase in the number of stores, coupled with lower-than-planned revenues, resulted in higher inventory and debt levels.
Despite the many shoppers who remain loyal to the chain, even in the face of mounting competition from discounters, the Manhattan-based Woolworth Corporation earlier this month announced that in the United States it would shutter 400, or about half, of its variety stores, along with 330, or about a third, of its Kinney and Footquarters shoe stores.
The company said that in both the United States and Canada, the 970 general merchandising and specialty stores that are to close represent $36 million in operating losses for the first half of 1993. .
Three of Westchester's nine Woolworths -- in Bronxville, Ossining and Tarrytown -- are among those to be shut. Two Woolworth stores at the Cross County Mall in Yonkers, another in that city on South Broadway, the Eastchester Woolworth on Post Road, the company's White Plains store on Mamaroneck Avenue and its Peekskill store on East Main Street will stay open, the company said.
"The continuation of unfavorable economic conditions in the United States and Canada, along with competitive pressures affecting customers' shopping habits and the generally depressed consumer-spending environment, have led us to accelerate our store closings," said William K. Lavin, Woolworth's chairman and chief executive officer. In an earlier interview he said that third-quarter earnings were expected to fall sharply from last year's figure.
Woolworth's decline may reflect the end of one merchandising era, but it is taking place as another retail trend is on the rise. In Westchester, along with other communities across the country, deep discounters are gaining a strong foothold despite -- and possibly because of -- the hard economic times. The discounters, including so-called big-box retailers -- like Tops Appliance City, which sell goods from warehouse-like stores, are playing to recession-weary consumers who want the best buy for their dollar.
 
County Is Now Affordable
 What is different in Westchester is that the discount trend has taken hold here somewhat later than it did in the rest of the country, said Noreen L. Preston, an economist for the County Office of Economic Development. Now that real estate prices and interest rates have dropped, discount retailers are finally able to afford outlets in a county that heretofore has been known for its high-priced land, she said.
"The discounters up to now have been slow to come here, especially the big-box retailers who tend to be stand-alones that require huge amounts of land for parking spaces," Ms. Preston said.
Joseph E. Phelan, budget director for the county, said that while he was "sorry to see some Woolworths go," the influx of the deep-discount retailers was good news for Westchester sales revenues.
"This kind of business can do nothing but good for us," Mr. Phelan said, adding that the county would likely exceed its original projection of $170 million in sales-tax revenues this year because of the discount stores.
Keith Foxe, a researcher for the International Council of Shopping Centers in Manhattan, called Westchester, with its high per capita income and high density per square mile, "under-stored compared to other areas in big markets."
 
Looking for Amenities
 The Office of Economic Development recently reported that since the third quarter of last year, six major discounters have opened new outlets in Westchester: Barnes & Noble in Hartsdale, Bradlees in Yonkers, the Pace Membership Warehouse in Elmsford, T. J. Maxx in Yorktown Heights, Tops Appliance City in Hawthorne and World of Interior Design in White Plains. Even more discounters are expected to move into the county next year, including the Home Depot, a home-improvement chain.
Discount-hungry consumers may want rock-bottom prices, but market research has shown that they also look for amenities like food courts, bright lighting and easy-to-navigate shopping areas, said Coleman F. Nee, a spokesman for Bradlees (based in Braintree, Mass.), which opened its first Westchester store earlier this year on Central Park Avenue in Yonkers.
Bradlees, a $1.8 billion-a-year general merchandiser with 126 stores in eight states, also plans to open a 125,000-square-foot store in New Rochelle next year.
At the new 207,000 square-foot, three-story Bradlees in Yonkers, the site of a former Alexander's, the decor is super modern and user friendly -- with wide aisles, bright lighting, pristine white-tile floors and comfortable areas of gray carpeting.
"We went for broke with this store," said Roy Polayes, the manager, pointing to neatly defined retail areas within the store and bulging racks of clothing with both brand-name and private-label merchandise. "We have a great selection and all price points -- good, better, best, you name it."
 
Like Hot Dogs and Apple Pie
 By contrast, at the 30-year-old Woolworth in Eastchester, boxes of laundry detergent were stacked haphazardly next to framed Snow White posters, and the stock along the narrow aisles was depleted in many sections of the store. But at the checkout counter, Denise Shanahan, a clerk, said the store's appearance did not seem to keep customers away.
"People still keep saying, 'Thank God, you're not closing,' " Ms. Shanahan said.
One couple shopping at the store, Dominic and Ceil Berardi of Eastchester, said that so long as Woolworth stayed open, they would remain loyal. "This place is like hot dogs and apple pie," said Mr. Berardi, who is 65. "It's American, and we love it."
But Mr. Polayes said Bradlees was an all-American store too, combining the best of both worlds. He called it "the 90's equivalent" of the old-fashioned variety store.
"Only instead of hot dogs, it's tacos and pizza in our food court," he said. "And instead of apple pie, it's bagels and croissants. We may be big, but we are also warm and friendly."
In Hawthorne, Tops Appliance City, a chain that sells a broad range of appliances and consumer electronics, based in Edison, N.J., recently opened what it calls a megastore -- a 65,000-square-foot outlet larger than the usual 30,000- to 40,000-square-foot superstore, said Philip Schoonover, the company's executive vice president.
 
'Demographics Were Right'
 The Hawthorne store is the first in Westchester for Tops Appliance City, a $400 million-a-year retailer with seven outlets. The company's research studies showed that Westchester was understored and ripe for development of discount retailing, Mr. Schoonover said.
"The demographics were right," he said. "We look for an average household income of $57,000, a family size of four and a head of household who is about 37 years old."
The pricing is deep discount and aims for high sales volumes for every square foot of selling space -- "a formula that seems to thrive in tough economic times," Mr. Schoonover said.
But all is not bare bones inside the store, where the atmosphere is bright, with high ceilings and spacious aisles. A model of a pink Cadillac is suspended above the car-stereo section, "like the Hard Rock Cafe," Mr. Schoonover said.
Although consumers and the county's budget director welcomed the large discounters, some residents and small-business owners said there was a negative side to all of the growth. In Port Chester and Yonkers, where Home Depot hopes to locate two of its first three Westchester stores, opposition to those plans has been fierce. The $7 billion retailer, based in Atlanta, has more than 230 stores in 21 states, said Nell B. Parker, a spokeswoman for the company.
Joseph Chira, chairman of the Rye Citizens Committee, a public interest group, said that big so-called magnet discounters, which draw from a broad marketing area, often have a negative impact on nearby residential areas because the stores attract more traffic to side streets than the streets were designed to handle.
Port Chester has approved the Home Depot's application to build where an office complex stands, close to major highways. But Mr. Chira's group has filed two lawsuits in State Supreme Court in White Plains, claiming that Port Chester's Planning Commission did not conduct the proper environmental impact studies, among other things. Also the City of Rye has sued Port Chester, claiming that rezoning to accommodate the discounter could result in unsafe traffic conditions on nearby streets in Rye.
 
'It's All a Lot of Hype'
 In Yonkers, Herbert Lockwood, president of Citizens, Businesses and Labor for Better Planning in Westchester and the owner of Lockwood Building Supply and Home Center on the Saw Mill River Road in Yonkers, said his organization opposed city-approved plans for a Home Depot, a Walmart and other discounters on Austin Avenue.
Mr. Lockwood said his group is concerned that there may be hazardous wastes in the ground from a former dump and that fumes like methane gas might leak into the stores; he also questioned the discounters' claims that they will generate new jobs and tax revenues.
"They will only take away from existing businesses, but it won't mean more for the city in the long run. It's all a lot of hype," Mr. Lockwood said.
In addition to the Port Chester and Yonkers stores, the Home Depot also plans to put a store in New Rochelle.
The Office of Economic Development had some survival suggestions for small businesses that will be threatened by the discounters. Ms. Preston recommended that owners try to "fill in the gaps" that big discounters sometimes overlook. She said they should emphasize service and offer specialty or unusual goods that the discounters do not carry.
"But no matter what," Ms. Preston said, "it's going to be a tough time ahead for them."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Shoppers at the Bradlees store on Central Avenue in Yonkers as they perused isles stocked with merchandise (pg. 1); The Woolworth store on White Plains Road in Eastchester. The chain has lost shoppers to large discount stores. (Chris Maynard for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: County To Buy Mt. Vernon Stadium

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER

DATELINE: MOUNT VERNON

 BODY:
UNDER a plan developed by Mayor Ronald A. Blackwood of Mount Vernon and County Executive Andrew P. O'Rourke, this city's aging Memorial Field Stadium will be renovated and made available for both countywide and local sporting and entertainment events by the spring of 1996.
The county has agreed to purchase the rundown stadium -- built in 1930 -- along with the adjoining field and to spend $6 million to refurbish the six-acre site, Mr. O'Rourke said. When completed, it will be the largest and most modern sports stadium in the county. 
 The County Executive also explained that acquisition of the field coincides with plans to build a major retail development across the street from the stadium on Sanford Boulevard. Halpern Enterprises in Tarrytown is negotiating to raze three buildings there and build a new structure that would house a major discount retailer. The developer is also planning parking for up to 700 cars.
Mount Vernon's comptroller, Peter P. Pucillo, called the Sandford Boulevard corridor "ripe for development." In addition to the developer's plan, the United States Postal Service is moving a nearby distribution center to Harrison in two years, and that site could become available for commercial development, the comptroller said.
 
Appraisal Awaited
 Under the agreement, the county will build a state-of-the-art graded and artificially surfaced competitive playing field for soccer, football, baseball and field hockey at Memorial Stadium. And it will refurbish the existing 3,900-seat concrete bleachers and build a grandstand to accommodate 6,000 spectators.
Also, the locker rooms, restrooms and electrical systems at the stadium are to be renovated.
The county's purchase price has yet to be decided, subject to an appraisal of the property. Once ownership is transferred from the city to the county, the county's Department of Parks, Recreation and Conservation will oversee activities there. Existing tennis courts adjacent to the field will remain under the jurisdiction of the city.
Under the agreement, yearly Mount Vernon events are to be given preference in scheduling by the County Parks Department, and local residents who use the field for recreational purposes like jogging will be able to continue to do so.
 
A Saving in Operating Expenses
 Also under the agreement, the city has the option to buy back the field.
Mr. Pucillo said that budget problems kept Mount Vernon from tackling the renovations on its own.
"We have too many other pressing needs, and we have to keep cutting back and cutting back," he said. "We barely have enough money to pay for police and fire protection."
He said the city had a major tax increase this year "just to pay for basic services, and we certainly couldn't do that again for this."
In addition to saving up to $100,000 in yearly operating expenses, the city will receive a percentage of sales-tax revenues from concessions and tickets, said Constance G. Post, the city's Commissioner of Planning and Community Development.
 
'Brand New Money'
 She said that several factors -- including the continued deterioration of the stadium, the county's ongoing interest in the property over several years and plans to develop the large shopping area across the street -- had influenced the city's decision to move ahead on the deal.
The commissioner said there would be an initial injection of cash from the sale of the stadium and more money later from attendance at stadium events.
"We're not talking about recycled money, that moves from one side of town to the other," she said. "We're talking about brand new money from people who don't usually come to Mount Vernon but who will visit now and also shop across the street. It's going to be an economic shot in the arm for us." ELSA BRENNER
