HEADLINE: A Long Road on Affordable Housing

BYLINE: By MERRI ROSENBERG

DATELINE: WHITE PLAINS

 BODY:
A RECENT proposal to build two five-unit apartment buildings for homeless families on a residential stretch of the Post Road here drew protests from some members of neighborhood associations a few weeks ago and highlighted the tensions surrounding the issue of affordable housing in the county.
These units were proposed by the nonprofit housing organization known as Shore -- the acronym stands for Sheltering Homeless Is Our Responsibility -- and were targeted for homeless families or others who qualified. 
 Jane Perkinson, chairwoman of the permanent housing committee of Shore, said: "The state-of-emergency housing has improved in the county, with a number of good facilities during the past four or five years as people have become more aware of the problem. The hitch is that the supply of permanent housing isn't there, and that is a major need. And each time it dwindles, it isn't replaced."
Susan Case, a resident of the Prospect Park section of White Plains where the proposed apartments would be built, said: "There is a sense that nobody wants things in their backyard, but I think we have a real responsibility to pay attention to the problems of society and not block these efforts. I understand the fears, that people hear the word 'homeless' and hear something different from what I hear. But I think Shore is very impressive, very responsible and very sensitive to neighborhood needs. They screen their tenants carefully."
 
Commuting Is an Alternative
 Despite the common perception that Westchester is an enclave of affluent single-family homes on one-acre lots, the reality is that even many county employees can't afford to live here -- nor can many who were reared here continue to live in the same communities with their families, unless they share housing. According to 1990 housing data, the latest figures available, 14,116 county residents were living in overcrowded conditions.
The Shore program is similar to, but not the same as, a county plan for affordable housing put forth by County Executive Andrew P. O'Rourke and yet to be adopted by the County Board of Legislators. As a step toward possible acceptance of the plan, a report on "Affordable Housing Allocation Plan" for Westchester County was adopted unanimously on July 15 by the county's Housing Implementation Commission and presented to the Board.
The report is being studied by urban planners at the Rutgers University Center for Urban Policy Research in New Brunswick, N.J. It outlines the housing needs of families living or working in Westchester and having incomes from $29,431 to the median county income of $58,862. Some of the proposed housing will have units that can be rented, and in those cases the proposed rental range is $600 to $1,000 a month.
With rentals frequently expensive and hard to come by and purchase prices beyond the budget of most working people, municipal workers, county staff members, teachers and hospital workers often commute from Rockland or Putnam Counties, and many retirees, unless they live in houses with paid-up mortgages, are unable to remain in the communities in which they have spent most of their lives. Even some retirees with paid-up mortgages are forced to move because of high taxes or other high costs of living.
 
Court Action Remains a Threat
 After Mr. O'Rourke proposed building 5,000 affordable housing units in the county over a 10-year period, he appointed the Housing Implementation Commission. It consists of 22 advocates of affordable housing, who were charged with assessing the need for permanent housing as well as providing voluntary guidelines to help municipalities respond to court pressure. The commission suggested that housing be distributed according to a municipality's population, individual household income, existence of overcrowded housing and usable municipal land for housing projects.
George M. Raymond, chairman of the Housing Implementation Commission, said: "The county won't go into any municipality against its wishes. There is no compulsion in this concept. These are just guidelines. There is a strong need to provide affordable housing for young people who are just getting started, senior citizens whose income is insufficient to afford housing and all the workers -- janitors, waitresses, municipal workers, single parents -- who need places to live.
"The court has ruled in 1977 that a municipality has a responsibility to meet its share of regional needs for affordable housing, as does the 1990 case against North Salem. If municipalities don't do this voluntarily, then the courts can charge them. There is a real judicial threat to municipal independence. The process is slow, but I hope it will be recognized as the right way to go."

SECTION: Section 7; Page 32; Column 1; Book Review Desk

LENGTH: 1556 words

HEADLINE: UNIVERSITY PRESSES;
Eavesdropping on Hitler's Scientists

BYLINE: By David A. Hollinger; David A. Hollinger, a professor of history at the University of California, Berkeley, is the author of "Morris R. Cohen and the Scientific Ideal" and "In the American Province: Studies in the History and Historiography of Ideas."

 BODY:

OPERATION EPSILON
The Farm Hall Transcripts.
Introduction by Sir Charles Frank.
Illustrated. 313 pp. Berkeley:
University of California Press. $30. 
 "THE moment we are no longer dangerous we are also no longer interesting," Carl Friedrich von Weizsacker observed ruefully to Werner Heisenberg upon learning that the United States had exploded a nuclear weapon over Japan. Heisenberg, the discoverer of the uncertainty principle and a winner of the Nobel Prize in Physics, and his colleague, a son of Baron Ernst von Weizsacker, Hitler's deputy foreign minister, were among 10 German scientists interned -- and clandestinely wiretapped -- at the end of World War II at Farm Hall, a secluded estate in England. The captured scientists had supposed that their expertise would be needed by the Americans and the British. Their wartime work on nuclear energy, after all, had been the obvious reason for their capture by an Allied raiding party behind enemy lines and their confinement. But suddenly it was clear that the German scientific team had little to teach its captors. "These fellows have succeeded in separating isotopes," exclaimed one of the startled and annoyed Germans. "What is there left for us to do?"
How men as proud as Heisenberg and von Weizsacker dealt with the realization that they were bit players rather than stars in the great tragedy of nuclear weaponry -- military resources to be isolated from the Soviets rather than intellectual capital needed by the West -- is one of many compelling themes in what have come to be known as the Farm Hall transcripts. These English translations of selected conversations recorded over a period of six months were made in 1945 by British intelligence agents. The transcripts have been the subject of extensive speculation since their existence was acknowledged 31 years ago. Declassified only in 1992, they have now been published under the title "Operation Epsilon" (the original code name for this extraordinary eavesdropping project), with an introduction by Sir Charles Frank, a physicist who had known most of these German scientists and who was a member of the British intelligence team working on the Farm Hall project.
Speculation about these transcripts had focused on the character and scope of Germany's nuclear energy program: How much of the relevant science did the Germans understand? How hard did they work to produce a bomb? To what extent did their failure result from moral qualms? The transcripts do illuminate these issues, but largely by confirming conclusions already reached by historians of science, and the real significance of the transcripts lies elsewhere.
None of the German scientists, including Heisenberg, understood exactly how a bomb would work. Most had applied themselves diligently to the building of a reactor, although within a program to which only limited resources had been committed on account of the German assumption that the war would be over before a nuclear weapon could be built. This assumption, and attendant judgments about military efficiency, constrained the German nuclear program more than did the qualms a few of the scientists apparently did feel about helping Hitler get a superweapon.
To read "Operation Epsilon" with only these questions in mind, however, is to miss much of its value. Here is a cohort of some of the era's finest scientists, who had chosen to remain in the service of one of history's most iniquitous regimes. We observe them in private discourse about themselves and about some of the most portentous events of the age, including the dropping on a civilian population of an explosive of the sort they, themselves, might conceivably have made, but did not.
"Operation Epsilon" enables us to watch at least four spontaneous dramas taking place simultaneously, with the same cast. One demonstrates the social-psychological trauma of constructing a meaning for the German failure to build an atomic bomb. Another is the political and professional scramble to identify the interests of German science in the postwar world and to develop a coherent strategy for advancing those interests in relation to the British and American rivalry with the Soviet Union. A third is the jockeying for position in the new, de-Nazified scientific community of postwar Germany. A fourth is the moral renegotiation of each scientist's relation with Nazism.
If the drama of deflation and confusion in the wake of the American nuclear success is the most obvious and accessible of these dramas, the reconfiguration of the external and internal politics of the German scientific community will be of the most interest to students of the political sociology of science. Heisenberg and von Weizsacker were especially calculating about the need of the Germans to replace Third Reich politics with cold war politics. They favored the resumption of open scientific communication partly because they feared that if the Americans and the British continued the secrecy adopted during the war, Germans would be frozen out of the most interesting science. Heisenberg actually tried to negotiate with British scientists who visited Farm Hall, threatening, in effect, to defect to the Soviet Union if the British and the Americans did not guarantee the Germans control over their various institutes in Germany and sufficient capitalization to enable them to operate at a pace comparable to that of leading American laboratories. When a colleague warned that such cooperation with Germany's victorious enemies might get the scientists in trouble at home, Heisenberg explained that it would be done "cleverly," making it appear to "the masses" that scientists were working "with fury and gnashing of teeth" under "the wicked Anglo-Saxon control."
At the level of the German scientific community's internal politics, we can observe a host of professional jealousies and personal conflicts, voiced in one-on-one confidences. Max von Laue, who had won the Nobel Prize in Physics in 1914, was a nice guy but a hopelessly incompetent administrator; the physicist Karl Wirtz was seen as insufferably vain but as someone who always got things done; von Weizsacker made slaves of his underlings and was comically slow at simple calculations. In these unguarded conversations, Heisenberg himself comes across as formidable not only in his technical prowess, but also in his abilities as a political strategist. Whatever we may conclude about Heisenberg's soul, he was surely a man of parts, capable of great deception and of persuading those around him to say and do what he wished. At Farm Hall, there was no doubt about who was in charge.
"Operation Epsilon" is a magnificent source for the study of how German intellectuals dealt with Nazism. Two German physicists at Farm Hall, who had been party members, Kurt Diebner and Erich Bagge, felt very much on the defensive and sought desperately to win the confidence of their colleagues. Diebner tried to ingratiate himself with the revered Otto Hahn, the co-discoverer of nuclear fission (his Nobel Prize in Chemistry was announced while he was at Farm Hall), by stressing the opportunism of his decision to join the party. Diebner claimed to have made a deal with the non-Nazi Karl Wirtz: if the Nazis won, Diebner would help Wirtz; if they lost, Wirtz would help Diebner. Whatever these men may have felt in 1941 or 1942, by the summer of 1945 they shared, even in private, a sense that the Nazi regime had been a colossal nuisance to science. Although the transcripts contain a handful of direct references to Nazi atrocities, Nazism figures in these pages more as an inconvenience than a crime.
That the listeners were alert for signs of remorse is apparent from the cover letters sent by the translators to British intelligence. These letters call attention to a rare remark about the wanton murder of Jews and Poles, and to a reference made to the killing of the parents of Samuel A. Goudsmit, the Dutch-born American physicist who had in fact headed the intelligence mission that selected "the Farm Hall 10" for internment. These men brooded little about Hitler's atrocities, and about their own complicity in them. What instead exercised Erich Bagge, for example, was the fate of Germans at the mercy of occupying Moroccan troops brought into Germany by the hated French.
Throughout all of these interlocking dramas, members of the cast were overwhelmingly defined by two identities. They were "scientists," and they were "Germans." Neither, they imagined, had anything to do with politics. The news from Hiroshima brought out these personas vividly. Their pride as scientists and their patriotism as Germans made all of them deeply ashamed not to have built an atomic bomb during the war, even though several of them expressed contradictory relief that they had not done so. With the exception of the legendary anti-Nazi von Laue, these men were so thoroughly caught up in their technical roles as scientists and in their nationality as Germans that they reflected very little on the distinctive politics of the regime they had served. This was true even of those who had been party members. The Farm Hall 10, four of whom are still alive, were good scientific soldiers, largely oblivious to the consequences of their professionalism and their nationalism.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 41; Column 2; Book Review Desk

LENGTH: 306 words

HEADLINE: UNIVERSITY PRESSES -- IN SHORT: NONFICTION;
Rebels With Causes

BYLINE: By Hanna Rubin

 BODY:
The writers and revolutionaries who gave Greenwich Village its reputation for bohemianism at the beginning of the century were reformers at heart. Their mission was to make a better world, not better art. As John Reed put it, "This class struggle plays hell with your poetry." They found a haven in the Village, a backwater unaffected by the grid system imposed by city planners on the rest of New York. GREENWICH VILLAGE: Culture and Counterculture (Museum of the City of New York/Rutgers University, $29.95), a fine collection of essays and photographs edited by Rick Beard, a former official of the Museum of the City of New York, and Leslie Cohen Berlowitz, director of the Humanities Council of New York University, examines the peculiar confluence of geography and economics that made the American bohemians feel at home. For example, few thoroughfares linked the Village with the business district to the south or with the flourishing shopping district just north; this preserved the neighborhood's intimate quality. As the essayists -- who include Alfred Kazin, Daniel Aaron and Denis Donoghue -- point out, the anonymous rooming houses and studios offered liberation from conventional restraints. Still, personal relations took a back seat to politics. Village residents expressed outrage at the needless deaths in the Triangle Shirtwaist factory fire and were early supporters of the suffragettes. After World War I, many of the original Villagers moved on, the artistic types were shrewd entrepreneurs playing to an increasing tourist trade, and the neighborhood's character remained; the Beat Generation writers of the 1950's hung out in the Village for the same reasons John Reed and Emma Goldman had 40 years earlier. The rapture was fading, but the memory is wonderfully preserved in this book.
 HANNA RUBIN
